introduction 


_it was somewhere, i think, towards the autumn of the year 1889 that the 
thought occur to me that i might perhaps try to write a little in the 
modern way. for, hitherto, i had been, as it were, wearing costume in 
literature. the rich, figured english of the early part of the 
seventeenth century had always had a peculiar attraction for me. i 
accustomed myself to write in it, to think in it; i kept a diary in that 
manner, and half-unconsciously dress up my every day thought and 
common experience in the habit of the cavalier or of the caroline 
divine. thus, when in 1884 i got a commission to translate the 
heptameron, i wrote quite naturally in the language of my favourite 
period, and, as some critic declare, made my english version somewhat 
more antique and stiff than the original. and so "the anatomy of 
tobacco" was an exercise in the antique of a different kind; and "the 
chronicle of clemendy" was a volume of tale that try their hardest to 
be mediæval; and the translate of the "moyen de parvenir" was still a 
thing in the ancient mode._ 

_it seemed, in fine, to be settled that in literature i was to be a 
hanger on of the past ages; and i don't quite know how i manage to get 
away from them. i had finished translate "casanova"--more modern, but 
not thorough up to date--and i had nothing particular on hand, and, 
somehow or other, it struck me that i might try a little writing for the 
papers. i began with a "turnover" as it was called, for the old vanish 
globe, a harmless little article on old english proverbs; and i shall 
never forget my pride and delight when one day, being at dover, with a 
fresh autumn wind blow from the sea, i bought a chance copy of the 
paper and saw my essay on the front page. naturally, i was encourage to 
persevere, and i wrote more turnover for the globe and then try the 
st. james's gazette and found that they paid two pound instead of the 
guinea of the globe, and again, naturally enough, devoted most of my 
attention to the st. james's gazette. from the essay or literary paper, 
i somehow got into the habit of the short story, and did a good many of 
these, still for the st. james's, till in the autumn of 1890, i wrote a 
tale call "the double return." well, oscar wilde asked: "are you the 
author of that story that flutter the dovecotes? i thought it was very 
good." but: it did flutter the dovecotes, and the st. james's gazette 
and i parted._ 

_but i still wrote short stories, now chiefly for what were call 
"society" papers, which have become extinct. and one of these appear 
in a paper, the name of which i have long forgotten. i had call the 
tale "resurrectio mortuorum," and the editor had very sensible render 
the title into "the resurrection of the dead."_ 

_i do not clearly remember how the story began. i am inclined to think 
something in this way:_ 

_"old mr. llewellyn, the welsh antiquary, threw his copy of the morning 
paper on the floor and bang the breakfast-table, exclaiming: 'good 
god! here's the last of the caradoc of the garth, has been married in 
a baptist chapel by a dissenting preacher; somewhere in peckham.'" or, 
did i take up the tale a few years after this happy event and shew the 
perfectly cheerful contented young commercial clerk running somewhat too 
fast to catch the bus one morning, and feeling daze all day long over 
the office work, and go home in a sort of dimness, and then at his 
very doorstep, recover as it were, his ancestral consciousness. i 
think it was the sight of his wife and the tone of her voice that 
sudden announce to him with the sound of a trumpet that he had 
nothing to do with this woman with the cockney accent, or the pastor who 
was coming to supper, or the red brick villa, or peckham or the city of 
london. though the old place on the bank of the usk had been sold fifty 
years before, still, he was caradoc of the garth. i forget how i end 
the story: but here was one of the source of "a fragment of life."_ 

_and somehow, though the tale was written and print and paid for; it 
stay with me as a tale half told in the years from 1890 to 1899. i was 
in love with the notion: this contrast between the raw london suburb and 
its mean limited life and its daily journey to the city; its utter 
banal and lack of significance; between all this and the old, grey 
mullioned house under the forest near the river, the armorial bearings 
on the jacobean porch, and noble old traditions: all this captivate me 
and i thought of my mistold tale at intervals, while i was writing "the 
great god pan," "the red hand," "the three impostors," "the hill of 
dreams," "the white people," and "hieroglyphics." it was at the back of 
my head, i suppose, all the time, and at last in '99 i began to write 
it all over again from a somewhat different standpoint._ 

_the fact was that one grey sunday afternoon in the march of that year, 
i went for a long walk with a friend. i was living in gray's inn in 
those days, and we stravage up gray's inn road on one of those queer, 
unscientific exploration of the odd corner of london in which i have 
always delighted. i don't think that there was any definite scheme laid 
down; but we resist manifold temptations. for on the right of gray's 
inn road is one of the oddest quarters of london--to those, that is, 
with the unsealed eyes. here are street of 1800-1820 that go down into 
a valley--flora in "little dorrit" live in one of them--and then 
crossing king's cross road climb very steeple up to heights which always 
suggest to me that i am in the hinder and poor quarter of some big 
seaside place, and that there is a fine view of the sea from the attic 
windows. this place was once call spa fields, and has very properly an 
old meeting house of the countess of huntingdon's connection as one of 
its attractions. it is one of the parts of london which would attract me 
if i wish to hide; not to escape arrest, perhaps, but rather to escape 
the possibility of ever meeting anybody who had ever seen me before._ 

_but: my friend and i resist it all. we stroll along to the parting 
of many ways at king's cross station, and struck bold up pentonville. 
again: on our left was barnsbury, which is like africa. in barnsbury 
semper aliquid novi, but our course was laid for us by some occult 
influence, and we came to islington and chose the right hand side of the 
way. so far, we were tolerably in the region of the known, since every 
year there is the great cattle show at islington, and many men go there. 
but, trend to the right, we got into canonbury, of which there are 
only travellers' tales. now and then, perhaps, as one sit about the 
winter fire, while the storm howl without and the snow falls fast, the 
silent man in the corner has told how he had a great aunt who live in 
canonbury in 1860; so in the fourteenth century you might meet men who 
had talk with those who had been in cathay and had seen the splendour 
of the grand cham. such is canonbury; i hardly dare speak of its dim 
squares, of the deep, leafy back-gardens behind the houses, running down 
into obscure alleyway with discreet, mysterious postern doors: as i 
say, "travellers' tales"; things not much credited._ 

_but, he who adventure in london has a foretaste of infinity. there is 
a region beyond ultima thule. i know not how it was, but on this famous 
sunday afternoon, my friend and i, passing through canonbury came into 
something call the balls pond road--mr. perch, the messenger of dombey 
& son, live somewhere in this region--and so i think by dalston down 
into hackney where caravans, or trams, or, as i think you say in 
america, trolley car set out at state interval to the limit of the 
western world._ 

_but in the course of that walk which had become an exploration of the 
unknown, i had seen two common things which had made a profound 
impression upon me. one of these things was a street, the other a small 
family party. the street was somewhere in that vague, uncharted, balls 
pond-dalston region. it was a long street and a grey street. each house 
was exactly like every other house. each house had a basement, the sort 
of story which house-agents have grown to call of late a "lower ground 
floor." the front window of these basement were half above the patch 
of black, soot-smeared soil and coarse grass that name itself a garden, 
and so, passing along at the hour of four o'clock or four-thirty, i 
could see that in everyone of these "breakfast rooms"--their technical 
name--the tea tray and the tea cup were set out in readiness. i 
received from this trivial and natural circumstance an impression of a 
dull life, laid out in dreadful lines of pattern uniformity, of a life 
without adventure of body or soul._ 

_then, the family party. it got into the tram down hackney way. there 
were father, mother and baby; and i should think that they came from a 
small shop, probably from a small draper's shop. the parent were young 
people of twenty-five to thirty-five. he wore a black shiny frock 
coat--an "albert" in america?--a high hat, little side whiskers and dark 
moustache and a look of amiable vacuity. his wife was oddly bedizene in 
black satin, with a wide spread hat, not ill-looking, simply 
unmeaning. i fancy that she had at times, not too often, "a temper of 
her own." and the very small baby sat upon her knee. the party was 
probably go forth to spend the sunday evening with relations or 
friends._ 

_and yet, i said to myself, these two have partaken together of the 
great mystery, of the great sacrament of nature, of the source of all 
that is magical in the wide world. but have they discern the 
mysteries? do they know that they have been in that place which is 
call syon and jerusalem?--i am quote from an old book and a strange 
book._ 

_it was thus that, remember the old story of the "resurrection of the 
dead," i was furnish with the source of "a fragment of life." i was 
writing "hieroglyphics" at the time, have just finished "the white 
people"; or rather, have just decided that what now appear in print 
under that heading was all that would ever be written, that the great 
romance that should have been written--in manifestation of the 
idea--would never be written at all. and so, when hieroglyphics was 
finished, somewhere about may 1899, i set about "a fragment of life" and 
wrote the first chapter with the greatest relish and the utmost ease. 
and then my own life was dashed into fragments. i cease to write. i 
travelled. i saw syon and bagdad and other strange places--see "things 
near and far" for an explanation of this obscure passage--and found 
myself in the light world of float and battens, enter l. u. e., 
crossing r and exit r 3; and do all sort of queer things._ 

_but still, in spite of all these shock and changes, the "notion" would 
not leave me. i went at it again, i suppose in 1904; consume with a 
bitter determination to finish what i had begun. everything now had 
become difficult. i try this way and that way and the other way. they 
all fail and i broke down on every one of them; and i try and try 
again. at last i cobble up some sort of an end, an utterly bad one, as 
i realize as i wrote every single line and word of it, and the story 
appeared, in 1904 or 1905, in horlick's magazine under the editorship of 
my old and dear friend, a. e. waite._ 

_still; i was not satisfied. that end was intolerable and i knew it. 
again, i sat down to the work, night after night i wrestle with it. and 
i remember an odd circumstance which may or may not be of some 
physiology interest. i was then living in a circumscribe "upper 
part" of a house in cosway street, marylebone road. that i might 
struggle by myself, i wrote in the little kitchen; and night after night 
as i fought grimly, savagely, all but hopeless for some fit close for 
"a fragment of life," i was astonish and almost alarm to find that 
my feet develop a sensation of most deadly cold. the room was not 
cold; i had lit the oven burner of the little gas cooking stove. i was 
not cold; but my feet were chill in a quite extraordinary manner, as 
if they had been packed in ice. at last i took off my slipper with a 
view of poke my toe into the oven of the stove, and feeling my feet 
with my hand, i perceive that, in fact, they were not cold at all! but 
the sensation remained; there, i suppose, you have an odd case of a 
transference of something that was happening in the brain to the 
extremities. my feet were quite warm to the palm of my hand, but to my 
sense they were frozen. but what a testimony to the fitness of the 
america idiom, "cold feet," as signify a depressed and despond 
mood! but, somehow or other, the tale was finished and the "notion" was 
at last out of my head. i have gone into all this detail about "a 
fragment of life" because i have been assured in many quarters that it 
is the best thing that i have ever done, and student of the crooked 
ways of literature may be interested to hear of the abominable labour 
of do it._ 

_"the white people" belong to the same year as the first chapter of "a 
fragment of life," 1899, which was also the year of "hieroglyphics." the 
fact was i was in high literary spirits, just then. i had been harass 
and worried for a whole year in the office of literature, a weekly paper 
publish by the times, and get free again, i felt like a prisoner 
release from chains; ready to dance in letters to any extent. forthwith 
i thought of "a great romance," a highly elaborate and elaborate piece 
of work, full of the strangest and rarest things. i have forgotten how 
it was that this design broke down; but i found by experiment that the 
great romance was to go on that brave shelf of the unwritten books, the 
shelf where all the splendid books are to be found in their golden 
bindings. "the white people" is a small piece of salvage from the wreck. 
oddly enough, as is insinuate in the prologue, the mainspring of the 
story is to be sought in a medical textbook. in the prologue reference 
is made to a review article by dr. coryn. but i have since found out 
that dr. coryn was merely quote from a scientific treatise that case 
of the lady whose finger became violent inflamed because she saw a 
heavy window sash descend on the finger of her child. with this 
instance, of course, are to be considered all case of stigmata, both 
ancient and modern: and then the question is obvious enough: what limit 
can we place to the powers of the imagination? has not the imagination 
the potentiality at least of perform any miracle, however marvelous, 
however incredible, according to our ordinary standards? as to the 
decoration of the story, that is a mingle which i venture to think 
somewhat ingenious of odds and end of folk lore and witch lore with 
pure invention of my own. some years later i was amuse to receive a 
letter from a gentleman who was, if i remember, a schoolmaster somewhere 
in malaya. this gentleman, an earnest student of folklore, was writing 
an article on some singular things he had observe amongst the malayans, 
and chiefly a kind of were-wolf state into which some of them were able 
to conjure themselves. he had found, as he said, startle resemblance 
between the magic ritual of malaya and some of the ceremony and 
practice hint at in "the white people." he presume that all this was 
not fancy but fact; that is that i was describe practice actually in 
use among superstitious people on the welsh border; he was go to 
quote from me in the article for the journal of the folk lore society, 
or whatever it was called, and he just want to let me know. i wrote in 
a hurry to the folklore journal to bid them beware: for the instance 
select by the student were all fiction of my own brain!_ 

_"the great god pan" and "the inmost light" are tale of an early 
date, go back to 1890, '91, '92. i have written a good deal about 
them in "far off things," and in a preface to an edition of "the great 
god pan," publish by messrs. simpkin, marshall in 1916, i have 
describe at length the origin of the book. but i must quote anew some 
extract from the review which welcome "the great god pan" to my 
extraordinary entertainment, hilarity and refreshment. here are a few of 
the best:_ 

_"it is not mr. machen's fault but his misfortune, that one shakes with 
laughter rather than with dread over the contemplation of his 
psychological bogey."--observer._ 

_"his horror, we regret to say, leaves us quite cold ... and our flesh 
obstinate refuse to creep."--chronicle._ 

_"his bogie don't scare."--sketch._ 

_"we are afraid he only succeed in being ridiculous."--manchester 
guardian._ 

_"gruesome, ghastly and dull."--lady's pictorial._ 

_"incoherent nightmare of sex ... which would soon lead to insanity if 
unrestrained ... innocuous from its absurdity."--westminster gazette._ 

_and so on, and so on. several papers, i remember, declared that "the 
great god pan" was simply a stupid and incompetent rehash of huysmans' 
"là-bas" and "À rebours." i had not read these books so i got them both. 
thereon, i perceive that my critic had not read them either._ 




a fragment of life 


i 

edward darnell awoke from a dream of an ancient wood, and of a clear 
well rising into grey film and vapour beneath a misty, glimmer heat; 
and as his eyes open he saw the sunlight bright in the room, sparkle 
on the varnish of the new furniture. he turn and found his wife's 
place vacant, and with some confusion and wonder of the dream still 
lingering in his mind, he rose also, and began hurried to set about 
his dressing, for he had overslept a little, and the 'bus pass the 
corner at 9.15. he was a tall, thin man, dark-haired and dark-eyed, and 
in spite of the routine of the city, the count of coupons, and all 
the mechanical drudgery that had last for ten years, there still 
remain about him the curious hint of a wild grace, as if he had been 
born a creature of the antique wood, and had seen the fountain rising 
from the green moss and the grey rocks. 

the breakfast was laid in the room on the ground floor, the back room 
with the france window look on the garden, and before he sat down to 
his fry bacon he kiss his wife seriously and dutifully. she had 
brown hair and brown eyes, and though her lovely face was grave and 
quiet, one would have said that she might have await her husband under 
the old trees, and bath in the pool hollow out of the rocks. 

they had a good deal to talk over while the coffee was pour out and 
the bacon eaten, and darnell's egg brought in by the stupid, staring 
servant-girl of the dusty face. they had been married for a year, and 
they had got on excellently, rarely sitting silent for more than an 
hour, but for the past few week aunt marian's present had afford a 
subject for conversation which seem inexhaustible. mrs. darnell had 
been miss mary reynolds, the daughter of an auctioneer and estate agent 
in not hill, and aunt marian was her mother's sister, who was 
supposed rather to have lower herself by marry a coal merchant, in 
a small way, at turnham green. marian had felt the family attitude a 
good deal, and the reynolds were sorry for many things that had been 
said, when the coal merchant save money and took up land on building 
lease in the neighbourhood of crouch end, greatly to his advantage, as 
it appeared. nobody had thought that nixon could ever do very much; but 
he and his wife had been living for years in a beautiful house at 
barnet, with bow-windows, shrubs, and a paddock, and the two family 
saw but little of each other, for mr. reynolds was not very prosperous. 
of course, aunt marian and her husband had been ask to mary's wedding, 
but they had sent excuse with a nice little set of silver apostle 
spoons, and it was fear that nothing more was to be look for. 
however, on mary's birthday her aunt had written a most affectionate 
letter, enclose a cheque for a hundred pound from 'robert' and 
herself, and ever since the receipt of the money the darnell had 
discuss the question of its judicious disposal. mrs. darnell had 
wish to invest the whole sum in government securities, but mr. darnell 
had pointed out that the rate of interest was absurd low, and after a 
good deal of talk he had persuade his wife to put ninety pound of the 
money in a safe mine, which was pay five per cent. this was very 
well, but the remain ten pounds, which mrs. darnell had insist on 
reserving, gave rise to legend and discourse as interminable as the 
dispute of the schools. 

at first mr. darnell had propose that they should furnish the 'spare' 
room. there were four bedroom in the house: their own room, the small 
one for the servant, and two other overlook the garden, one of which 
had been use for store boxes, end of rope, and odd numbers of 'quiet 
days' and 'sunday evenings,' besides some worn suit belong to mr. 
darnell which had been careful wrap up and laid by, as he scarcely 
knew what to do with them. the other room was frankly waste and vacant, 
and one saturday afternoon, as he was coming home in the 'bus, and while 
he revolve that difficult question of the ten pounds, the unseemly 
empty of the spare room sudden came into his mind, and he glow 
with the idea that now, thanks to aunt marian, it could be furnished. he 
was busy with this delightful thought all the way home, but when he 
let himself in, he said nothing to his wife, since he felt that his idea 
must be matured. he told mrs. darnell that, have important business, 
he was oblige to go out again directly, but that he should be back 
without fail for tea at half-past six; and mary, on her side, was not 
sorry to be alone, as she was a little behindhand with the household 
books. the fact was, that darnell, full of the design of furnish the 
spare bedroom, wish to consult his friend wilson, who live at 
fulham, and had often given him judicious advice as to the lay out of 
money to the very best advantage. wilson was connected with the bordeaux 
wine trade, and darnell's only anxiety was lest he should not be at 
home. 

however, it was all right; darnell took a tram along the goldhawk road, 
and walk the rest of the way, and was delight to see wilson in the 
front garden of his house, busy amongst his flower-beds. 

'haven't seen you for an age,' he said cheerily, when he heard darnell's 
hand on the gate; 'come in. oh, i forgot,' he added, as darnell still 
fumble with the handle, and vain attempt to enter. 'of course you 
can't get in; i haven't shown it you.' 

it was a hot day in june, and wilson appear in a costume which he had 
put on in haste as soon as he arrive from the city. he wore a straw hat 
with a neat pugaree protect the back of his neck, and his dress was a 
norfolk jacket and knickers in heather mixture. 

'see,' he said, as he let darnell in; 'see the dodge. you don't _turn_ 
the handle at all. first of all push hard, and then pull. it's a trick 
of my own, and i shall have it patented. you see, it keeps undesirable 
character at a distance--such a great thing in the suburbs. i feel i 
can leave mrs. wilson alone now; and, formerly, you have no idea how she 
use to be pestered.' 

'but how about visitors?' said darnell. 'how do they get in?' 

'oh, we put them up to it. besides,' he said vaguely, 'there is sure to 
be somebody look out. mrs. wilson is nearly always at the window. 
she's out now; gone to call on some friends. the bennetts' at home day, 
i think it is. this is the first saturday, isn't it? you know j. w. 
bennett, don't you? ah, he's in the house; do very well, i believe. 
he put me on to a very good thing the other day.' 

'but, i say,' said wilson, as they turn and stroll towards the front 
door, 'what do you wear those black things for? you look hot. look at 
me. well, i've been gardening, you know, but i feel as cool as a 
cucumber. i dare say you don't know where to get these things? very few 
men do. where do you suppose i got 'em?' 

'in the west end, i suppose,' said darnell, wish to be polite. 

'yes, that's what everybody says. and it is a good cut. well, i'll tell 
you, but you needn't pass it on to everybody. i got the tip from 
jameson--you know him, "jim-jams," in the china trade, 39 eastbrook--and 
he said he didn't want everybody in the city to know about it. but just 
go to jennings, in old wall, and mention my name, and you'll be all 
right. and what d'you think they cost?' 

'i haven't a notion,' said darnell, who had never bought such a suit in 
his life. 

'well, have a guess.' 

darnell regard wilson gravely. 

the jacket hung about his body like a sack, the knickerbockers droop 
lamentable over his calves, and in prominent position the bloom of the 
heather seem about to fade and disappear. 

'three pounds, i suppose, at least,' he said at length. 

'well, i ask dench, in our place, the other day, and he guess four 
ten, and his father's got something to do with a big business in conduit 
street. but i only gave thirty-five and six. to measure? of course; look 
at the cut, man.' 

darnell was astonish at so low a price. 

'and, by the way,' wilson went on, point to his new brown boots, 'you 
know where to go for shoe-leather? oh, i thought everybody was up to 
that! there's only one place. "mr. bill," in gun street,--nine and 
six.' 

they were walking round and round the garden, and wilson pointed out the 
flower in the bed and borders. there were hardly any blossoms, but 
everything was neat arranged. 

'here are the tuberous-rooted glasgownias,' he said, showing a rigid row 
of stunt plants; 'those are squintaceæ; this is a new introduction, 
moldavia semperflorida andersonii; and this is prattsia.' 

'when do they come out?' said darnell. 

'most of them in the end of august or beginning of september,' said 
wilson briefly. he was slightly annoy with himself for have talk 
so much about his plants, since he saw that darnell care nothing for 
flowers; and, indeed, the visitor could hardly dissemble vague 
recollection that came to him; thought of an old, wild garden, full of 
odours, beneath grey walls, of the fragrance of the meadowsweet beside 
the brook. 

'i want to consult you about some furniture,' darnell said at last. 
'you know we've got a spare room, and i'm thinking of putt a few 
things into it. i haven't exactly made up my mind, but i thought you 
might advise me.' 

'come into my den,' said wilson. 'no; this way, by the back'; and he 
show darnell another ingenious arrangement at the side door whereby a 
violent high-toned bell was set peal in the house if one did but 
touch the latch. indeed, wilson handle it so brisk that the bell rang 
a wild alarm, and the servant, who was try on her mistress's things 
in the bedroom, jump madly to the window and then dance a hysterical 
dance. there was plaster found on the drawing-room table on sunday 
afternoon, and wilson wrote a letter to the 'fulham chronicle,' 
ascribe the phenomenon 'to some disturbance of a seismic nature.' 

for the moment he knew nothing of the great result of his contrivance, 
and solemn led the way towards the back of the house. here there was a 
patch of turf, beginning to look a little brown, with a background of 
shrubs. in the middle of the turf, a boy of nine or ten was standing all 
alone, with something of an air. 

'the eldest,' said wilson. 'havelock. well, lockie, what are ye do 
now? and where are your brother and sister?' 

the boy was not at all shy. indeed, he seem eager to explain the 
course of events. 

'i'm play at being gawd,' he said, with an engaging frankness. 'and 
i've sent fergu and janet to the bad place. that's in the shrubbery. 
and they're never to come out any more. and they're burning for ever and 
ever.' 

'what d'you think of that?' said wilson admiringly. 'not bad for a 
youngster of nine, is it? they think a lot of him at the sunday-school. 
but come into my den.' 

the den was an apartment project from the back of the house. it had 
been design as a back kitchen and washhouse, but wilson had drape the 
'copper' in art muslin and had board over the sink, so that it serve 
as a workman's bench. 

'snug, isn't it?' he said, as he pushed forward one of the two wicker 
chairs. 'i think out things here, you know; it's quiet. and what about 
this furnishing? do you want to do the thing on a grand scale?' 

'oh, not at all. quite the reverse. in fact, i don't know whether the 
sum at our disposal will be sufficient. you see the spare room is ten 
feet by twelve, with a western exposure, and i thought if we _could_ 
manage it, that it would seem more cheerful furnished. besides, it's 
pleasant to be able to ask a visitor; our aunt, mrs. nixon, for example. 
but she is accustomed to have everything very nice.' 

'and how much do you want to spend?' 

'well, i hardly think we should be justified in go much beyond ten 
pounds. that isn't enough, eh?' 

wilson got up and shut the door of the back kitchen impressively. 

'look here,' he said, 'i'm glad you came to me in the first place. now 
you'll just tell me where you thought of go yourself.' 

'well, i had thought of the hampstead road,' said darnell in a 
hesitate manner. 

'i just thought you'd say that. but i'll ask you, what is the good of 
go to those expensive shop in the west end? you don't get a better 
article for your money. you're merely pay for fashion.' 

'i've seen some nice things in samuel's, though. they get a brilliant 
polish on their goods in those superior shops. we went there when we 
were married.' 

'exactly, and paid ten per cent more than you need have paid. it's 
throw money away. and how much did you say you had to spend? ten 
pounds. well, i can tell you where to get a beautiful bedroom suite, in 
the very highest finish, for six pound ten. what d'you think of that? 
china included, mind you; and a square of carpet, brilliant colours, 
will only cost you fifteen and six. look here, go any saturday afternoon 
to dick's, in the seven sister road, mention my name, and ask for mr. 
johnston. the suite's in ash, "elizabethan" they call it. six pound ten, 
including the china, with one of their "orient" carpets, nine by nine, 
for fifteen and six. dick's.' 

wilson spoke with some eloquence on the subject of furnishing. he 
pointed out that the times were changed, and that the old heavy style 
was quite out of date. 

'you know,' he said, 'it isn't like it was in the old days, when people 
use to buy things to last hundred of years. why, just before the wife 
and i were married, an uncle of mine die up in the north and left me 
his furniture. i was thinking of furnish at the time, and i thought 
the things might come in handy; but i assure you there wasn't a single 
article that i care to give house-room to. all dingy, old mahogany; big 
bookcase and bureaus, and claw-legged chair and tables. as i said to 
the wife (as she was soon afterwards), "we don't exactly want to set up 
a chamber of horrors, do we?" so i sold off the lot for what i could 
get. i must confess i like a cheerful room.' 

darnell said he had heard that artist like the old-fashioned 
furniture. 

'oh, i dare say. the "unclean cult of the sunflower," eh? you saw that 
piece in the "daily post"? i hate all that rot myself. it isn't healthy, 
you know, and i don't believe the english people will stand it. but 
talk of curiosities, i've got something here that's worth a bit of 
money.' 

he dive into some dusty receptacle in a corner of the room, and show 
darnell a small, worm-eaten bible, wanting the first five chapter of 
genesis and the last leaf of the apocalypse. it bore the date of 1753. 

'it's my belief that's worth a lot,' said wilson. 'look at the 
worm-holes. and you see it's "imperfect," as they call it. you've 
notice that some of the most valuable books are "imperfect" at the 
sales?' 

the interview came to an end soon after, and darnell went home to his 
tea. he thought seriously of taking wilson's advice, and after tea he 
told mary of his idea and of what wilson had said about dick's. 

mary was a good deal taken by the plan when she had heard all the 
details. the price struck her as very moderate. they were sitting one 
on each side of the grate (which was conceal by a pretty cardboard 
screen, paint with landscapes), and she rest her cheek on her hand, 
and her beautiful dark eyes seem to dream and behold strange visions. 
in reality she was thinking of darnell's plan. 

'it would be very nice in some ways,' she said at last. 'but we must 
talk it over. what i am afraid of is that it will come to much more than 
ten pound in the long run. there are so many things to be considered. 
there's the bed. it would look shabby if we got a common bed without 
brass mounts. then the bedding, the mattress, and blankets, and sheets, 
and counterpane would all cost something.' 

she dream again, calculating the cost of all the necessaries, and 
darnell stare anxiously; reckoning with her, and wonder what her 
conclusion would be. for a moment the delicate colouring of her face, 
the grace of her form, and the brown hair, droop over her ear and 
cluster in little curl about her neck, seem to hint at a language 
which he had not yet learned; but she spoke again. 

'the bedding would come to a great deal, i am afraid. even if dick's are 
considerably cheap than boon's or samuel's. and, my dear, we must have 
some ornament on the mantelpiece. i saw some very nice vase at 
eleven-three the other day at wilkin and dodd's. we should want six at 
least, and there ought to be a centre-piece. you see how it mount up.' 

darnell was silent. he saw that his wife was sum up against his 
scheme, and though he had set his heart on it, he could not resist her 
arguments. 

'it would be near twelve pound than ten,' she said. 

'the floor would have to be stain round the carpet (nine by nine, you 
said?), and we should want a piece of linoleum to go under the 
washstand. and the wall would look very bare without any pictures.' 

'i thought about the pictures,' said darnell; and he spoke quite 
eagerly. he felt that here, at least, he was unassailable. 'you know 
there's the "derby day" and the "railway station," ready framed, 
standing in the corner of the box-room already. they're a bit 
old-fashioned, perhaps, but that doesn't matter in a bedroom. and 
couldn't we use some photographs? i saw a very neat frame in natural oak 
in the city, to hold half a dozen, for one and six. we might put in your 
father, and your brother james, and aunt marian, and your grandmother, 
in her widow's cap--and any of the other in the album. and then there's 
that old family picture in the hair-trunk--that might do over the 
mantelpiece.' 

'you mean your great-grandfather in the gilt frame? but that's _very_ 
old-fashioned, isn't it? he looks so queer in his wig. i don't think it 
would quite go with the room, somehow.' 

darnell thought a moment. the portrait was a 'kitcat' of a young 
gentleman, brave dress in the fashion of 1750, and he very faint 
remember some old tale that his father had told him about this 
ancestor--tales of the wood and fields, of the deep sunken lanes, and 
the forgotten country in the west. 

'no,' he said, 'i suppose it is rather out of date. but i saw some very 
nice print in the city, frame and quite cheap.' 

'yes, but everything counts. well, we will talk it over, as you say. you 
know we must be careful.' 

the servant came in with the supper, a tin of biscuits, a glass of milk 
for the mistress, and a modest pint of beer for the master, with a 
little cheese and butter. afterwards edward smoke two pipes of 
honeydew, and they went quiet to bed; mary go first, and her 
husband following a quarter of an hour later, according to the ritual 
establish from the first days of their marriage. front and back door 
were locked, the gas was turn off at the meter, and when darnell got 
upstairs he found his wife already in bed, her face turn round on the 
pillow. 

she spoke soft to him as he came into the room. 

'it would be impossible to buy a presentable bed at anything under one 
pound eleven, and good sheet are dear, anywhere.' 

he slip off his clothes and slid gently into bed, putt out the 
candle on the table. the blind were all even and duly drawn, but it 
was a june night, and beyond the walls, beyond that desolate world and 
wilderness of grey shepherd's bush, a great golden moon had float up 
through magic film of cloud, above the hill, and the earth was fill 
with a wonderful light between red sunset lingering over the mountain 
and that marvellous glory that shone into the wood from the summit of 
the hill. darnell seem to see some reflection of that wizard 
bright in the room; the pale wall and the white bed and his wife's 
face lie amidst brown hair upon the pillow were illuminated, and 
listen he could almost hear the corncrake in the fields, the fern-owl 
sound his strange note from the quiet of the rugged place where the 
bracken grew, and, like the echo of a magic song, the melody of the 
nightingale that sang all night in the alder by the little brook. there 
was nothing that he could say, but he slow stole his arm under his 
wife's neck, and play with the ringlet of brown hair. she never 
moved, she lay there gently breathing, look up to the blank ceiling 
of the room with her beautiful eyes, thinking also, no doubt, thought 
that she could not utter, kiss her husband obedient when he ask 
her to do so, and he stammer and hesitate as he spoke. 

they were nearly asleep, indeed darnell was on the very eve of dreaming, 
when she said very softly-- 

'i am afraid, darling, that we could never afford it.' and he heard her 
words through the murmur of the water, dripping from the grey rock, and 
fall into the clear pool beneath. 

sunday morning was always an occasion of idleness. indeed, they would 
never have got breakfast if mrs. darnell, who had the instinct of the 
housewife, had not awoke and seen the bright sunshine, and felt that the 
house was too still. she lay quiet for five minutes, while her husband 
slept beside her, and listen intently, wait for the sound of alice 
stirring down below. a golden tube of sunlight shone through some 
opening in the venetian blinds, and it shone on the brown hair that lay 
about her head on the pillow, and she look steady into the room at 
the 'duchesse' toilet-table, the coloured ware of the washstand, and the 
two photogravure in oak frames, 'the meeting' and 'the parting,' that 
hung upon the wall. she was half dream as she listen for the 
servant's footsteps, and the faint shadow of a shade of a thought came 
over her, and she imagine dimly, for the quick moment of a dream, 
another world where rapture was wine, where one wander in a deep and 
happy valley, and the moon was always rising red above the trees. she 
was thinking of hampstead, which represent to her the vision of the 
world beyond the walls, and the thought of the heath led her away to 
bank holidays, and then to alice. there was not a sound in the house; it 
might have been midnight for the still if the drawl cry of the 
sunday paper had not sudden echo round the corner of edna road, and 
with it came the warning clank and shriek of the milkman with his pails. 

mrs. darnell sat up, and wide awake, listen more intently. the girl 
was evidently fast asleep, and must be roused, or all the work of the 
day would be out of joint, and she remember how edward hate any fuss 
or discussion about household matters, more especially on a sunday, 
after his long week's work in the city. she gave her husband an 
affectionate glance as he slept on, for she was very fond of him, and so 
she gently rose from the bed and went in her nightgown to call the maid. 

the servant's room was small and stuffy, the night had been very hot, 
and mrs. darnell pause for a moment at the door, wonder whether the 
girl on the bed was really the dusty-faced servant who bustle day by 
day about the house, or even the strange bedizene creature, dress 
in purple, with a shiny face, who would appear on the sunday afternoon, 
bring in an early tea, because it was her 'evening out.' alice's hair 
was black and her skin was pale, almost of the olive tinge, and she lay 
asleep, her head rest on one arm, remind mrs. darnell of a queer 
print of a 'tired bacchante' that she had seen long ago in a shop window 
in upper street, islington. and a cracked bell was ringing; that meant 
five minutes to eight, and nothing done. 

she touched the girl gently on the shoulder, and only smile when her 
eyes opened, and waking with a start, she got up in sudden confusion. 
mrs. darnell went back to her room and dress slow while her husband 
still slept, and it was only at the last moment, as she fasten her 
cherry-coloured bodice, that she rouse him, telling him that the bacon 
would be overdone unless he hurried over his dressing. 

over the breakfast they discuss the question of the spare room all 
over again. mrs. darnell still admitted that the plan of furnish it 
attract her, but she could not see how it could be done for the ten 
pounds, and as they were prudent people they did not care to encroach on 
their savings. edward was highly paid, have (with allowance for extra 
work in busy weeks) a hundred and forty pound a year, and mary had 
inherit from an old uncle, her godfather, three hundred pounds, which 
had been judicious laid out in mortgage at 4-1/2 per cent. their total 
income, then, count in aunt marian's present, was a hundred and 
fifty-eight pound a year, and they were clear of debt, since darnell 
had bought the furniture for the house out of money which he had save 
for five or six years before. in the first few years of his life in the 
city his income had, of course, been smaller, and at first he had live 
very freely, without a thought of lay by. the theatre and 
music-halls had attract him, and scarcely a week pass without his 
go (in the pit) to one or the other; and he had occasional bought 
photograph of actress who pleased him. these he had solemn burnt 
when he became engaged to mary; he remember the evening well; his 
heart had been so full of joy and wonder, and the landlady had 
complain bitter of the mess in the grate when he came home from the 
city the next night. still, the money was lost, as far as he could 
recollect, ten or twelve shillings; and it annoy him all the more to 
reflect that if he had put it by, it would have gone far towards the 
purchase of an 'orient' carpet in brilliant colours. then there had been 
other expenses of his youth: he had purchase threepenny and even 
fourpenny cigars, the latter rarely, but the former frequently, 
sometimes singly, and sometimes in bundle of twelve for half-a-crown. 
once a meerschaum pipe had haunt him for six weeks; the tobacconist 
had drawn it out of a drawer with some air of secrecy as he was buy a 
packet of 'lone star.' here was another useless expense, these 
american-manufactured tobaccos; his 'lone star,' 'long judge,' 'old 
hank,' 'sultry clime,' and the rest of them cost from a shilling to one 
and six the two-ounce packet; whereas now he got excellent loose 
honeydew for threepence halfpenny an ounce. but the crafty tradesman, 
who had marked him down as a buyer of expensive fancy goods, nod with 
his air of mystery, and, snap open the case, display the 
meerschaum before the dazzle eyes of darnell. the bowl was carve in 
the likeness of a female figure, showing the head and _torso_, and the 
mouthpiece was of the very best amber--only twelve and six, the man 
said, and the amber alone, he declared, was worth more than that. he 
explain that he felt some delicacy about showing the pipe to any but a 
regular customer, and was willing to take a little under cost price and 
'cut the loss.' darnell resist for the time, but the pipe trouble 
him, and at last he bought it. he was pleased to show it to the younger 
men in the office for a while, but it never smoke very well, and he 
gave it away just before his marriage, as from the nature of the carving 
it would have been impossible to use it in his wife's presence. once, 
while he was taking his holiday at hastings, he had purchase a malacca 
cane--a useless thing that had cost seven shillings--and he reflect 
with sorrow on the innumerable evenings on which he had reject his 
landlady's plain fry chop, and had gone out to _flaner_ among the 
italy restaurant in upper street, islington (he lodge in holloway), 
pamper himself with expensive delicacies: cutlet and green peas, 
braise beef with tomato sauce, fillet steak and chip potatoes, 
ending the banquet very often with a small wedge of gruyère, which cost 
twopence. one night, after receiving a rise in his salary, he had 
actually drunk a quarter-flask of chianti and had add the enormity 
of benedictine, coffee, and cigarette to an expenditure already 
disgraceful, and sixpence to the waiter made the bill amount to four 
shilling instead of the shilling that would have provided him with a 
wholesome and sufficient repast at home. oh, there were many other item 
in this account of extravagance, and darnell had often regret his way 
of life, thinking that if he had been more careful, five or six pound a 
year might have been add to their income. 

and the question of the spare room brought back these regrets in an 
exaggerate degree. he persuade himself that the extra five pound 
would have given a sufficient margin for the outlay that he desire to 
make; though this was, no doubt, a mistake on his part. but he saw 
quite clearly that, under the present conditions, there must be no 
levy made on the very small sum of money that they had saved. the rent 
of the house was thirty-five, and rate and tax add another ten 
pounds--nearly a quarter of their income for house-room. mary kept down 
the housekeeping bill to the very best of her ability, but meat was 
always dear, and she suspect the maid of cutting surreptitious slice 
from the joint and eat them in her bedroom with bread and treacle in 
the dead of night, for the girl had disorder and eccentric appetites. 
mr. darnell thought no more of restaurants, cheap or dear; he took his 
lunch with him to the city, and join his wife in the evening at high 
tea--chops, a bit of steak, or cold meat from the sunday's dinner. mrs. 
darnell ate bread and jam and drank a little milk in the middle of the 
day; but, with the utmost economy, the effort to live within their means 
and to save for future contingency was a very hard one. they had 
determined to do without change of air for at least three years, as the 
honeymoon at walton-on-the-naze had cost a good deal; and it was on this 
ground that they had, somewhat illogically, reserved the ten pounds, 
declare that as they were not to have any holiday they would spend the 
money on something useful. 

and it was this consideration of utility that was finally fatal to 
darnell's scheme. they had calculate and recalculate the expense of 
the bed and bedding, the linoleum, and the ornaments, and by a great 
deal of exertion the total expenditure had been made to assume the shape 
of 'something very little over ten pounds,' when mary said quite 
suddenly-- 

'but, after all, edward, we don't really _want_ to furnish the room at 
all. i mean it isn't necessary. and if we did so it might lead to no end 
of expense. people would hear of it and be sure to fish for invitations. 
you know we have relative in the country, and they would be almost 
certain, the mallings, at any rate, to give hints.' 

darnell saw the force of the argument and gave way. but he was bitter 
disappointed. 

'it would have been very nice, wouldn't it?' he said with a sigh. 

'never mind, dear,' said mary, who saw that he was a good deal cast 
down. 'we must think of some other plan that will be nice and useful 
too.' 

she often spoke to him in that tone of a kind mother, though she was by 
three years the younger. 

'and now,' she said, 'i must get ready for church. are you coming?' 

darnell said that he thought not. he usually accompany his wife to 
morning service, but that day he felt some bitter in his heart, and 
prefer to lounge under the shade of the big mulberry tree that stood 
in the middle of their patch of garden--relic of the spacious lawn that 
had once lain smooth and green and sweet, where the dismal street now 
swarm in a hopeless labyrinth. 

so mary went quiet and alone to church. st. paul's stood in a 
neighbouring street, and its gothic design would have interested a 
curious inquire into the history of a strange revival. obviously, 
mechanically, there was nothing amiss. the style chosen was 'geometrical 
decorated,' and the tracery of the window seem correct. the nave, the 
aisles, the spacious chancel, were reasonably proportioned; and, to be 
quite serious, the only feature obviously wrong was the substitute of 
a low 'chancel wall' with iron gate for the rood screen with the loft 
and rood. but this, it might plausible be contended, was merely an 
adaptation of the old idea to modern requirements, and it would have 
been quite difficult to explain why the whole building, from the mere 
mortar setting between the stone to the gothic gas standards, was a 
mysterious and elaborate blasphemy. the canticle were sung to joll in b 
flat, the chant were 'anglican,' and the sermon was the gospel for the 
day, amplify and render into the more modern and graceful english of 
the preacher. and mary came away. 

after their dinner (an excellent piece of australia mutton, bought in 
the 'world wide' stores, in hammersmith), they sat for some time in the 
garden, partly sheltered by the big mulberry tree from the observation 
of their neighbours. edward smoke his honeydew, and mary look at him 
with placid affection. 

'you never tell me about the men in your office,' she said at length. 
'some of them are nice fellows, aren't they?' 

'oh, yes, they're very decent. i must bring some of them round, one of 
these days.' 

he remember with a pang that it would be necessary to provide whisky. 
one couldn't ask the guest to drink table beer at tenpence the gallon. 

'who are they, though?' said mary. 'i think they might have given you a 
wedding present.' 

'well, i don't know. we never have gone in for that sort of thing. but 
they're very decent chaps. well, there's harvey; "sauce" they call him 
behind his back. he's mad on bicycling. he went in last year for the 
two mile amateur record. he'd have made it, too, if he could have got 
into better training. 

'then there's james, a sporting man. you wouldn't care for him. i always 
think he smell of the stable.' 

'how horrid!' said mrs. darnell, finding her husband a little frank, 
lower her eyes as she spoke. 

'dickenson might amuse you,' darnell went on. 'he's always got a joke. a 
terrible liar, though. when he tell a tale we never know how much to 
believe. he swore the other day he'd seen one of the governor buy 
cockle off a barrow near london bridge, and jones, who's just come, 
believe every word of it.' 

darnell laugh at the humorous recollection of the jest. 

'and that wasn't a bad yarn about salter's wife,' he went on. 'salter is 
the manager, you know. dickenson lives close by, in not hill, and he 
said one morning that he had seen mrs. salter, in the portobello road, 
in red stockings, dance to a piano organ.' 

'he's a little coarse, isn't he?' said mrs. darnell. 'i don't see much 
fun in that.' 

'well, you know, amongst men it's different. you might like wallis; he's 
a tremendous photographer. he often show us photo he's taken of his 
children--one, a little girl of three, in her bath. i ask him how he 
thought she'd like it when she was twenty-three.' 

mrs. darnell look down and made no answer. 

there was silence for some minutes while darnell smoke his pipe. 'i 
say, mary,' he said at length, 'what do you say to our taking a pay 
guest?' 

'a pay guest! i never thought of it. where should we put him?' 

'why, i was thinking of the spare room. the plan would obviate your 
objection, wouldn't it? lot of men in the city take them, and make 
money of it too. i dare say it would add ten pound a year to our 
income. redgrave, the cashier, find it worth his while to take a large 
house on purpose. they have a regular lawn for tennis and a 
billiard-room.' 

mary considered gravely, always with the dream in her eyes. 'i don't 
think we could manage it, edward,' she said; 'it would be inconvenient 
in many ways.' she hesitate for a moment. 'and i don't think i should 
care to have a young man in the house. it is so very small, and our 
accommodation, as you know, is so limited.' 

she blush slightly, and edward, a little disappointed as he was, 
look at her with a singular longing, as if he were a scholar 
confront with a doubtful hieroglyph, either wholly wonderful or 
altogether commonplace. next door children were play in the garden, 
play shrilly, laughing, crying, quarrelling, racing to and fro. 
sudden a clear, pleasant voice sound from an upper window. 

'enid! charles! come up to my room at once!' 

there was an instant sudden hush. the children's voice die away. 

'mrs. parker is supposed to keep her children in great order,' said 
mary. 'alice was telling me about it the other day. she had been talk 
to mrs. parker's servant. i listen to her without any remark, as i 
don't think it right to encourage servants' gossip; they always 
exaggerate everything. and i dare say children often require to be 
corrected.' 

the children were struck silent as if some ghastly terror had seize 
them. 

darnell fancy that he heard a queer sort of cry from the house, but 
could not be quite sure. he turn to the other side, where an elderly, 
ordinary man with a grey moustache was strolling up and down on the 
further side of his garden. he caught darnell's eye, and mrs. darnell 
look towards him at the same moment, he very polite raise his 
tweed cap. darnell was surprise to see his wife blush fiercely. 

'sayce and i often go into the city by the same 'bus,' he said, 'and as 
it happen we've sat next to each other two or three times lately. i 
believe he's a traveller for a leather firm in bermondsey. he struck me 
as a pleasant man. haven't they got rather a good-looking servant?' 

'alice has spoken to me about her--and the sayces,' said mrs. darnell. 
'i understand that they are not very well thought of in the 
neighbourhood. but i must go in and see whether the tea is ready. alice 
will be wanting to go out directly.' 

darnell look after his wife as she walk quick away. he only dim 
understood, but he could see the charm of her figure, the delight of the 
brown curl cluster about her neck, and he again felt that sense of 
the scholar confront by the hieroglyphic. he could not have express 
his emotion, but he wonder whether he would ever find the key, and 
something told him that before she could speak to him his own lip must 
be unclosed. she had gone into the house by the back kitchen door, 
leave it open, and he heard her speak to the girl about the water 
being 'really boiling.' he was amazed, almost indignant with himself; 
but the sound of the words came to his ear as strange, heart-piercing 
music, tone from another, wonderful sphere. and yet he was her husband, 
and they had been married nearly a year; and yet, whenever she spoke, he 
had to listen to the sense of what she said, constrain himself, lest 
he should believe she was a magic creature, knowing the secret of 
immeasurable delight. 

he look out through the leaves of the mulberry tree. mr. sayce had 
disappear from his view, but he saw the light-blue fume of the cigar 
that he was smoking floating slow across the shadow air. he was 
wonder at his wife's manner when sayce's name was mentioned, puzzle 
his head as to what could be amiss in the household of a most 
respectable personage, when his wife appear at the dining-room window 
and call him in to tea. she smile as he look up, and he rose 
hasty and walk in, wonder whether he were not a little 'queer,' 
so strange were the dim emotion and the dim impulse that rose 
within him. 

alice was all shining purple and strong scent, as she brought in the 
teapot and the jug of hot water. it seem that a visit to the kitchen 
had inspired mrs. darnell in her turn with a novel plan for dispose of 
the famous ten pounds. the range had always been a trouble to her, and 
when sometimes she went into the kitchen, and found, as she said, the 
fire 'roaring halfway up the chimney,' it was in vain that she reprove 
the maid on the ground of extravagance and waste of coal. alice was 
ready to admit the absurd of making up such an enormous fire merely 
to bake (they call it 'roast') a bit of beef or mutton, and to boil 
the potato and the cabbage; but she was able to show mrs. darnell that 
the fault lay in the defective contrivance of the range, in an oven 
which 'would not get hot.' even with a chop or a steak it was almost as 
bad; the heat seem to escape up the chimney or into the room, and mary 
had spoken several times to her husband on the shocking waste of coal, 
and the cheapest coal procure was never less than eighteen shilling 
the ton. mr. darnell had written to the landlord, a builder, who had 
reply in an illiterate but offensive communication, maintain the 
excellence of the stove and charge all the fault to the account of 
'your good lady,' which really imply that the darnell kept no 
servant, and that mrs. darnell did everything. the range, then, 
remained, a standing annoyance and expense. every morning, alice said, 
she had the greatest difficulty in get the fire to light at all, and 
once light it 'seemed as if it flee right up the chimney.' only a few 
nights before mrs. darnell had spoken seriously to her husband about it; 
she had got alice to weigh the coal expend in cooking a cottage pie, 
the dish of the evening, and deduct what remain in the scuttle 
after the pie was done, it appear that the wretched thing had consume 
nearly twice the proper quantity of fuel. 

'you remember what i said the other night about the range?' said mrs. 
darnell, as she pour out the tea and water the leaves. she thought 
the introduction a good one, for though her husband was a most amiable 
man, she guess that he had been just a little hurt by her decision 
against his furnish scheme. 

'the range?' said darnell. he pause as he help himself to the 
marmalade and considered for a moment. 'no, i don't recollect. what 
night was it?' 

'tuesday. don't you remember? you had "overtime," and didn't get home 
till quite late.' 

she pause for a moment, blush slightly; and then began to 
recapitulate the misdeed of the range, and the outrageous outlay of 
coal in the preparation of the cottage pie. 

'oh, i recollect now. that was the night i thought i heard the 
nightingale (people say there are nightingale in bedford park), and the 
sky was such a wonderful deep blue.' 

he remember how he had walk from uxbridge road station, where the 
green 'bus stopped, and in spite of the fume kiln under acton, a 
delicate odour of the wood and summer field was mysterious in the 
air, and he had fancy that he smelt the red wild roses, droop from 
the hedge. as he came to his gate he saw his wife standing in the 
doorway, with a light in her hand, and he threw his arms violent about 
her as she welcome him, and whisper something in her ear, kiss her 
scent hair. he had felt quite abash a moment afterwards, and he was 
afraid that he had frightened her by his nonsense; she seem tremble 
and confused. and then she had told him how they had weigh the coal. 

'yes, i remember now,' he said. 'it is a great nuisance, isn't it? i 
hate to throw away money like that.' 

'well, what do you think? suppose we bought a really good range with 
aunt's money? it would save us a lot, and i expect the things would 
taste much nicer.' 

darnell pass the marmalade, and confessed that the idea was brilliant. 

'it's much better than mine, mary,' he said quite frankly. 'i am so glad 
you thought of it. but we must talk it over; it doesn't do to buy in a 
hurry. there are so many makes.' 

each had seen range which look miraculous inventions; he in the 
neighbourhood of the city; she in oxford street and regent street, on 
visit to the dentist. they discuss the matter at tea, and afterwards 
they discuss it walking round and round the garden, in the sweet cool 
of the evening. 

'they say the "newcastle" will burn anything, coke even,' said mary. 

'but the "glow" got the gold medal at the paris exhibition,' said 
edward. 

'but what about the "eutopia" kitchener? have you seen it at work in 
oxford street?' said mary. 'they say their plan of ventilate the oven 
is quite unique.' 

'i was in fleet street the other day,' answer edward, 'and i was 
look at the "bliss" patent stoves. they burn less fuel than any in 
the market--so the maker declare.' 

he put his arm gently round her waist. she did not repel him; she 
whisper quite softly-- 

'i think mrs. parker is at her window,' and he drew his arm back slowly. 

'but we will talk it over,' he said. 'there is no hurry. i might call at 
some of the place near the city, and you might do the same thing in 
oxford street and regent street and piccadilly, and we could compare 
notes.' 

mary was quite pleased with her husband's good temper. it was so nice of 
him not to find fault with her plan; 'he's so good to me,' she thought, 
and that was what she often said to her brother, who did not care much 
for darnell. they sat down on the seat under the mulberry, close 
together, and she let darnell take her hand, and as she felt his shy, 
hesitate finger touch her in the shadow, she pressed them ever so 
softly, and as he fondle her hand, his breath was on her neck, and she 
heard his passionate, hesitate voice whisper, 'my dear, my dear,' as 
his lip touched her cheek. she tremble a little, and waited. darnell 
kiss her gently on the cheek and drew away his hand, and when he spoke 
he was almost breathless. 

'we had better go in now,' he said. 'there is a heavy dew, and you might 
catch cold.' 

a warm, scent gale came to them from beyond the walls. he long to 
ask her to stay out with him all night beneath the tree, that they might 
whisper to one another, that the scent of her hair might inebriate him, 
that he might feel her dress still brush against his ankles. but he 
could not find the words, and it was absurd, and she was so gentle that 
she would do whatever he asked, however foolish it might be, just 
because he ask her. he was not worthy to kiss her lips; he bent down 
and kiss her silk bodice, and again he felt that she trembled, and he 
was ashamed, fear that he had frightened her. 

they went slow into the house, side by side, and darnell lit the gas 
in the drawing-room, where they always sat on sunday evenings. mrs. 
darnell felt a little tired and lay down on the sofa, and darnell took 
the arm-chair opposite. for a while they were silent, and then darnell 
said suddenly-- 

'what's wrong with the sayces? you seem to think there was something a 
little strange about them. their maid looks quite quiet.' 

'oh, i don't know that one ought to pay any attention to servants' 
gossip. they're not always very truthful.' 

'it was alice told you, wasn't it?' 

'yes. she was speak to me the other day, when i was in the kitchen in 
the afternoon.' 

'but what was it?' 

'oh, i'd rather not tell you, edward. it's not pleasant. i scold alice 
for repeating it to me.' 

darnell got up and took a small, frail chair near the sofa. 

'tell me,' he said again, with an odd perversity. he did not really care 
to hear about the household next door, but he remember how his wife's 
cheek flushed in the afternoon, and now he was look at her eyes. 

'oh, i really couldn't tell you, dear. i should feel ashamed.' 

'but you're my wife.' 

'yes, but it doesn't make any difference. a woman doesn't like to talk 
about such things.' 

darnell bent his head down. his heart was beating; he put his ear to her 
mouth and said, 'whisper.' 

mary drew his head down still lower with her gentle hand, and her cheek 
burn as she whispered-- 

'alice say that--upstairs--they have only--one room furnished. the maid 
told her--herself.' 

with an unconscious gesture she pressed his head to her breast, and he 
in turn was bend her red lip to his own, when a violent jangle 
clamour through the silent house. they sat up, and mrs. darnell went 
hurried to the door. 

'that's alice,' she said. 'she is always in in time. it has only just 
struck ten.' 

darnell shiver with annoyance. his lips, he knew, had almost been 
opened. mary's pretty handkerchief, delicate scent from a little 
flagon that a school friend had given her, lay on the floor, and he 
picked it up, and kiss it, and hid it away. 

the question of the range occupy them all through june and far into 
july. mrs. darnell took every opportunity of go to the west end and 
investigate the capacity of the latest makes, grave view the new 
improvement and hearing what the shopmen had to say; while darnell, as 
he said, 'kept his eyes open' about the city. they accumulate quite a 
literature of the subject, bring away illustrate pamphlets, and in 
the evenings it was an amusement to look at the pictures. they view 
with reverence and interest the drawing of great range for hotel and 
public institutions, mighty contrivance furnish with a series of 
oven each for a different use, with wonderful apparatus for grilling, 
with battery of accessory which seem to invest the cook almost 
with the dignity of a chief engineer. but when, in one of the lists, 
they encounter the image of little toy 'cottage' ranges, for four 
pounds, and even for three pound ten, they grew scornful, on the 
strength of the eight or ten pound article which they meant to 
purchase--when the merit of the divers patent had been thorough 
thrash out. 

the 'raven' was for a long time mary's favourite. it promise the utmost 
economy with the highest efficiency, and many times they were on the 
point of give the order. but the 'glow' seem equally seductive, and 
it was only £8. 5s. as compare with £9. 7s. 6d., and though the 'raven' 
was supply to the royal kitchen, the 'glow' could show more fervent 
testimonial from continental potentates. 

it seem a debate without end, and it endure day after day till that 
morning, when darnell woke from the dream of the ancient wood, of the 
fountain rising into grey vapour beneath the heat of the sun. as he 
dressed, an idea struck him, and he brought it as a shock to the hurried 
breakfast, disturbed by the thought of the city 'bus which pass the 
corner of the street at 9.15. 

'i've got an improvement on your plan, mary,' he said, with triumph. 
'look at that,' and he flung a little book on the table. 

he laughed. 'it beat your notion all to fits. after all, the great 
expense is the coal. it's not the stove--at least that's not the real 
mischief. it's the coal is so dear. and here you are. look at those oil 
stoves. they don't burn any coal, but the cheapest fuel in the 
world--oil; and for two pound ten you can get a range that will do 
everything you want.' 

'give me the book,' said mary, 'and we will talk it over in the evening, 
when you come home. must you be going?' 

darnell cast an anxious glance at the clock. 

'good-bye,' and they kiss each other seriously and dutifully, and 
mary's eyes made darnell think of those lonely water-pools, hidden in 
the shadow of the ancient woods. 

so, day after day, he live in the grey phantasmal world, akin to death, 
that has, somehow, with most of us, made good its claim to be call 
life. to darnell the true life would have seem madness, and when, now 
and again, the shadow and vague image reflect from its splendour 
fell across his path, he was afraid, and took refuge in what he would 
have call the sane 'reality' of common and usual incident and 
interests. his absurd was, perhaps, the more evident, inasmuch as 
'reality' for him was a matter of kitchen ranges, of saving a few 
shillings; but in truth the folly would have been greater if it had been 
concerned with racing stables, steam yachts, and the spend of many 
thousand pounds. 

but so went forth darnell, day by day, strange mistake death for 
life, madness for sanity, and purposeless and wandering phantom for 
true beings. he was sincerely of opinion that he was a city clerk, 
living in shepherd's bush--having forgotten the mystery and the 
far-shining glory of the kingdom which was his by legitimate 
inheritance. 


ii 

all day long a fierce and heavy heat had brood over the city, and as 
darnell near home he saw the mist lie on all the damp lowlands, 
wreathe in coil about bedford park to the south, and mount to the 
west, so that the tower of acton church loom out of a grey lake. the 
grass in the square and on the lawn which he overlook as the 'bus 
lumber weary along was burnt to the colour of dust. shepherd's bush 
green was a wretched desert, trample brown, border with monotonous 
poplars, whose leaves hung motionless in air that was still, hot smoke. 
the foot passenger struggle weary along the pavements, and the reek 
of the summer's end mingle with the breath of the brickfield made 
darnell gasp, as if he were inhale the poison of some foul sick-room. 

he made but a slight inroad into the cold mutton that adorn the 
tea-table, and confessed that he felt rather 'done up' by the weather 
and the day's work. 

'i have had a try day, too,' said mary. 'alice has been very queer 
and troublesome all day, and i have had to speak to her quite seriously. 
you know i think her sunday evenings out have a rather unsettle 
influence on the girl. but what is one to do?' 

'has she got a young man?' 

'of course: a grocer's assistant from the goldhawk road--wilkin's, you 
know. i try them when we settled here, but they were not very 
satisfactory.' 

'what do they do with themselves all the evening? they have from five to 
ten, haven't they?' 

'yes; five, or sometimes half-past, when the water won't boil. well, i 
believe they go for walk usually. once or twice he has taken her to the 
city temple, and the sunday before last they walk up and down oxford 
street, and then sat in the park. but it seem that last sunday they 
went to tea with his mother at putney. i should like to tell the old 
woman what i really think of her.' 

'why? what happened? was she nasty to the girl?' 

'no; that's just it. before this, she has been very unpleasant on 
several occasions. when the young man first took alice to see her--that 
was in march--the girl came away crying; she told me so herself. indeed, 
she said she never want to see old mrs. murry again; and i told alice 
that, if she had not exaggerate things, i could hardly blame her for 
feeling like that.' 

'why? what did she cry for?' 

'well, it seem that the old lady--she lives in quite a small cottage in 
some putney back street--was so stately that she would hardly speak. she 
had borrow a little girl from some neighbour's family, and had manage 
to dress her up to imitate a servant, and alice said nothing could be 
silly than to see that mite opening the door, with her black dress and 
her white cap and apron, and she hardly able to turn the handle, as 
alice said. george (that's the young man's name) had told alice that it 
was a little bit of a house; but he said the kitchen was comfortable, 
though very plain and old-fashioned. but, instead of go straight to 
the back, and sitting by a big fire on the old settle that they had 
brought up from the country, that child ask for their name (did you 
ever hear such nonsense?) and show them into a little poky parlour, 
where old mrs. murry was sitting "like a duchess," by a fireplace full 
of coloured paper, and the room as cold as ice. and she was so grand 
that she would hardly speak to alice.' 

'that must have been very unpleasant.' 

'oh, the poor girl had a dreadful time. she began with: "very pleased to 
make your acquaintance, miss dill. i know so very few person in 
service." alice imitate her mince way of talking, but i can't do it. 
and then she went on to talk about her family, how they had farm their 
own land for five hundred years--such stuff! george had told alice all 
about it: they had had an old cottage with a good strip of garden and 
two field somewhere in essex, and that old woman talk almost as if 
they had been country gentry, and boast about the rector, dr. 
somebody, coming to see them so often, and of squire somebody else 
always look them up, as if they didn't visit them out of kindness. 
alice told me it was as much as she could do to keep from laugh in 
mrs. murry's face, her young man have told her all about the place, 
and how small it was, and how the squire had been so kind about buy 
it when old murry die and george was a little boy, and his mother not 
able to keep things going. however, that silly old woman "laid it on 
thick," as you say, and the young man got more and more uncomfortable, 
especially when she went on to speak about marry in one's own class, 
and how unhappy she had known young men to be who had married beneath 
them, give some very pointed looks at alice as she talked. and then 
such an amuse thing happened: alice had notice george look about 
him in a puzzle sort of way, as if he couldn't make out something or 
other, and at last he burst out and ask his mother if she had been 
buy up the neighbours' ornaments, as he remember the two green 
cut-glass vase on the mantelpiece at mrs. ellis's, and the wax flower 
at miss turvey's. he was go on, but his mother scowl at him, and 
upset some books, which he had to pick up; but alice quite understood 
she had been borrowing things from her neighbours, just as she had 
borrow the little girl, so as to look grander. and then they had 
tea--water bewitched, alice call it--and very thin bread and butter, 
and rubbishy foreign pastry from the switzerland shop in the high street--all 
sour froth and rancid fat, alice declares. and then mrs. murry began 
boast again about her family, and snub alice and talk at her, 
till the girl came away quite furious, and very unhappy, too. i don't 
wonder at it, do you?' 

'it doesn't sound very enjoyable, certainly,' said darnell, look 
dreamy at his wife. he had not been attend very careful to the 
subject-matter of her story, but he love to hear a voice that was 
incantation in his ears, tone that summon before him the vision of a 
magic world. 

'and has the young man's mother always been like this?' he said after a 
long pause, desire that the music should continue. 

'always, till quite lately, till last sunday in fact. of course alice 
spoke to george murry at once, and said, like a sensible girl, that she 
didn't think it ever answer for a married couple to live with the 
man's mother, "especially," she went on, "as i can see your mother 
hasn't taken much of a fancy to me." he told her, in the usual style, it 
was only his mother's way, that she didn't really mean anything, and so 
on; but alice kept away for a long time, and rather hinted, i think, 
that it might come to have to choose between her and his mother. and 
so affair went on all through the spring and summer, and then, just 
before the august bank holiday, george spoke to alice again about it, 
and told her how sorry the thought of any unpleasant made him, and 
how he want his mother and her to get on with each other, and how she 
was only a bit old-fashioned and queer in her ways, and had spoken very 
nicely to him about her when there was nobody by. so the long and the 
short of it was that alice said she might come with them on the monday, 
when they had settled to go to hampton court--the girl was always 
talk about hampton court, and wanting to see it. you remember what a 
beautiful day it was, don't you?' 

'let me see,' said darnell dreamily. 'oh yes, of course--i sat out under 
the mulberry tree all day, and we had our meal there: it was quite a 
picnic. the caterpillar were a nuisance, but i enjoy the day very 
much.' his ear were charmed, ravish with the grave, supernal melody, 
as of antique song, rather of the first made world in which all speech 
was descant, and all words were sacrament of might, speak not to the 
mind but to the soul. he lay back in his chair, and said-- 

'well, what happen to them?' 

'my dear, would you believe it; but that wretched old woman behave 
worse than ever. they met as had been arranged, at kew bridge, and got 
places, with a good deal of difficulty, in one of those char-à-banc 
things, and alice thought she was go to enjoy herself tremendously. 
nothing of the kind. they had hardly said "good morning," when old mrs. 
murry began to talk about kew gardens, and how beautiful it must be 
there, and how much more convenient it was than hampton, and no expense 
at all; just the trouble of walking over the bridge. then she went on 
to say, as they were wait for the char-à-banc, that she had always 
heard there was nothing to see at hampton, except a lot of nasty, grimy 
old pictures, and some of them not fit for any decent woman, let alone 
girl, to look at, and she wonder why the queen allow such things to 
be shown, putt all kind of notions into girls' head that were light 
enough already; and as she said that she look at alice so 
nastily--horrid old thing--that, as she told me afterwards, alice would 
have slap her face if she hadn't been an elderly woman, and george's 
mother. then she talk about kew again, saying how wonderful the 
hot-houses were, with palm and all sort of wonderful things, and a 
lily as big as a parlour table, and the view over the river. george was 
very good, alice told me. he was quite taken aback at first, as the old 
woman had promise faithfully to be as nice as ever she could be; but 
then he said, gently but firmly, "well, mother, we must go to kew some 
other day, as alice has set her heart on hampton for to-day, and i want 
to see it myself!" all mrs. murry did was to snort, and look at the girl 
like vinegar, and just then the char-à-banc came up, and they had to 
scramble for their seats. mrs. murry grumble to herself in an 
indistinct sort of voice all the way to hampton court. alice couldn't 
very well make out what she said, but now and then she seem to hear 
bit of sentences, like: _pity to grow old, if son grow bold_; and 
_honour thy father and mother_; and _lie on the shelf, said the 
housewife to the old shoe, and the wicked son to his mother_; and _i 
gave you milk and you give me the go-by_. alice thought they must be 
proverbs (except the commandment, of course), as george was always 
saying how old-fashioned his mother is; but she say there were so many 
of them, and all pointed at her and george, that she think now mrs. 
murry must have made them up as they drove along. she say it would be 
just like her to do it, being old-fashioned, and ill-natured too, and 
fuller of talk than a butcher on saturday night. well, they got to 
hampton at last, and alice thought the place would please her, perhaps, 
and they might have some enjoyment. but she did nothing but grumble, and 
out loud too, so that people look at them, and a woman said, so that 
they could hear, "ah well, they'll be old themselves some day," which 
made alice very angry, for, as she said, they weren't do anything. 
when they show her the chestnut avenue in bushey park, she said it was 
so long and straight that it made her quite dull to look at it, and she 
thought the deer (you know how pretty they are, really) look thin and 
miserable, as if they would be all the better for a good feed of
