hog-wash, with plenty of meal in it. she said she knew they weren't 
happy by the look in their eyes, which seem to tell her that their 
keeper beat them. it was the same with everything; she said she 
remember market-gardens in hammersmith and gunnersbury that had a 
better show of flowers, and when they took her to the place where the 
water is, under the trees, she burst out with its being rather hard to 
tramp her off her leg to show her a common canal, with not so much as a 
barge on it to liven it up a bit. she went on like that the whole day, 
and alice told me she was only too thankful to get home and get rid of 
her. wasn't it wretched for the girl?' 

'it must have been, indeed. but what happen last sunday?' 

'that's the most extraordinary thing of all. i notice that alice was 
rather queer in her manner this morning; she was a long time washing 
up the breakfast things, and she answer me quite sharp when i call 
to her to ask when she would be ready to help me with the wash; and when 
i went into the kitchen to see about something, i notice that she was 
go about her work in a sulky sort of way. so i ask her what was the 
matter, and then it all came out. i could scarcely believe my own ear 
when she mumble out something about mrs. murry thinking she could do 
very much better for herself; but i ask her one question after another 
till i had it all out of her. it just show one how foolish and 
empty-headed these girl are. i told her she was no better than a 
weather-cock. if you will believe me, that horrid old woman was quite 
another person when alice went to see her the other night. why, i can't 
think, but so she was. she told the girl how pretty she was; what a neat 
figure she had; how well she walked; and how she'd known many a girl not 
half so clever or well-looking earn her twenty-five or thirty pound 
a year, and with good families. she seem to have gone into all sort of 
details, and made elaborate calculation as to what she would be able to 
save, "with decent folks, who don't screw, and pinch, and lock up 
everything in the house," and then she went off into a lot of 
hypocrite nonsense about how fond she was of alice, and how she 
could go to her grave in peace, knowing how happy her dear george would 
be with such a good wife, and about her savings from good wages helping 
to set up a little home, ending up with "and, if you take an old woman's 
advice, deary, it won't be long before you hear the marriage bells."' 

'i see,' said darnell; 'and the upshot of it all is, i suppose, that the 
girl is thorough dissatisfied?' 

'yes, she is so young and silly. i talk to her, and remind her of 
how nasty old mrs. murry had been, and told her that she might change 
her place and change for the worse. i think i have persuade her to 
think it over quietly, at all events. do you know what it is, edward? i 
have an idea. i believe that wicked old woman is try to get alice to 
leave us, that she may tell her son how changeable she is; and i suppose 
she would make up some of her stupid old proverbs: "a changeable wife, a 
troublesome life," or some nonsense of the kind. horrid old thing!' 

'well, well,' said darnell, 'i hope she won't go, for your sake. it 
would be such a bother for you, hunting for a fresh servant.' 

he refill his pipe and smoke placidly, refresh somewhat after the 
empty and the burden of the day. the france window was wide open, 
and now at last there came a breath of quicken air, distill by the 
night from such tree as still wore green in that arid valley. the song 
to which darnell had listen in rapture, and now the breeze, which even 
in that dry, grim suburb still bore the word of the woodland, had 
summon the dream to his eyes, and he meditate over matter that his 
lip could not express. 

'she must, indeed, be a villainous old woman,' he said at length. 

'old mrs. murry? of course she is; the mischievous old thing! try to 
take the girl from a comfortable place where she is happy.' 

'yes; and not to like hampton court! that show how bad she must be, 
more than anything.' 

'it is beautiful, isn't it?' 

'i shall never forget the first time i saw it. it was soon after i went 
into the city; the first year. i had my holiday in july, and i was 
get such a small salary that i couldn't think of go away to the 
seaside, or anything like that. i remember one of the other men want 
me to come with him on a walking tour in kent. i should have like that, 
but the money wouldn't run to it. and do you know what i did? i live in 
great college street then, and the first day i was off, i stay in bed 
till past dinner-time, and lounge about in an arm-chair with a pipe all 
the afternoon. i had got a new kind of tobacco--one and four for the 
two-ounce packet--much dear than i could afford to smoke, and i was 
enjoy it immensely. it was awfully hot, and when i shut the window 
and drew down the red blind it grew hotter; at five o'clock the room was 
like an oven. but i was so pleased at not have to go into the city, 
that i didn't mind anything, and now and again i read bit from a queer 
old book that had belong to my poor dad. i couldn't make out what a 
lot of it meant, but it fitted in somehow, and i read and smoke till 
tea-time. then i went out for a walk, thinking i should be better for a 
little fresh air before i went to bed; and i went wandering away, not 
much notice where i was going, turning here and there as the fancy 
took me. i must have gone mile and miles, and a good many of them round 
and round, as they say they do in australia if they lose their way in 
the bush; and i am sure i couldn't have gone exactly the same way all 
over again for any money. anyhow, i was still in the street when the 
twilight came on, and the lamp-lighters were trot round from one 
lamp to another. it was a wonderful night: i wish you had been there, my 
dear.' 

'i was quite a little girl then.' 

'yes, i suppose you were. well, it was a wonderful night. i remember, i 
was walking in a little street of little grey house all alike, with 
stucco coping and stucco door-posts; there were brass plate on a lot 
of the doors, and one had "maker of shell boxes" on it, and i was quite 
pleased, as i had often wonder where those box and things that you 
buy at the seaside came from. a few children were play about in the 
road with some rubbish or other, and men were singing in a small 
public-house at the corner, and i happen to look up, and i notice 
what a wonderful colour the sky had turned. i have seen it since, but i 
don't think it has ever been quite what it was that night, a dark blue, 
glowing like a violet, just as they say the sky looks in foreign 
countries. i don't know why, but the sky or something made me feel quite 
queer; everything seem change in a way i couldn't understand. i 
remember, i told an old gentleman i knew then--a friend of my poor 
father's, he's been dead for five years, if not more--about how i felt, 
and he look at me and said something about fairyland; i don't know 
what he meant, and i dare say i didn't explain myself properly. but, do 
you know, for a moment or two i felt as if that little back street was 
beautiful, and the noise of the children and the men in the public-house 
seem to fit in with the sky and become part of it. you know that old 
saying about "treading on air" when one is glad! well, i really felt 
like that as i walked, not exactly like air, you know, but as if the 
pavement was velvet or some very soft carpet. and then--i suppose it was 
all my fancy--the air seem to smell sweet, like the incense in 
catholic churches, and my breath came queer and catchy, as it do when 
one get very excited about anything. i felt altogether stranger than 
i've ever felt before or since.' 

darnell stop sudden and look up at his wife. she was watch him 
with part lips, with eager, wonder eyes. 

'i hope i'm not tire you, dear, with all this story about nothing. you 
have had a worry day with that stupid girl; hadn't you better go to 
bed?' 

'oh, no, please, edward. i'm not a bit tired now. i love to hear you 
talk like that. please go on.' 

'well, after i had walk a bit further, that queer sort of feeling 
seem to fade away. i said a bit further, and i really thought i had 
been walking about five minutes, but i had look at my watch just 
before i got into that little street, and when i look at it again it 
was eleven o'clock. i must have done about eight miles. i could scarcely 
believe my own eyes, and i thought my watch must have gone mad; but i 
found out afterwards it was perfectly right. i couldn't make it out, and 
i can't now; i assure you the time pass as if i walk up one side of 
edna road and down the other. but there i was, right in the open 
country, with a cool wind blow on me from a wood, and the air full of 
soft rustling sounds, and note of bird from the bushes, and the 
singing noise of a little brook that ran under the road. i was standing 
on the bridge when i took out my watch and struck a wax light to see the 
time; and it came upon me sudden what a strange evening it had been. 
it was all so different, you see, to what i had been do all my life, 
particularly for the year before, and it almost seem as if i couldn't 
be the man who had been go into the city every day in the morning and 
coming back from it every evening after writing a lot of uninterest 
letters. it was like being pitch all of a sudden from one world into 
another. well, i found my way back somehow or other, and as i went along 
i made up my mind how i'd spend my holiday. i said to myself, "i'll have 
a walking tour as well as ferrars, only mine is to be a tour of london 
and its environs," and i had got it all settled when i let myself into 
the house about four o'clock in the morning, and the sun was shining, 
and the street almost as still as the wood at midnight!' 

'i think that was a capital idea of yours. did you have your tour? did 
you buy a map of london?' 

'i had the tour all right. i didn't buy a map; that would have spoilt 
it, somehow; to see everything plot out, and named, and measured. 
what i want was to feel that i was go where nobody had been before. 
that's nonsense, isn't it? as if there could be any such place in 
london, or england either, for the matter of that.' 

'i know what you mean; you want to feel as if you were go on a sort 
of voyage of discovery. isn't that it?' 

'exactly, that's what i was try to tell you. besides, i didn't want 
to buy a map. i made a map.' 

'how do you mean? did you make a map out of your head?' 

'i'll tell you about it afterwards. but do you really want to hear about 
my grand tour?' 

'of course i do; it must have been delightful. i call it a most original 
idea.' 

'well, i was quite full of it, and what you said just now about a voyage 
of discovery remind me of how i felt then. when i was a boy i was 
awfully fond of reading of great travellers--i suppose all boys are--and 
of sailor who were driven out of their course and found themselves in 
latitudes where no ship had ever sail before, and of people who 
discover wonderful city in strange countries; and all the second day 
of my holiday i was feeling just as i use to when i read these books. 
i didn't get up till pretty late. i was tired to death after all those 
mile i had walked; but when i had finished my breakfast and fill my 
pipe, i had a grand time of it. it was such nonsense, you know; as if 
there could be anything strange or wonderful in london.' 

'why shouldn't there be?' 

'well, i don't know; but i have thought afterwards what a silly lad i 
must have been. anyhow, i had a great day of it, plan what i would 
do, half making-believe--just like a kid--that i didn't know where i 
might find myself, or what might happen to me. and i was enormously 
pleased to think it was all my secret, that nobody else knew anything 
about it, and that, whatever i might see, i would keep to myself. i had 
always felt like that about the books. of course, i love reading them, 
but it seem to me that, if i had been a discoverer, i would have kept 
my discovery a secret. if i had been columbus, and, if it could 
possibly have been managed, i would have found america all by myself, 
and never have said a word about it to anybody. fancy! how beautiful it 
would be to be walking about in one's own town, and talk to people, 
and all the while to have the thought that one knew of a great world 
beyond the seas, that nobody else dream of. i should have love that! 

'and that is exactly what i felt about the tour i was go to make. i 
made up my mind that nobody should know; and so, from that day to this, 
nobody has heard a word of it.' 

'but you are go to tell me?' 

'you are different. but i don't think even you will hear everything; not 
because i won't, but because i can't tell many of the things i saw.' 

'things you saw? then you really did see wonderful, strange things in 
london?' 

'well, i did and i didn't. everything, or pretty nearly everything, that 
i saw is standing still, and hundred of thousand of people have look 
at the same sights--there were many place that the fellow in the 
office knew quite well, i found out afterwards. and then i read a book 
call "london and its surroundings." but (i don't know how it is) 
neither the men at the office nor the writer of the book seem to have 
seen the things that i did. that's why i stop reading the book; it 
seem to take the life, the real heart, out of everything, making it as 
dry and stupid as the stuff bird in a museum. 

'i thought about what i was go to do all that day, and went to bed 
early, so as to be fresh. i knew wonderful little about london, 
really; though, except for an odd week now and then, i had spent all my 
life in town. of course i knew the main streets--the strand, regent 
street, oxford street, and so on--and i knew the way to the school i 
use to go to when i was a boy, and the way into the city. but i had 
just kept to a few tracks, as they say the sheep do on the mountains; 
and that made it all the easy for me to imagine that i was go to 
discover a new world.' 

darnell pause in the stream of his talk. he look keen at his wife 
to see if he were weary her, but her eyes gaze at him with unabated 
interest--one would have almost said that they were the eyes of one who 
long and half expect to be initiate into the mysteries, who knew 
not what great wonder was to be revealed. she sat with her back to the 
open window, frame in the sweet dusk of the night, as if a painter had 
made a curtain of heavy velvet behind her; and the work that she had 
been do had fallen to the floor. she support her head with her two 
hand placed on each side of her brow, and her eyes were as the well in 
the wood of which darnell dream in the night-time and in the day. 

'and all the strange tale i had ever heard were in my head that 
morning,' he went on, as if continue the thought that had fill his 
mind while his lip were silent. 'i had gone to bed early, as i told 
you, to get a thorough rest, and i had set my alarum clock to wake me 
at three, so that i might set out at an hour that was quite strange for 
the beginning of a journey. there was a hush in the world when i awoke, 
before the clock had rung to arouse me, and then a bird began to sing 
and twitter in the elm tree that grew in the next garden, and i look 
out of the window, and everything was still, and the morning air 
breathe in pure and sweet, as i had never known it before. my room was 
at the back of the house, and most of the garden had tree in them, and 
beyond these tree i could see the back of the house of the next 
street rising like the wall of an old city; and as i look the sun 
rose, and the great light came in at my window, and the day began. 

'and i found that when i was once out of the street just about me that 
i knew, some of the queer feeling that had come to me two days before 
came back again. it was not nearly so strong, the street no long 
smelt of incense, but still there was enough of it to show me what a 
strange world i pass by. there were things that one may see again and 
again in many london streets: a vine or a fig tree on a wall, a lark 
singing in a cage, a curious shrub blossom in a garden, an odd shape 
of a roof, or a balcony with an uncommon-looking trellis-work in iron. 
there's scarcely a street, perhaps, where you won't see one or other of 
such things as these; but that morning they rose to my eyes in a new 
light, as if i had on the magic spectacles in the fairy tale, and just 
like the man in the fairy tale, i went on and on in the new light. i 
remember go through wild land on a high place; there were pools of 
water shining in the sun, and great white house in the middle of dark, 
rock pines, and then on the turn of the height i came to a little 
lane that went aside from the main road, a lane that led to a wood, and 
in the lane was a little old shadow house, with a bell turret in the 
roof, and a porch of trellis-work all dim and fade into the colour of 
the sea; and in the garden there were grow tall, white lilies, just 
as we saw them that day we went to look at the old pictures; they were 
shining like silver, and they fill the air with their sweet scent. it 
was from near that house i saw the valley and high place far away in 
the sun. so, as i say, i went "on and on," by wood and fields, till i 
came to a little town on the top of a hill, a town full of old house 
bowing to the ground beneath their years, and the morning was so still 
that the blue smoke rose up straight into the sky from all the 
roof-tops, so still that i heard far down in the valley the song of a 
boy who was singing an old song through the street as he went to 
school, and as i pass through the awakening town, beneath the old, 
grave houses, the church bell began to ring. 

'it was soon after i had left this town behind me that i found the 
strange road. i saw it branch off from the dusty high road, and it 
look so green that i turn aside into it, and soon i felt as if i had 
really come into a new country. i don't know whether it was one of the 
road the old roman made that my father use to tell me about; but it 
was cover with deep, soft turf, and the great tall hedge on each side 
look as if they had not been touched for a hundred years; they had 
grown so broad and high and wild that they met overhead, and i could 
only get glimpse here and there of the country through which i was 
passing, as one pass in a dream. the strange road led me on and on, 
up and down hill; sometimes the rose bush had grown so thick that i 
could scarcely make my way between them, and sometimes the road 
broaden out into a green, and in one valley a brook, span by an old 
wooden bridge, ran across it. i was tired, and i found a soft and shady 
place beneath an ash tree, where i must have slept for many hours, for 
when i woke up it was late in the afternoon. so i went on again, and at 
last the green road came out into the highway, and i look up and saw 
another town on a high place with a great church in the middle of it, 
and when i went up to it there was a great organ sound from within, 
and the choir was singing.' 

there was a rapture in darnell's voice as he spoke, that made his story 
well-nigh swell into a song, and he drew a long breath as the words 
ended, fill with the thought of that far-off summer day, when some 
enchantment had informed all common things, transmute them into a 
great sacrament, cause earthly works to glow with the fire and the 
glory of the everlasting light. 

and some splendour of that light shone on the face of mary as she sat 
still against the sweet gloom of the night, her dark hair making her 
face more radiant. she was silent for a little while, and then she 
spoke-- 

'oh, my dear, why have you wait so long to tell me these wonderful 
things? i think it is beautiful. please go on.' 

'i have always been afraid it was all nonsense,' said darnell. 'and i 
don't know how to explain what i feel. i didn't think i could say so 
much as i have to-night.' 

'and did you find it the same day after day?' 

'all through the tour? yes, i think every journey was a success. of 
course, i didn't go so far afield every day; i was too tired. often i 
rest all day long, and went out in the evening, after the lamp were 
lit, and then only for a mile or two. i would roam about old, dim 
squares, and hear the wind from the hill whisper in the trees; and 
when i knew i was within call of some great glittering street, i was 
sunk in the silence of ways where i was almost the only passenger, and 
the lamp were so few and faint that they seem to give out shadow 
instead of light. and i would walk slowly, to and fro, perhaps for an 
hour at a time, in such dark streets, and all the time i felt what i 
told you about its being my secret--that the shadow, and the dim lights, 
and the cool of the evening, and tree that were like dark low cloud 
were all mine, and mine alone, that i was living in a world that nobody 
else knew of, into which no one could enter. 

'i remember one night i had gone farther. it was somewhere in the far 
west, where there are orchard and gardens, and great broad lawn that 
slope down to tree by the river. a great red moon rose that night 
through mists of sunset, and thin, filmy clouds, and i wander by a 
road that pass through the orchards, till i came to a little hill, 
with the moon showing above it glowing like a great rose. then i saw 
figures pass between me and the moon, one by one, in a long line, each 
bent double, with great pack upon their shoulders. one of them was 
singing, and then in the middle of the song i heard a horrible shrill 
laugh, in the thin cracked voice of a very old woman, and they 
disappear into the shadow of the trees. i suppose they were people 
go to work, or coming from work in the gardens; but how like it was 
to a nightmare! 

'i can't tell you about hampton; i should never finish talking. i was 
there one evening, not long before they closed the gates, and there were 
very few people about. but the grey-red, silent, echo courts, and the 
flower fall into dreamland as the night came on, and the dark yew 
and shadowy-looking statues, and the far, still stretch of water 
beneath the avenues; and all melting into a blue mist, all being hidden 
from one's eyes, slowly, surely, as if veil were dropped, one by one, 
on a great ceremony! oh! my dear, what could it mean? far away, across 
the river, i heard a soft bell ring three times, and three times, and 
again three times, and i turn away, and my eyes were full of tears. 

'i didn't know what it was when i came to it; i only found out 
afterwards that it must have been hampton court. one of the men in the 
office told me he had taken an a. b. c. girl there, and they had great 
fun. they got into the maze and couldn't get out again, and then they 
went on the river and were nearly drowned. he told me there were some 
spicy pictures in the galleries; his girl shriek with laughter, so he 
said.' 

mary quite disregard this interlude. 

'but you told me you had made a map. what was it like?' 

'i'll show it you some day, if you want to see it. i marked down all the 
place i had gone to, and made signs--things like queer letters--to 
remind me of what i had seen. nobody but myself could understand it. i 
want to draw pictures, but i never learnt how to draw, so when i try 
nothing was like what i want it to be. i try to draw a picture of 
that town on the hill that i came to on the evening of the first day; i 
want to make a steep hill with house on top, and in the middle, but 
high above them, the great church, all spire and pinnacles, and above 
it, in the air, a cup with ray coming from it. but it wasn't a success. 
i made a very strange sign for hampton court, and gave it a name that i 
made up out of my head.' 

the darnell avoid one another's eyes as they sat at breakfast the 
next morning. the air had lighten in the night, for rain had fallen at 
dawn; and there was a bright blue sky, with vast white cloud rolling 
across it from the south-west, and a fresh and joyous wind blew in at 
the open window; the mists had vanished. and with the mists there seem 
to have vanish also the sense of strange things that had possessed 
mary and her husband the night before; and as they look out into the 
clear light they could scarcely believe that the one had spoken and the 
other had listen a few hour before to history very far remove from 
the usual current of their thought and of their lives. they glance 
shy at one another, and spoke of common things, of the question 
whether alice would be corrupt by the insidious mrs. murry, or whether 
mrs. darnell would be able to persuade the girl that the old woman must 
be actuate by the worst motives. 

'and i think, if i were you,' said darnell, as he went out, 'i should 
step over to the stores and complain of their meat. that last piece of 
beef was very far from being up to the mark--full of sinew.' 


iii 

it might have been different in the evening, and darnell had mature a 
plan by which he hope to gain much. he intended to ask his wife if she 
would mind have only one gas, and that a good deal lowered, on the 
pretext that his eyes were tired with work; he thought many things might 
happen if the room were dim lit, and the window opened, so that they 
could sit and watch the night, and listen to the rustling murmur of the 
tree on the lawn. but his plan were made in vain, for when he got to 
the garden gate his wife, in tears, came forth to meet him. 

'oh, edward,' she began, 'such a dreadful thing has happened! i never 
like him much, but i didn't think he would ever do such awful things.' 

'what do you mean? who are you talk about? what has happened? is it 
alice's young man?' 

'no, no. but come in, dear. i can see that woman opposite watch us: 
she's always on the look out.' 

'now, what is it?' said darnell, as they sat down to tea. 'tell me, 
quick! you've quite frightened me.' 

'i don't know how to begin, or where to start. aunt marian has thought 
that there was something queer for weeks. and then she found--oh, well, 
the long and short of it is that uncle robert has been carry on 
dreadfully with some horrid girl, and aunt has found out everything!' 

'lord! you don't say so! the old rascal! why, he must be near seventy 
than sixty!' 

'he's just sixty-five; and the money he has given her----' 

the first shock of surprise over, darnell turn resolute to his 
mince. 

'we'll have it all out after tea,' he said; 'i am not go to have my 
meal spoilt by that old fool of a nixon. fill up my cup, will you, 
dear?' 

'excellent mince this,' he went on, calmly. 'a little lemon juice and a 
bit of ham in it? i thought there was something extra. alice all right 
to-day? that's good. i expect she's get over all that nonsense.' 

he went on calm chatter in a manner that astonish mrs. darnell, 
who felt that by the fall of uncle robert the natural order had been 
inverted, and had scarcely touched food since the intelligence had 
arrive by the second post. she had start out to keep the appointment 
her aunt had made early in the morning, and had spent most of the day in 
a first-class waiting-room at victoria station, where she had heard all 
the story. 

'now,' said darnell, when the table had been cleared, 'tell us all about 
it. how long has it been go on?' 

'aunt think now, from little things she remembers, that it must have 
been go on for a year at least. she say there has been a horrid kind 
of mystery about uncle's behaviour for a long time, and her nerves were 
quite shaken, as she thought he must be involved with anarchists, or 
something dreadful of the sort.' 

'what on earth made her think that?' 

'well, you see, once or twice when she was out walking with her husband, 
she has been startle by whistles, which seem to follow them 
everywhere. you know there are some nice country walk at barnet, and 
one in particular, in the field near totteridge, that uncle and aunt 
rather made a point of go to on fine sunday evenings. of course, this 
was not the first thing she noticed, but, at the time, it made a great 
impression on her mind; she could hardly get a wink of sleep for week 
and weeks.' 

'whistling?' said darnell. 'i don't quite understand. why should she be 
frightened by whistling?' 

'i'll tell you. the first time it happen was one sunday in last may. 
aunt had a fancy they were being follow a sunday or two before, but 
she didn't see or hear anything, except a sort of crackling noise in the 
hedge. but this particular sunday they had hardly got through the stile 
into the fields, when she heard a peculiar kind of low whistle. she took 
no notice, thinking it was no concern of hers or her husband's, but as 
they went on she heard it again, and then again, and it follow them 
the whole walk, and it made her so uncomfortable, because she didn't 
know where it was coming from or who was do it, or why. then, just as 
they got out of the field into the lane, uncle said he felt quite 
faint, and he thought he would try a little brandy at the "turpin's

saw his face was quite purple--more like apoplexy, as she says, than 
faint fits, which make people look a sort of greenish-white. but she 
said nothing, and thought perhaps uncle had a peculiar way of faint

slip into the public, and aunt say she thought she saw a little 
figure rise out of the dusk and slip in after him, but she couldn't be 
sure. and when uncle came out he look red instead of purple, and said 
he felt much better; and so they went home quiet together, and nothing 
more was said. you see, uncle had said nothing about the whistling, and 
aunt had been so frightened that she didn't dare speak, for fear they 
might be both shot. 

'she wasn't thinking anything more about it, when two sunday afterwards 
the very same thing happen just as it had before. this time aunt 
pluck up a spirit, and ask uncle what it could be. and what do you 
think he said? "birds, my dear, birds." of course aunt said to him that 
no bird that ever flew with wings made a noise like that: sly, and low, 
with pause in between; and then he said that many rare sort of bird 
live in north middlesex and hertfordshire. "nonsense, robert," said 
aunt, "how can you talk so, considering it has follow us all the way, 
for a mile or more?" and then uncle told her that some bird were so 
attach to man that they would follow one about for mile sometimes; he 
said he had just been reading about a bird like that in a book of 
travels. and do you know that when they got home he actually show her 
a piece in the "hertfordshire naturalist" which they took in to oblige a 
friend of theirs, all about rare bird found in the neighbourhood, all 
the most outlandish names, aunt says, that she had never heard or

purple sandpiper, which, the paper said, had "a low shrill note, 
constant repeated." and then he took down a book of siberian travels 
from the bookcase and show her a page which told how a man was 
follow by a bird all day long through a forest. and that's what aunt 
marian say vex her more than anything almost; to think that he should 
be so artful and ready with those books, twist them to his own wicked 
ends. but, at the time, when she was out walking, she simply couldn't 
make out what he meant by talk about bird in that random, silly sort 
of way, so unlike him, and they went on, that horrible whistle 
following them, she look straight ahead and walking fast, really 
feeling more huffy and put out than frightened. and when they got to the 
next stile, she got over and turn round, and "lo and behold," as she 
says, there was no uncle robert to be seen! she felt herself go quite 
white with alarm, thinking of that whistle, and making sure he'd been 
spirited away or snatch in some way or another, and she had just 
scream out "robert" like a mad woman, when he came quite slow round 
the corner, as cool as a cucumber, holding something in his hand. he 
said there were some flower he could never pass, and when aunt saw that 
he had got a dandelion torn up by the roots, she felt as if her head 
were go round.' 

mary's story was sudden interrupted. for ten minutes darnell had been 
writhe in his chair, suffering torture in his anxiety to avoid 
wound his wife's feelings, but the episode of the dandelion was too 
much for him, and he burst into a long, wild shriek of laughter, 
aggravate by suppression into the semblance of a red indian's 
war-whoop. alice, who was washing-up in the scullery, drop some three 
shillings' worth of china, and the neighbour ran out into their garden 
wonder if it were murder. mary gaze reproachful at her husband. 

'how can you be so unfeeling, edward?' she said, at length, when 
darnell had pass into the feeble of exhaustion. 'if you had seen 
the tear rolling down poor aunt marian's cheek as she told me, i don't 
think you would have laughed. i didn't think you were so hard-hearted.' 

'my dear mary,' said darnell, faintly, through sob and catching of the 
breath, 'i am awfully sorry. i know it's very sad, really, and i'm not 
unfeeling; but it is such an odd tale, now, isn't it? the sandpiper, you 
know, and then the dandelion!' 

his face twitch and he ground his teeth together. mary look grave 
at him for a moment, and then she put her hand to her face, and darnell 
could see that she also shook with merriment. 

'i am as bad as you,' she said, at last. 'i never thought of it in that 
way. i'm glad i didn't, or i should have laugh in aunt marian's face, 
and i wouldn't have done that for the world. poor old thing; she cry 
as if her heart would break. i met her at victoria, as she ask me, and 
we had some soup at a confectioner's. i could scarcely touch it; her 
tear kept drop into the plate all the time; and then we went to the 
waiting-room at the station, and she cry there terribly.' 

'well,' said darnell, 'what happen next? i won't laugh any more.' 

'no, we mustn't; it's much too horrible for a joke. well, of course aunt 
went home and wonder and wonder what could be the matter, and try 
to think it out, but, as she says, she could make nothing of it. she 
began to be afraid that uncle's brain was give way through overwork, 
as he had stop in the city (as he said) up to all hour lately, and 
he had to go to yorkshire (wicked old story-teller!), about some very 
tiresome business connected with his leases. but then she reflect that 
however queer he might be getting, even his queer couldn't make 
whistle in the air, though, as she said, he was always a wonderful man. 
so she had to give that up; and then she wonder if there were anything 
the matter with her, as she had read about people who heard noise when 
there was really nothing at all. but that wouldn't do either, because 
though it might account for the whistling, it wouldn't account for the

any of uncle's queerness. so aunt said she could think of nothing but to 
read the bible every day from the beginning, and by the time she got

absent-minded, and not as nice to her as he might be, but she put that

the morning. still, she felt it was no good bother her head over what 
couldn't be made out or explain anyway, and she was just settle

happened. the whistle follow them just as it did before, and poor 
aunt set her teeth and said nothing to uncle, as she knew he would only 
tell her stories, and they were walking on, not saying a word, when 
something made her look back, and there was a horrible boy with red

was a dreadful face, with something unnatural about it, as if it had 
been a dwarf, and before she had time to have a good look, it pop 
back like lightning, and aunt all but faint away.' 

'a red-headed _boy_?' said darnell. 'i thought----what an extraordinary 
story this is. i've never heard of anything so queer. who was the boy?' 

'you will know in good time,' said mrs. darnell. 'it _is_ very strange, 
isn't it?' 

'strange!' darnell ruminate for a while. 

'i know what i think, mary,' he said at length. 'i don't believe a word 
of it. i believe your aunt is go mad, or has gone mad, and that she 
has delusions. the whole thing sound to me like the invention of a 
lunatic.' 

'you are quite wrong. every word is true, and if you will let me go on, 
you will understand how it all happened.' 

'very good, go ahead.' 

'let me see, where was i? oh, i know, aunt saw the boy grin in the 
hedge. yes, well, she was dreadfully frightened for a minute or two; 
there was something so queer about the face, but then she pluck up a 
spirit and said to herself, "after all, better a boy with red hair than 
a big man with a gun," and she made up her mind to watch uncle robert 
closely, as she could see by his look he knew all about it; he seem as

he said something to her about the fine sunset, she took no notice. 
"don't you hear what i say, marian?" he said, speak quite crossly,

knew he thought she hadn't heard the whistling. sudden uncle pretended 
to see a beautiful spray of honeysuckle high up in the hedge, and he 
said he must get it for aunt, only she must go on ahead, as it made him 
nervous to be watched. she said she would, but she just step aside 
behind a bush where there was a sort of cover in the hedge, and found 
she could see him quite well, though she scratch her face terribly 
with poke it into a rose bush. and in a minute or two out came the boy 
from behind the hedge, and she saw uncle and him talking, and she knew 
it was the same boy, as it wasn't dark enough to hide his flaming red 
head. and uncle put out his hand as if to catch him, but he just dart 
into the bush and vanished. aunt never said a word at the time, but 
that night when they got home she charged uncle with what she'd seen and 
ask him what it all meant. he was quite taken aback at first, and 
stammer and stutter and said a spy wasn't his notion of a good wife, 
but at last he made her swear secrecy, and told her that he was a very 
high freemason, and that the boy was an emissary of the order who 
brought him message of the greatest importance. but aunt didn't believe 
a word of it, as an uncle of hers was a mason, and he never behave like 
that. it was then she began to be afraid that it was really anarchists, 
or something of the kind, and every time the bell rang she thought that 
uncle had been found out, and the police had come for him.' 

'what nonsense! as if a man with house property would be an anarchist.' 

'well, she could see there must be some horrible secret, and she didn't 
know what else to think. and then she began to have the things through 
the post.' 

'things through the post! what do you mean by that?' 

'all sort of things; bit of broken bottle-glass, packed careful as 
if it were jewellery; parcel that unroll and unroll worse than 
china boxes, and then had "cat" in large letters when you came to the 
middle; old artificial teeth, a cake of red paint, and at last 
cockroaches.' 

'cockroaches by post! stuff and nonsense; your aunt's mad.' 

'edward, she show me the box; it was made to hold cigarettes, and 
there were three dead cockroach inside. and when she found a box of 
exactly the same kind, half-full of cigarettes, in uncle's great-coat 
pocket, then her head began to turn again.' 

darnell groaned, and stir uneasy in his chair, feeling that the 
tale of aunt marian's domestic trouble was putt on the semblance of 
an evil dream. 

'anything else?' he asked. 

'my dear, i haven't repeated half the things poor aunt told me this 
afternoon. there was the night she thought she saw a ghost in the 
shrubbery. she was anxious about some chicken that were just due to 
hatch out, so she went out after dark with some egg and bread-crumbs, in 
case they might be out. and just before her she saw a figure gliding by 
the rhododendrons. it look like a short, slim man dress as they 
use to be hundred of years ago; she saw the sword by his side, and 
the feather in his cap. she thought she should have died, she said, and 
though it was gone in a minute, and she try to make out it was all her 
fancy, she faint when she got into the house. uncle was at home that 
night, and when she came to and told him he ran out, and stay out for 
half-an-hour or more, and then came in and said he could find nothing; 
and the next minute aunt heard that low whistle just outside the window, 
and uncle ran out again.' 

'my dear mary, do let us come to the point. what on earth do it all 
lead to?' 

'haven't you guessed? why, of course it was that girl all the time.' 

'girl? i thought you said it was a boy with a red head?' 

'don't you see? she's an actress, and she dress up. she won't leave 
uncle alone. it wasn't enough that he was with her nearly every evening 
in the week, but she must be after him on sunday too. aunt found a 
letter the horrid thing had written, and so it has all come out. enid 
vivian she call herself, though i don't suppose she has any right to 
one name or the other. and the question is, what is to be done?' 

'let us talk of that again. i'll have a pipe, and then we'll go to bed.' 

they were almost asleep when mary said suddenly-- 

'doesn't it seem queer, edward? last night you were telling me such 
beautiful things, and to-night i have been talk about that 
disgraceful old man and his goings on.' 

'i don't know,' answer darnell, dreamily. 'on the wall of that great 
church upon the hill i saw all kind of strange grin monsters, 
carve in stone.' 

the misdemeanour of mr. robert nixon brought in their train 
consequence strange beyond imagination. it was not that they continue 
to develop on the somewhat fantastic lines of these first adventure 
which mrs. darnell had related; indeed, when 'aunt marian' came over to 
shepherd's bush, one sunday afternoon, darnell wonder how he had had 
the heart to laugh at the misfortune of a broken-hearted woman. 

he had never seen his wife's aunt before, and he was strange surprise 
when alice show her into the garden where they were sitting on the 
warm and misty sunday in september. to him, save during these latter 
days, she had always been associate with idea of splendour and 
success: his wife had always mention the nixon with a tinge of 
reverence; he had heard, many times, the epic of mr. nixon's struggle 
and of his slow but triumphant rise. mary had told the story as she had 
received it from her parents, beginning with the flight to london from 
some small, dull, and unprosperous town in the flattest of the midlands, 
long ago, when a young man from the country had great chance of 
fortune. robert nixon's father had been a grocer in the high street, and 
in after days the successful coal merchant and builder love to tell of 
that dull provincial life, and while he glorify his own victories, he 
gave his hearer to understand that he came of a race which had also 
known how to achieve. that had been long ago, he would explain: in the 
days when that rare citizen who desire to go to london or to york was 
forced to rise in the dead of night, and make his way, somehow or other, 
by ten mile of quagmirish, wandering lane to the great north road, 
there to meet the 'lightning' coach, a vehicle which stood to all the 
countryside as the visible and tangible embodiment of tremendous 
speed--'and indeed,' as nixon would add, 'it was always up to time, 
which is more than can be said of the dunham branch line nowadays!' it 
was in this ancient dunham that the nixon had wage successful trade 
for perhaps a hundred years, in a shop with bulge bay window look 
on the market-place. there was no competition, and the townsfolk, and 
well-to-do farmers, the clergy and the country families, look upon the 
house of nixon as an institution fixed as the town hall (which stood on 
roman pillars) and the parish church. but the change came: the railway 
crept near and nearer, the farmer and the country gentry became less 
well-to-do; the tanning, which was the local industry, suffer from a 
great business which had been establish in a large town, some twenty 
mile away, and the profit of the nixon grew less and less. hence the 
hegira of robert, and he would dilate on the poorness of his beginnings, 
how he saved, by little and little, from his sorry wage of city clerk, 
and how he and a fellow clerk, 'who had come into a hundred pounds,' saw 
an opening in the coal trade--and fill it. it was at this stage of 
robert's fortunes, still far from magnificent, that miss marian reynolds 
had encounter him, she being on a visit to friends in gunnersbury. 
afterwards, victory follow victory; nixon's wharf became a landmark to 
bargemen; his power stretch abroad, his dusky fleet went outwards to 
the sea, and inward by all the far reach of canals. lime, cement, and 
brick were add to his merchandise, and at last he hit upon the great 
stroke--that extensive taking up of land in the north of london. nixon 
himself ascribe this _coup_ to native sagacity, and the possession of 
capital; and there were also obscure rumour to the effect that some one 
or other had been 'done' in the course of the transaction. however that 
might be, the nixon grew wealthy to excess, and mary had often told her 
husband of the state in which they dwelt, of their liveried servants, of 
the glory of their drawing-room, of their broad lawn, shadow by a 
splendid and ancient cedar. and so darnell had somehow been led into 
conceive the lady of this demesne as a personage of no small pomp. he 
saw her, tall, of dignified port and presence, inclining, it might be, 
to some measure of obesity, such a measure as was not unbefitt in an 
elderly lady of position, who live well and live at ease. he even 
imagine a slight ruddy of complexion, which went very well with 
hair that was beginning to turn grey, and when he heard the door-bell 
ring, as he sat under the mulberry on the sunday afternoon, he bent 
forward to catch sight of this stately figure, clad, of course, in the 
richest, blackest silk, girt about with heavy chain of gold. 

he start with amaze when he saw the strange presence that follow 
the servant into the garden. mrs. nixon was a little, thin old woman, 
who bent as she feeble trot after alice; her eyes were on the ground, 
and she did not lift them when the darnell rose to greet her. she 
glance to the right, uneasily, as she shook hand with darnell, to the 
left when mary kiss her, and when she was placed on the garden seat 
with a cushion at her back, she look away at the back of the house in 
the next street. she was dress in black, it was true, but even darnell 
could see that her gown was old and shabby, that the fur trimming of her 
cape and the fur boa which was twist about her neck were dingy and 
disconsolate, and had all the melancholy air which fur wear when it is 
seen in a second-hand clothes-shop in a back street. and her 
gloves--they were black kid, wrinkle with much wear, fade to a bluish 
hue at the finger-tips, which show sign of painful mending. her hair, 
plastered over her forehead, look dull and colourless, though some 
greasy matter had evidently been use with a view of produce a 
becoming gloss, and on it perch an antique bonnet, adorn with black 
pendant that rattle paralytic one against the other. 

and there was nothing in mrs. nixon's face to correspond with the 
imaginary picture that darnell had made of her. she was sallow, 
wrinkled, pinched; her nose ran to a sharp point, and her red-rimmed 
eyes were a queer water-grey, that seem to shrink alike from the light 
and from encounter with the eyes of others. as she sat beside his wife 
on the green garden-seat, darnell, who occupy a wicker-chair brought 
out from the drawing-room, could not help feeling that this shadowy and 
evasive figure, mutter reply to mary's polite questions, was almost 
impossible remote from his conception of the rich and powerful aunt, 
who could give away a hundred pound as a mere birthday gift. she would 
say little at first; yes, she was feeling rather tired, it had been so 
hot all the way, and she had been afraid to put on lighter things as 
one never knew at this time of year what it might be like in the 
evenings; there were apt to be cold mists when the sun went down, and 
she didn't care to risk bronchitis. 

'i thought i should never get here,' she went on, raise her voice to 
an odd querulous pipe. 'i'd no notion it was such an out-of-the-way 
place, it's so many years since i was in this neighbourhood.' 

she wipe her eyes, no doubt thinking of the early days at turnham 
green, when she married nixon; and when the pocket-handkerchief had done 
its office she replace it in a shabby black bag which she clutch 
rather than carried. darnell noticed, as he watch her, that the bag 
seem full, almost to bursting, and he speculate idle as to the nature 
of its contents: correspondence, perhaps, he thought, further proof of 
uncle robert's treacherous and wicked dealings. he grew quite 
uncomfortable, as he sat and saw her glancing all the while furtive 
away from his wife and himself, and presently he got up and stroll 
away to the other end of the garden, where he lit his pipe and walk to 
and fro on the gravel walk, still astound at the gulf between the real 
and the imagine woman. 

presently he heard a hiss whisper, and he saw mrs. nixon's head 
incline to his wife's. mary rose and came towards him. 

'would you mind sitting in the drawing-room, edward?' she murmured. 
'aunt say she can't bring herself to discuss such a delicate matter 
before you. i dare say it's quite natural.' 

'very well, but i don't think i'll go into the drawing-room. i feel as 
if a walk would do me good. you mustn't be frightened if i am a little 
late,' he said; 'if i don't get back before your aunt goes, say good-bye 
to her for me.' 

he stroll into the main road, where the tram were hum to and fro. 
he was still confuse and perplexed, and he try to account for a 
certain relief he felt in remove himself from the presence of mrs. 
nixon. he told himself that her grief at her husband's ruffianly conduct 
was worthy of all pitiful respect, but at the same time, to his shame, 
he had felt a certain physical aversion from her as she sat in his 
garden in her dingy black, dab her red-rimmed eyes with a damp 
pocket-handkerchief. he had been to the zoo when he was a lad, and he 
still remember how he had shrunk with horror at the sight of certain 
reptile slow crawl over one another in their slimy pond. but he 
was enrage at the similarity between the two sensations, and he walk 
brisk on that level and monotonous road, look about him at the 
unhandsome spectacle of suburban london keeping sunday. 

there was something in the tinge of antiquity which still exist in 
acton that soothe his mind and drew it away from those unpleasant 
contemplations, and when at last he had penetrate rampart after rampart 
of brick, and heard no more the harsh shriek and laughter of the people 
who were enjoy themselves, he found a way into a little sheltered 
field, and sat down in peace beneath a tree, whence he could look out on 
a pleasant valley. the sun sank down beneath the hills, the cloud 
change into the likeness of blossom rose-gardens; and he still sat 
there in the gathering dark till a cool breeze blew upon him, and 
he rose with a sigh, and turn back to the brick rampart and the 
glimmer streets, and the noisy idle saunter to and fro in the 
procession of their dismal festival. but he was murmur to himself 
some words that seem a magic song, and it was with uplift heart that 
he let himself into his house. 

mrs. nixon had gone an hour and a half before his return, mary told him. 
darnell sigh with relief, and he and his wife stroll out into the 
garden and sat down side by side. 

they kept silence for a time, and at last mary spoke, not without a 
nervous tremor in her voice. 

'i must tell you, edward,' she began, 'that aunt has made a proposal 
which you ought to hear. i think we should consider it.' 

'a proposal? but how about the whole affair? is it still go on?' 

'oh, yes! she told me all about it. uncle is quite unrepentant. it seem 
he has taken a flat somewhere in town for that woman, and furnish it 
in the most costly manner. he simply laugh at aunt's reproaches, and 
say he means to have some fun at last. you saw how broken she was?' 

'yes; very sad. but won't he give her any money? wasn't she very badly 
dress for a woman in her position?' 

'aunt has no end of beautiful things, but i fancy she likes to hoard 
them; she has a horror of spoil her dresses. it isn't for want of 
money, i assure you, as uncle settled a very large sum on her two years 
ago, when he was everything that could be desire as a husband. and that 
bring me to what i want to say. aunt would like to live with us. she 
would pay very liberally. what do you say?' 

'would like to live with us?' exclaim darnell, and his pipe drop 
from his hand on to the grass. he was stupefy by the thought of aunt 
marian as a boarder, and sat staring vacant before him, wonder what 
new monster the night would next produce. 

'i knew you wouldn't much like the idea,' his wife went on. 'but i do 
think, dearest, that we ought not to refuse without very serious 
consideration. i am afraid you did not take to poor aunt very much.' 

darnell shook his head dumbly. 

'i thought you didn't; she was so upset, poor thing, and you didn't see 
her at her best. she is really so good. but listen to me, dear. do you 
think we have the right to refuse her offer? i told you she has money of 
her own, and i am sure she would be dreadfully offend if we said we 
wouldn't have her. and what would become of me if anything happen to 
you? you know we have very little saved.' 

darnell groaned. 

'it seem to me,' he said, 'that it would spoil everything. we are so 
happy, mary dear, by ourselves. of course i am extremely sorry for your 
aunt. i think she is very much to be pitied. but when it come to have 
her always here----' 

'i know, dear. don't think i am look forward to the prospect; you 
know i don't want anybody but you. still, we ought to think of the 
future, and besides we shall be able to live so very much better. i 
shall be able to give you all sort of nice things that i know you ought 
to have after all that hard work in the city. our income would be 
doubled.' 

'do you mean she would pay us £150 a year?' 

'certainly. and she would pay for the spare room being furnished, and 
any extra she might want. she told me, specially, that if a friend or 
two came now and again to see her, she would gladly bear the cost of a 
fire in the drawing-room, and give something towards the gas bill, with 
a few shilling for the girl for any additional trouble. we should 
certainly be more than twice as well off as we are now. you see, edward, 
dear, it's not the sort of offer we are likely to have again. besides, 
we must think of the future, as i said. do you know aunt took a great 
fancy to you?' 

he shudder and said nothing, and his wife went on with her argument. 

'and, you see, it isn't as if we should see so very much of her. she 
will have her breakfast in bed, and she told me she would often go up to 
her room in the evening directly after dinner. i thought that very nice 
and considerate. she quite understand that we shouldn't like to have a 
third person always with us. don't you think, edward, that, considering 
everything, we ought to say we will have her?' 

'oh, i suppose so,' he groaned. 'as you say, it's a very good offer, 
financially, and i am afraid it would be very imprudent to refuse. but i 
don't like the notion, i confess.' 

'i am so glad you agree with me, dear. depend upon it, it won't be half 
so bad as you think. and putt our own advantage on one side, we shall 
really be do poor aunt a very great kindness. poor old dear, she 
cry bitter after you were gone; she said she had made up her mind 
not to stay any long in uncle robert's house, and she didn't know 
where to go, or what would become of her, if we refuse to take her in. 
she quite broke down.' 

'well, well; we will try it for a year, anyhow. it may be as you say; we 
shan't find it quite so bad as it seem now. shall we go in?' 

he stoop for his pipe, which lay as it had fallen, on the grass. he 
could not find it, and lit a wax match which show him the pipe, and 
close beside it, under the seat, something that look like a page torn 
from a book. he wonder what it could be, and picked it up. 

the gas was lit in the drawing-room, and mrs. darnell, who was arrange 
some notepaper, wish to write at once to mrs. nixon, cordially 
accept her proposal, when she was startle by an exclamation from her 
husband. 

'what is the matter?' she said, startle by the tone of his voice. 'you 
haven't hurt yourself?' 

'look at this,' he replied, hand her a small leaflet; 'i found it 
under the garden seat just now.' 

mary glance with bewilder at her husband and read as follows:-- 

the new and chosen seed of abraham 

prophecy to be fulfill in the present year 

1. the sailing of a fleet of one hundred and forty and four vessel 
for tarshish and the isles. 

2. destruction of the power of the dog, including all the 
instrument of anti-abrahamic legislation. 

3. return of the fleet from tarshish, bearing with it the gold of 
arabia, destined to be the foundation of the new city of abraham. 

4. the search for the bride, and the bestow of the seal on the 
seventy and seven. 

5. the countenance of father to become luminous, but with a greater 
glory than the face of moses. 

6. the pope of rome to be stoned with stone in the valley call 
berek-zittor. 

7. father to be acknowledge by three great rulers. two great ruler 
will deny father, and will immediately perish in the effluvia of 
father's indignation. 

8. binding of the beast with the little horn, and all judge cast 
down. 

9. finding of the bride in the land of egypt, which has been 
reveal to father as now existing in the western part of london. 

10. bestow of the new tongue on the seventy and seven, and on the 
one hundred and forty and four. father proceeds to the bridal 
chamber. 

11. destruction of london and rebuild of the city call no, 
which is the new city of abraham. 

12. father united to the bride, and the present earth remove to the 
sun for the space of half an hour. 

mrs. darnell's brow clear as she read matter which seem to her 
harmless if incoherent. from her husband's voice she had been led to 
fear something more tangible unpleasant than a vague catena of 
prophecies. 

'well,' she said, 'what about it?' 

'what about it? don't you see that your aunt drop it, and that she 
must be a rage lunatic?' 

'oh, edward! don't say that. in the first place, how do you know that 
aunt drop it at all? it might easily have blown over from any of the 
other gardens. and, if it were hers, i don't think you should call her a 
lunatic. i don't believe, myself, that there are any real prophets now; 
but there are many good people who think quite differently. i knew an 
old lady once who, i am sure, was very good, and she took in a paper 
every week that was full of prophecy and things very like this. nobody 
call her mad, and i have heard father say that she had one of the 
sharpest head for business he had ever come across.' 

'very good; have it as you like. but i believe we shall both be sorry.' 

they sat in silence for some time. alice came in after her 'evening 
out,' and they sat on, till mrs. darnell said she was tired and want 
to go to bed. 

her husband kiss her. 'i don't think i will come up just yet,' he 
said; 'you go to sleep, dearest. i want to think things over. no, no; i 
am not go to change my mind: your aunt shall come, as i said. but 
there are one or two things i should like to get settled in my mind.' 

he meditate for a long while, pace up and down the room. light after 
light was extinguish in edna road, and the people of the suburb slept 
all around him, but still the gas was alight in darnell's drawing-room, 
and he walk soft up and down the floor. he was thinking that about 
the life of mary and himself, which had been so quiet, there seem to 
be gathering on all side grotesque and fantastic shapes, omen of 
confusion and disorder, threat of madness; a strange company from 
another world. it was as if into the quiet, sleep street of some 
little ancient town among the hill there had come from afar the sound 
of drum and pipe, snatch of wild song, and there had burst into the 
market-place the mad company of the players, strange bedizened, 
dance a furious measure to their hurry music, drawing forth the 
citizen from their sheltered home and peaceful lives, and allure 
them to mingle in the significant figures of their dance. 

yet afar and near (for it was hidden in his heart) he beheld the glimmer 
of a sure and constant star. beneath, dark came on, and mists and 
shadow closed about the town. the red, flicker flame of torch was 
kindle in the midst of it. the song grew louder, with more insistent, 
magical tones, surge and fall in unearthly modulations, the very 
speech of incantation; and the drum beat madly, and the pipe shrill to 
a scream, summon all to issue forth, to leave their peaceful hearths; 
for a strange rite was preconize in their midst. the street that were 
wont to be so still, so hush with the cool and tranquil veil of 
darkness, asleep beneath the patronage of the evening star, now dance 
with glimmer lanterns, resound with the cries of those who hurried 
forth, drawn as by a magistral spell; and the song swell and 
triumphed, the reverberant beating of the drum grew louder, and in the 
midst of the awaken town the players, fantastic arrayed, perform 
their interlude under the red blaze of torches. he knew not whether they 
were players, men that would vanish sudden as they came, disappear 
by the track that climb the hill; or whether they were indeed 
magicians, worker of great and efficacious spells, who knew the secret 
word by which the earth may be transform into the hall of gehenna, so 
that they that gaze and listened, as at a passing spectacle, should be 
entrap by the sound and the sight present to them, should be drawn 
into the elaborate figures of that mystic dance, and so should be 
whirl away into those unend maze on the wild hill that were 
abhorred, there to wander for evermore. 

but darnell was not afraid, because of the daystar that had risen in his 
heart. it had dwelt there all his life, and had slow shone forth with 
clear and clear light, and he began to see that though his earthly 
steps might be in the ways of the ancient town that was beset by the 
enchanters, and resound with their song and their processions, yet he 
dwelt also in that serene and secure world of brightness, and from a 
great and unutterable height look on the confusion of the mortal 
pageant, behold mystery in which he was no true actor, hearing 
magic song that could by no means draw him down from the battlements of 
the high and holy city. 

his heart was fill with a great joy and a great peace as he lay down 
beside his wife and fell asleep, and in the morning, when he woke up, he 
was glad. 


iv 

in a haze as of a dream darnell's thought seem to move through the 
opening days of the next week. perhaps nature had not intended that he 
should be practical or much given to that which is usually call 'sound 
common sense,' but his training had made him desirous of good, plain 
quality of the mind, and he uneasy strove to account to himself for 
his strange mood of the sunday night, as he had often endeavour to 
interpret the fancies of his boyhood and early manhood. at first he was 
annoy by his want of success; the morning paper, which he always 
secure as the 'bus delay at uxbridge road station, fell from his 
hand unread, while he vain reasoned, assure himself that the 
threaten incursion of a whimsical old woman, though tiresome enough, 
was no rational excuse for those curious hour of meditation in which 
his thought seem to have dress themselves in unfamiliar, fantastic 
habits, and to parley with him in a strange speech, and yet a speech 
that he had understood. 

with such argument he perplexed his mind on the long, accustomed ride 
up the steep ascent of dutch park, past the incongruous hustle of 
not hill gate, where in one direction a road show the way to the 
snug, somewhat fade bower and retreat of bayswater, and in another 
one see the portal of the murky region of the slums. the customary 
companion of his morning's journey were in the seat about him; he 
heard the hum of their talk, as they dispute concerning politics, and 
the man next to him, who came from acton, ask him what he thought of 
the government now. there was a discussion, and a loud and excited one, 
just in front, as to whether rhubarb was a fruit or vegetable, and in 
his ear he heard redman, who was a near neighbour, praise the economy 
of 'the wife.' 

'i don't know how she do it. look here; what do you think we had 
yesterday? breakfast: fish-cakes, beautiful fried--rich, you know, 
lot of herbs, it's a receipt of her aunt's; you should just taste 'em. 
coffee, bread, butter, marmalade, and, of course, all the usual 
etceteras. dinner: roast beef, yorkshire, potatoes, greens, and 
horse-radish sauce, plum tart, cheese. and where will you get a better 
dinner than that? well, i call it wonderful, i really do.' 

but in spite of these distraction he fell into a dream as the 'bus 
roll and toss on its way citywards, and still he strove to solve the 
enigma of his vigil of the night before, and as the shape of tree and 
green lawn and house pass before his eyes, and as he saw the 
procession moving on the pavement, and while the murmur of the street 
sound in his ears, all was to him strange and unaccustomed, as if he 
move through the avenue of some city in a foreign land. it was, 
perhaps, on these mornings, as he rode to his mechanical work, that 
vague and floating fancies that must have long haunt his brain began 
to shape themselves, and to put on the form of definite conclusions, 
from which he could no long escape, even if he had wish it. darnell 
had received what is call a sound commercial education, and would 
therefore have found very great difficulty in putt into articulate 
speech any thought that was worth thinking; but he grew certain on these 
mornings that the 'common sense' which he had always heard exalted as 
man's supremest faculty was, in all probability, the smallest and 
least-considered item in the equipment of an ant of average 
intelligence. and with this, as an almost necessary corollary, came a 
firm belief that the whole fabric of life in which he move was sunken, 
past all thinking, in the grossest absurdity; that he and all his 
friends and acquaintance and fellow-workers were interested in matter 
in which men were never meant to be interested, were pursue aim which 
they were never meant to pursue, were, indeed, much like fair stone of 
an altar serving as a pigsty wall. life, it seem to him, was a great 
search for--he knew not what; and in the process of the age one by one 
the true mark upon the ways had been shattered, or buried, or the 
meaning of the words had been slow forgotten; one by one the sign had 
been turn awry, the true entrance had been thick overgrown, the 
very way itself had been divert from the heights to the depths, till 
at last the race of pilgrim had become hereditary stone-breakers and 
ditch-scourers on a track that led to destruction--if it led anywhere at 
all. darnell's heart thrill with a strange and tremble joy, with a 
sense that was all new, when it came to his mind that this great loss 
might not be a hopeless one, that perhaps the difficulty were by no 
means insuperable. it might be, he considered, that the stone-breaker 
had merely to throw down his hammer and set out, and the way would be 
plain before him; and a single step would free the delve in rubbish 
from the foul slime of the ditch. 

it was, of course, with difficulty and slow that these things became 
clear to him. he was an english city clerk, 'flourishing' towards the 
end of the nineteenth century, and the rubbish heap that had been 
accumulate for some century could not be clear away in an instant. 
again and again the spirit of nonsense that had been implant in him as 
in his fellow assured him that the true world was the visible and 
tangible world, the world in which good and faithful letter-copying was 
exchange for a certain quantum of bread, beef, and house-room, and 
that the man who copy letters well, did not beat his wife, nor lose 
money foolishly, was a good man, fulfill the end for which he had 
been made. but in spite of these arguments, in spite of their acceptance 
by all who were about him, he had the grace to perceive the utter 
falsity and absurd of the whole position. he was fortunate in his 
entire ignorance of sixpenny 'science,' but if the whole library had 
been project into his brain it would not have move him to 'deny in 
the dark that which he had known in the light.' darnell knew by 
experience that man is made a mystery for mystery and visions, for the 
realization in his consciousness of ineffable bliss, for a great joy 
that transmute the whole world, for a joy that surpass all joy and 
overcome all sorrows. he knew this certainly, though he knew it dimly; 
and he was apart from other men, prepare himself for a great 
experiment. 

with such thought as these for his secret and conceal treasure, he 
was able to bear the threaten invasion of mrs. nixon with something 
approach indifference. he knew, indeed, that her presence between 
his wife and himself would be unwelcome to him, and he was not without 
grave doubt as to the woman's sanity; but after all, what did it 
matter? besides, already a faint glimmer light had risen within him 
that show the profit of self-negation, and in this matter he had 
prefer his wife's will to his own. _et non sua poma_; to his 
astonishment he found a delight in deny himself his own wish, a 
process that he had always regard as thorough detestable. this was a 
state of things which he could not in the least understand; but, again, 
though a member of a most hopeless class, living in the most hopeless 
surroundings that the world has ever seen, though he knew as much of the 
_askesis_ as of china metaphysics; again, he had the grace not to deny 
the light that had begun to glimmer in his soul. 

and he found a present reward in the eyes of mary, when she welcome him 
home after his foolish labour in the cool of the evening. they sat 
together, hand in hand, under the mulberry tree, at the coming of the 
dusk, and as the ugly wall about them became obscure and vanish into 
the formless world of shadows, they seem to be free from the bondage 
of shepherd's bush, free to wander in that undisfigured, undefiled 
world that lie beyond the walls. of this region mary knew little or 
nothing by experience, since her relations had always been of one mind 
with the modern world, which has for the true country an instinctive and 
most significant horror and dread. mr. reynolds had also share in 
another odd superstition of these later days--that it is necessary to 
leave london at least once a year; consequently mary had some knowledge 
of various seaside resort on the south and east coasts, where 
londoner gather in hordes, turn the sands into one vast, bad 
music-hall, and derive, as they say, enormous benefit from the change. 
but experience such as these give but little knowledge of the country 
in its true and occult sense; and yet mary, as she sat in the dusk 
beneath the whisper tree, knew something of the secret of the wood, 
of the valley shut in by high hills, where the sound of pour water 
always echo from the clear brook. and to darnell these were nights of 
great dreams; for it was the hour of the work, the time of 
transmutation, and he who could not understand the miracle, who could 
scarcely believe in it, yet knew, secret and half consciously, that 
the water was being change into the wine of a new life. this was ever 
the inner music of his dreams, and to it he add on these still and 
sacred nights the far-off memory of that time long ago when, a child, 
before the world had overwhelm him, he journey down to the old grey 
house in the west, and for a whole month heard the murmur of the forest 
through his bedroom window, and when the wind was hushed, the washing of 
the tide about the reeds; and sometimes awake very early he had heard 
the strange cry of a bird as it rose from its nest among the reeds, and 
had look out and had seen the valley whiten to the dawn, and the 
winding river whiten as it swam down to the sea. the memory of all this 
had fade and become shadowy as he grew old and the chain of common 
life were rivet firm about his soul; all the atmosphere by which he 
was surround was well-nigh fatal to such thoughts, and only now and 
again in half-conscious moments or in sleep he had revisite that valley 
in the far-off west, where the breath of the wind was an incantation, 
and every leaf and stream and hill spoke of great and ineffable 
mysteries. but now the broken vision was in great part restore to him, 
and look with love in his wife's eyes he saw the gleam of water-pools 
in the still forest, saw the mists rising in the evening, and heard the 
music of the winding river. 

they were sitting thus together on the friday evening of the week that 
had begun with that odd and half-forgotten visit of mrs. nixon, when, to 
darnell's annoyance, the door-bell gave a discordant peal, and alice 
with some disturbance of manner came out and announce that a gentleman 
wish to see the master. darnell went into the drawing-room, where 
alice had lit one gas so that it flared and burnt with a rush sound, 
and in this distort light there wait a stout, elderly gentleman, 
whose countenance was altogether unknown to him. he stare blankly, and 
hesitated, about to speak, but the visitor began. 

'you don't know who i am, but i expect you'll know my name. it's nixon.' 

he did not wait to be interrupted. he sat down and plunge into 
narrative, and after the first few words, darnell, whose mind was not 
altogether unprepared, listen without much astonishment. 

'and the long and the short of it is,' mr. nixon said at last, 'she's 
gone stark, staring mad, and we had to put her away to-day--poor thing.' 

his voice broke a little, and he wipe his eyes hastily, for though 
stout and successful he was not unfeeling, and he was fond of his wife. 
he had spoken quickly, and had gone lightly over many detail which 
might have interested specialist in certain kind of mania, and 
darnell was sorry for his evident distress. 

'i came here,' he went on after a brief pause, 'because i found out she 
had been to see you last sunday, and i knew the sort of story she must 
have told.' 

darnell show him the prophetic leaflet which mrs. nixon had drop in 
the garden. 'did you know about this?' he said. 

'oh, _him_,' said the old man, with some approach to cheerfulness; 'oh 
yes, i thrash _him_ black and blue the day before yesterday.' 

'isn't he mad? who is the man?' 

'he's not mad, he's bad. he's a little welsh skunk name richards. he's 
been running some sort of chapel over at new barnet for the last few 
years, and my poor wife--she never could find the parish church good 
enough for her--had been go to his damn schism shop for the last 
twelve-month. it was all that finished her off. yes; i thrash _him_ 
the day before yesterday, and i'm not afraid of a summons either. i know 
him, and he know i know him.' 

old nixon whisper something in darnell's ear, and chuckle faint as 
he repeated for the third time his formula-- 

'i thrash _him_ black and blue the day before yesterday.' 

darnell could only murmur condolence and express his hope that mrs. 
nixon might recover. 

the old man shook his head. 

'i'm afraid there's no hope of that,' he said. 'i've had the best 
advice, but they couldn't do anything, and told me so.' 

presently he ask to see his niece, and darnell went out and prepared 
mary as well as he could. she could scarcely take in the news that her 
aunt was a hopeless maniac, for mrs. nixon, have been extremely stupid 
all her days, had naturally succeed in passing with her relations as 
typically sensible. with the reynolds family, as with the great majority 
of us, want of imagination is always equate with sanity, and though 
many of us have never heard of lombroso we are his ready-made converts. 
we have always believe that poet are mad, and if statistics 
unfortunately show that few poet have really been inhabitant of 
lunatic asylums, it is soothe to learn that nearly all poet have had 
whooping-cough, which is doubtless, like intoxication, a minor madness. 

'but is it really true?' she ask at length. 'are you certain uncle is 
not deceive you? aunt seem so sensible always.' 

she was help at last by recollect that aunt marian use to get up 
very early of mornings, and then they went into the drawing-room and 
talk to the old man. his evident kindly and honesty grew upon 
mary, in spite of a lingering belief in her aunt's fables, and when he 
left, it was with a promise to come to see them again. 

mrs. darnell said she felt tired, and went to bed; and darnell return 
to the garden and began to pace to and fro, collect his thoughts. his 
immeasurable relief at the intelligence that, after all, mrs. nixon was 
not coming to live with them taught him that, despite his submission, 
his dread of the event had been very great. the weight was removed, and 
now he was free to consider his life without reference to the grotesque 
intrusion that he had feared. he sigh for joy, and as he pace to and 
fro he savour the scent of the night, which, though it came faint 
to him in that brick-bound suburb, summon to his mind across many 
years the odour of the world at night as he had known it in that short 
sojourn of his boyhood; the odour that rose from the earth when the 
flame of the sun had gone down beyond the mountain, and the afterglow 
had pale in the sky and on the fields. and as he recover as best he 
could these lost dream of an enchant land, there came to him other 
image of his childhood, forgotten and yet not forgotten, dwelling 
unheeded in dark place of the memory, but ready to be summon forth. 
he remember one fantasy that had long haunt him. as he lay half 
asleep in the forest on one hot afternoon of that memorable visit to the 
country, he had 'made believe' that a little companion had come to him 
out of the blue mists and the green light beneath the leaves--a white 
girl with long black hair, who had play with him and whisper her 
secret in his ear, as his father lay sleep under a tree; and from 
that summer afternoon, day by day, she had been beside him; she had 
visit him in the wilderness of london, and even in recent years there 
had come to him now and again the sense of her presence, in the midst of 
the heat and turmoil of the city. the last visit he remember well; it 
was a few week before he married, and from the depths of some futile 
task he had look up with puzzle eyes, wonder why the close air 
sudden grew scent with green leaves, why the murmur of the tree and 
the wash of the river on the reeds came to his ears; and then that 
sudden rapture to which he had given a name and an individuality 
possessed him utterly. he knew then how the dull flesh of man can be 
like fire; and now, look back from a new standpoint on this and 
other experiences, he realize how all that was real in his life had
