been unwelcomed, uncherished by him, had come to him, perhaps, in virtue 
of merely negative quality on his part. and yet, as he reflected, he 
saw that there had been a chain of witness all through his life: again 
and again voice had whisper in his ear words in a strange language 
that he now recognize as his native tongue; the common street had not 
been lacking in vision of the true land of his birth; and in all the 
passing and repass of the world he saw that there had been emissary 
ready to guide his feet on the way of the great journey. 

a week or two after the visit of mr. nixon, darnell took his annual 
holiday. 

there was no question of walton-on-the-naze, or of anything of the kind, 
as he quite agree with his wife's longing for some substantial sum put 
by against the evil day. but the weather was still fine, and he lounge 
away the time in his garden beneath the tree, or he saunter out on 
long aimless walk in the western purlieus of london, not unvisited by 
that old sense of some great ineffable beauty, conceal by the dim and 
dingy veil of grey interminable streets. once, on a day of heavy rain 
he went to the 'box-room,' and began to turn over the papers in the old 
hair trunk--scraps and odds and end of family history, some of them in 
his father's handwriting, other in fade ink, and there were a few 
ancient pocket-books, fill with manuscript of a still early time, 
and in these the ink was glossy and black than any writing fluid 
supply by stationer of later days. darnell had hung up the portrait 
of the ancestor in this room, and had bought a solid kitchen table and 
a chair; so that mrs. darnell, seeing him look over his old 
documents, half thought of name the room 'mr. darnell's study.' he had 
not glance at these relics of his family for many years, but from the 
hour when the rainy morning sent him to them, he remain constant to 
research till the end of the holidays. it was a new interest, and he 
began to fashion in his mind a faint picture of his forefathers, and of 
their life in that grey old house in the river valley, in the western 
land of well and stream and dark and ancient woods. and there were 
stranger things than mere note on family history amongst that odd 
litter of old disregard papers, and when he went back to his work in 
the city some of the men fancy that he was in some vague manner 
change in appearance; but he only laugh when they ask him where he 
had been and what he had been do with himself. but mary notice that 
every evening he spent at least an hour in the box-room; she was rather 
sorry at the waste of time involved in reading old papers about dead 
people. and one afternoon, as they were out together on a somewhat 
dreary walk towards acton, darnell stop at a hopeless second-hand 
bookshop, and after scan the row of shabby books in the window, 
went in and purchase two volumes. they prove to be a latin dictionary 
and grammar, and she was surprise to hear her husband declare his 
intention of acquire the latin language. 

but, indeed, all his conduct impress her as indefinable altered; and 
she began to be a little alarmed, though she could scarcely have form 
her fear in words. but she knew that in some way that was all 
indefine and beyond the grasp of her thought their lives had alter 
since the summer, and no single thing wore quite the same aspect as 
before. if she look out into the dull street with its rare loiterers, 
it was the same and yet it had altered, and if she open the window in 
the early morning the wind that enter came with a change breath that 
spoke some message that she could not understand. and day by day pass 
by in the old course, and not even the four wall were altogether 
familiar, and the voice of men and women sound with strange notes, 
with the echo, rather, of a music that came over unknown hills. and day 
by day as she went about her household work, passing from shop to shop 
in those dull street that were a network, a fatal labyrinth of grey 
desolate on every side, there came to her sense half-seen image of 
some other world, as if she walk in a dream, and every moment must 
bring her to light and to awakening, when the grey should fade, and 
regions long desire should appear in glory. again and again it seem 
as if that which was hidden would be shown even to the sluggish 
testimony of sense; and as she went to and fro from street to street of 
that dim and weary suburb, and look on those grey material walls, they 
seem as if a light glow behind them, and again and again the mystic 
fragrance of incense was blown to her nostril from across the verge of 
that world which is not so much impenetrable as ineffable, and to her 
ear came the dream of a chant that spoke of hidden choir about all her 
ways. she struggle against these impressions, refuse her assent to 
the testimony of them, since all the pressure of credit opinion for 
three hundred years has been direct towards stamp out real 
knowledge, and so effectually has this been accomplished that we can 
only recover the truth through much anguish. and so mary pass the days 
in a strange perturbation, clinging to common things and common 
thoughts, as if she fear that one morning she would wake up in an 
unknown world to a change life. and edward darnell went day by day to 
his labour and return in the evening, always with that shining of 
light within his eyes and upon his face, with the gaze of wonder that 
was greater day by day, as if for him the veil grew thin and soon would 
disappear. 

from these great matter both in herself and in her husband mary shrank 
back, afraid, perhaps, that if she began the question the answer might 
be too wonderful. she rather taught herself to be trouble over little 
things; she ask herself what attraction there could be in the old 
record over which she supposed edward to be pore night after night in 
the cold room upstairs. she had glance over the papers at darnell's 
invitation, and could see but little interest in them; there were one or 
two sketches, roughly done in pen and ink, of the old house in the west: 
it look a shapeless and fantastic place, furnish with strange 
pillar and stranger ornament on the project porch; and on one side 
a roof dip down almost to the earth, and in the centre there was 
something that might almost be a tower rising above the rest of the 
building. then there were document that seem all name and dates, 
with here and there a coat of arms done in the margin, and she came upon 
a string of uncouth welsh name link together by the word 'ap' in a 
chain that look endless. there was a paper cover with sign and 
figures that meant nothing to her, and then there were the pocket-books, 
full of old-fashioned writing, and much of it in latin, as her husband 
told her--it was a collection as void of significance as a treatise on 
conic sections, so far as mary was concerned. but night after night 
darnell shut himself up with the musty rolls, and more than ever when he 
rejoin her he bore upon his face the blazonry of some great adventure. 
and one night she ask him what interested him so much in the papers he 
had shown her. 

he was delight with the question. somehow they had not talk much 
together for the last few weeks, and he began to tell her of the record 
of the old race from which he came, of the old strange house of grey 
stone between the forest and the river. the family went back and back, 
he said, far into the dim past, beyond the normans, beyond the saxons, 
far into the roman days, and for many hundred years they had been petty 
kings, with a strong fortress high up on the hill, in the heart of the 
forest; and even now the great mound remained, whence one could look 
through the tree towards the mountain on one side and across the yellow 
sea on the other. the real name of the family was not darnell; that was 
assume by one iolo ap taliesin ap iorwerth in the sixteenth 
century--why, darnell did not seem to understand. and then he told her 
how the race had dwindle in prosperity, century by century, till at 
last there was nothing left but the grey house and a few acre of land 
border the river. 

'and do you know, mary,' he said, 'i suppose we shall go and live there 
some day or other. my great-uncle, who has the place now, made money in 
business when he was a young man, and i believe he will leave it all to 
me. i know i am the only relation he has. how strange it would be. what 
a change from the life here.' 

'you never told me that. don't you think your great-uncle might leave 
his house and his money to somebody he know really well? you haven't 
seen him since you were a little boy, have you?' 

'no; but we write once a year. and from what i have heard my father say, 
i am sure the old man would never leave the house out of the family. do 
you think you would like it?' 

'i don't know. isn't it very lonely?' 

'i suppose it is. i forget whether there are any other house in sight, 
but i don't think there are any at all near. but what a change! no city, 
no streets, no people passing to and fro; only the sound of the wind and 
the sight of the green leaves and the green hills, and the song of the 
voice of the earth.'... he checked himself suddenly, as if he fear 
that he was about to tell some secret that must not yet be uttered; and 
indeed, as he spoke of the change from the little street in shepherd's 
bush to that ancient house in the wood of the far west, a change seem 
already to possess himself, and his voice put on the modulation of an 
antique chant. mary look at him steady and touched his arm, and he 
drew a long breath before he spoke again. 

'it is the old blood calling to the old land,' he said. 'i was 
forget that i am a clerk in the city.' 

it was, doubtless, the old blood that had sudden stir in him; the 
resurrection of the old spirit that for many century had been 
faithful to secret that are now disregard by most of us, that now day 
by day was quicken more and more in his heart, and grew so strong that 
it was hard to conceal. he was indeed almost in the position of the man 
in the tale, who, by a sudden electric shock, lost the vision of the 
things about him in the london streets, and gaze instead upon the sea 
and shore of an island in the antipodes; for darnell only clung with an 
effort to the interests and the atmosphere which, till lately, had 
seem all the world to him; and the grey house and the wood and the 
river, symbol of the other sphere, intrude as it were into the 
landscape of the london suburb. 

but he went on, with more restraint, telling his story of far-off 
ancestors, how one of them, the most remote of all, was call a saint, 
and was supposed to possess certain mysterious secret often allude to 
in the papers as the 'hidden song of iolo sant.' and then with an 
abrupt transition he recall memory of his father and of the strange, 
shiftless life in dingy lodgings in the backwater of london, of the dim 
stucco street that were his first recollections, of forgotten square 
in north london, and of the figure of his father, a grave bearded man 
who seem always in a dream, as if he too sought for the vision of a 
land beyond the strong walls, a land where there were deep orchard and 
many shining hills, and fountain and water-pools gleam under the 
leaves of the wood. 

'i believe my father earn his living,' he went on, 'such a living as 
he did earn, at the record office and the britain museum. he use to 
hunt up things for lawyer and country parson who want old deed 
inspected. he never made much, and we were always moving from one 
lodging to another--always to out-of-the-way place where everything 
seem to have run to seed. we never knew our neighbours--we move too 
often for that--but my father had about half a dozen friends, elderly 
men like himself, who use to come to see us pretty often; and then, if 
there was any money, the lodging-house servant would go out for beer, 
and they would sit and smoke far into the night. 

'i never knew much about these friends of his, but they all had the same 
look, the look of longing for something hidden. they talk of mystery 
that i never understood, very little of their own lives, and when they 
did speak of ordinary affair one could tell that they thought such 
matter as money and the want of it were unimportant trifles. when i 
grew up and went into the city, and met other young fellow and heard 
their way of talking, i wonder whether my father and his friends were 
not a little queer in their heads; but i know better now.' 

so night after night darnell talk to his wife, seeming to wander 
aimless from the dingy lodging-houses, where he had spent his boyhood 
in the company of his father and the other seekers, to the old house 
hidden in that far western valley, and the old race that had so long 
look at the setting of the sun over the mountain. but in truth there 
was one end in all that he spoke, and mary felt that beneath his words, 
however indifferent they might seem, there was hidden a purpose, that 
they were to embark on a great and marvellous adventure. 

so day by day the world became more magical; day by day the work of 
separation was being performed, the gross accident were being refined 
away. darnell neglect no instrument that might be useful in the work; 
and now he neither lounge at home on sunday mornings, nor did he 
accompany his wife to the gothic blasphemy which pretended to be a 
church. they had discover a little church of another fashion in a back 
street, and darnell, who had found in one of the old notebook the maxim 
_incredibilia sola credenda_, soon perceive how high and glorious a 
thing was that service at which he assisted. our stupid ancestor taught 
us that we could become wise by study books on 'science,' by meddle 
with test-tubes, geology specimens, microscopic preparations, and the 
like; but they who have cast off these folly know that they must read 
not 'science' books, but mass-books, and that the soul is made wise by 
the contemplation of mystic ceremony and elaborate and curious rites. 
in such things darnell found a wonderful mystery language, which spoke 
at once more secret and more directly than the formal creeds; and he 
saw that, in a sense, the whole world is but a great ceremony or 
sacrament, which teach under visible form a hidden and transcendent 
doctrine. it was thus that he found in the ritual of the church a 
perfect image of the world; an image purged, exalted, and illuminate, a 
holy house built up of shining and translucent stones, in which the 
burning torch were more significant than the wheeling stars, and the 
fume incense was a more certain token than the rising of the mist. his 
soul went forth with the albe procession in its white and solemn order, 
the mystic dance that signify rapture and a joy above all joys, and 
when he beheld love slain and rise again victorious he knew that he 
witnessed, in a figure, the consummation of all things, the bridal of 
all bridals, the mystery that is beyond all mysteries, accomplished from 
the foundation of the world. so day by day the house of his life became 
more magical. 

and at the same time he began to guess that if in the new life there are 
new and unheard-of joys, there are also new and unheard-of dangers. in 
his manuscript books which professed to deliver the outer sense of those 
mysterious 'hidden song of iolo sant' there was a little chapter that 
bore the heading: _fons sac non in communem vsum convertendu est_, 
and by diligence, with much use of the grammar and dictionary, darnell 
was able to construe the by no means complex latin of his ancestor. the 
special book which contained the chapter in question was one of the most 
singular in the collection, since it bore the title _terra de iolo_, and 
on the surface, with an ingenious concealment of its real symbolism, it 
affected to give an account of the orchards, fields, woods, roads, 
tenements, and waterway in the possession of darnell's ancestors. here, 
then, he read of the holy well, hidden in the wistman's wood--_sylva 
sapientum_--'a fountain of abundant water, which no heat of summer can 
ever dry, which no flood can ever defile, which is as a water of life, 
to them that thirst for life, a stream of cleanse to them that would 
be pure, and a medicine of such heal virtue that by it, through the 
might of god and the intercession of his saints, the most grievous 
wound are made whole.' but the water of this well was to be kept 
sacred perpetually, it was not to be use for any common purpose, nor to 
satisfy any bodily thirst; but ever to be esteem as holy, 'even as the 
water which the priest hath hallowed.' and in the margin a comment in a 
later hand taught darnell something of the meaning of these 
prohibitions. he was warn not to use the well of life as a mere luxury 
of mortal life, as a new sensation, as a means of making the insipid cup 
of everyday existence more palatable. 'for,' said the commentator, 'we 
are not call to sit as the spectator in a theatre, there to watch the 
play perform before us, but we are rather summon to stand in the 
very scene itself, and there fervent to enact our parts in a great and 
wonderful mystery.' 

darnell could quite understand the temptation that was thus indicated. 
though he had gone but a little way on the path, and had barely test 
the over-runnings of that mystic well, he was already aware of the 
enchantment that was transmute all the world about him, inform his 
life with a strange significance and romance. london seem a city of 
the arabia nights, and its labyrinth of street an enchant maze; its 
long avenue of light lamp were as starry systems, and its immensity 
became for him an image of the endless universe. he could well imagine 
how pleasant it might be to linger in such a world as this, to sit apart 
and dream, behold the strange pageant play before him; but the 
sacred well was not for common use, it was for the cleanse of the 
soul, and the heal of the grievous wound of the spirit. there must 
be yet another transformation: london had become bagdad; it must at 
last be transmute to syon, or in the phrase of one of his old 
documents, the city of the cup. 

and there were yet dark peril which the iolo mss. (as his father had 
name the collection) hint at more or less obscurely. there were 
suggestion of an awful region which the soul might enter, of a 
transmute that was unto death, of evocation which could summon the 
utmost forces of evil from their dark places--in a word, of that sphere 
which is represent to most of us under the crude and somewhat childish 
symbolism of black magic. and here again he was not altogether without a 
dim comprehension of what was meant. he found himself recall an odd 
incident that had happen long ago, which had remain all the years in 
his mind unheeded, amongst the many insignificant recollection of his 
childhood, and now rose before him, clear and distinct and full of 
meaning. it was on that memorable visit to the old house in the west, 
and the whole scene returned, with its smallest events, and the voice 
seem to sound in his ears. it was a grey, still day of heavy heat that 
he remembered: he had stood on the lawn after breakfast, and wonder at 
the great peace and silence of the world. not a leaf stir in the 
tree on the lawn, not a whisper came from the myriad leaves of the 
wood; the flower gave out sweet and heavy odour as if they breathe 
the dream of the summer night; and far down the valley, the winding 
river was like dim silver under that dim and silvery sky, and the far 
hill and wood and field vanish in the mist. the still of the 
air held him as with a charm; he leant all the morning against the rails 
that part the lawn from the meadow, breathing the mystic breath of 
summer, and watch the field brighten as with a sudden blossom of 
shining flower as the high mist grew thin for a moment before the 
hidden sun. as he watch thus, a man weary with heat, with some glance 
of horror in his eyes, pass him on his way to the house; but he stay 
at his post till the old bell in the turret rang, and they dine all 
together, master and servants, in the dark cool room that look 
towards the still leaves of the wood. he could see that his uncle was 
upset about something, and when they had finished dinner he heard him 
tell his father that there was trouble at a farm; and it was settled 
that they should all drive over in the afternoon to some place with a 
strange name. but when the time came mr. darnell was too deep in old 
books and tobacco smoke to be stir from his corner, and edward and 
his uncle went alone in the dog-cart. they drove swift down the narrow 
lane, into the road that follow the winding river, and cross the 
bridge at caermaen by the moulder roman walls, and then, skirt the 
deserted, echo village, they came out on a broad white turnpike road, 
and the limestone dust follow them like a cloud. then, suddenly, they 
turn to the north by such a road as edward had never seen before. it 
was so narrow that there was barely room for the cart to pass, and the 
footway was of rock, and the bank rose high above them as they slow 
climb the long, steep way, and the untrimm hedge on either side 
shut out the light. and the fern grew thick and green upon the banks, 
and hidden well drip down upon them; and the old man told him how 
the lane in winter was a torrent of swirl water, so that no one 
could pass by it. on they went, ascend and then again descending, 
always in that deep hollow under the wild woven boughs, and the boy 
wonder vain what the country was like on either side. and now the 
air grew darker, and the hedge on one bank was but the verge of a dark 
and rustling wood, and the grey limestone rocks had change to dark-red 
earth fleck with green patch and vein of marl, and sudden in the 
still from the depths of the wood a bird began to sing a melody that 
charm the heart into another world, that sang to the child's soul of 
the blessed faery realm beyond the wood of the earth, where the wound 
of man are healed. and so at last, after many turning and windings, 
they came to a high bare land where the lane broaden out into a kind 
of common, and along the edge of this place there were scattered three 
or four old cottages, and one of them was a little tavern. here they 
stopped, and a man came out and tether the tired horse to a post and 
gave him water; and old mr. darnell took the child's hand and led him by 
a path across the fields. the boy could see the country now, but it was 
all a strange, undiscovered land; they were in the heart of a wilderness 
of hill and valley that he had never look upon, and they were go 
down a wild, steep hillside, where the narrow path wound in and out 
amidst gorse and towering bracken, and the sun gleam out for a 
moment, there was a gleam of white water far below in a narrow valley, 
where a little brook pour and ripple from stone to stone. they went 
down the hill, and through a brake, and then, hidden in dark-green 
orchards, they came upon a long, low whitewash house, with a stone 
roof strange coloured by the growth of moss and lichens. mr. darnell 
knock at a heavy oaken door, and they came into a dim room where but 
little light enter through the thick glass in the deep-set window. 
there were heavy beam in the ceiling, and a great fireplace sent out an 
odour of burning wood that darnell never forgot, and the room seem to 
him full of women who talk all together in frightened tones. mr. 
darnell beckon to a tall, grey old man, who wore corduroy 
knee-breeches, and the boy, sitting on a high straight-backed chair, 
could see the old man and his uncle passing to and fro across the 
window-panes, as they walk together on the garden path. the women 
stop their talk for a moment, and one of them brought him a glass of 
milk and an apple from some cold inner chamber; and then, suddenly, from 
a room above there rang out a shrill and terrible shriek, and then, in a 
young girl's voice, a more terrible song. it was not like anything the 
child had ever heard, but as the man recall it to his memory, he knew 
to what song it might be compared--to a certain chant indeed that 
summons the angel and archangel to assist in the great sacrifice. but 
as this song chant of the heavenly army, so did that seem to summon all 
the hierarchy of evil, the host of lilith and samael; and the words 
that rang out with such awful modulations--_neumata inferorum_--were in 
some unknown tongue that few men have ever heard on earth. 

the women glare at one another with horror in their eyes, and he saw 
one or two of the oldest of them clumsy making an old sign upon their 
breasts. then they began to speak again, and he remember fragment of 
their talk. 

'she has been up there,' said one, point vague over her shoulder. 

'she'd never know the way,' answer another. 'they be all gone that 
went there.' 

'there be nought there in these days.' 

'how can you tell that, gwenllian? 'tis not for us to say that.' 

'my great-grandmother did know some that had been there,' said a very 
old woman. 'she told me how they was taken afterwards.' 

and then his uncle appear at the door, and they went their way as they 
had come. edward darnell never heard any more of it, nor whether the 
girl die or recover from her strange attack; but the scene had 
haunt his mind in boyhood, and now the recollection of it came to him 
with a certain note of warning, as a symbol of danger that might be in 
the way. 

* * * * * 

it would be impossible to carry on the history of edward darnell and of 
mary his wife to a greater length, since from this point their legend is 
full of impossible events, and seem to put on the semblance of the 
story of the graal. it is certain, indeed, that in this world they 
change their lives, like king arthur, but this is a work which no 
chronicle has care to describe with any amplitude of detail. darnell, 
it is true, made a little book, partly consist of queer verse which 
might have been written by an inspired infant, and partly made up of 
'notes and exclamations' in an odd dog-latin which he had picked up 
from the 'iolo mss.', but it is to be fear that this work, even if 
publish in its entirety, would cast but little light on a perplex 
story. he call this piece of literature 'in exitu israel,' and wrote 
on the title page the motto, doubtless of his own composition, '_nunc 
certe scio quod omnia legenda; omne historiæ, omne fabulæ, omni 
scriptura sint de me narrata_.' it is only too evident that his latin 
was not learnt at the feet of cicero; but in this dialect he relate the 
great history of the 'new life' as it was manifest to him. the 'poems' 
are even stranger. one, head (with an odd reminiscence of 
old-fashioned books) 'lines written on look down from a height in 
london on a board school sudden lit up by the sun' begin thus:-- 

one day when i was all alone 
i found a wondrous little stone, 
it lay forgotten on the road 
far from the ways of man's abode. 
when on this stone mine eyes i cast 
i saw my treasure found at last. 
i pressed it hard against my face, 
i cover it with my embrace, 
i hid it in a secret place. 
and every day i went to see 
this stone that was my ecstasy; 
and worship it with flower rare, 
and secret words and saying fair. 
o stone, so rare and red and wise 
o fragment of far paradise, 
o star, whose light is life! o sea, 
whose ocean is infinity! 
thou art a fire that ever burns, 
and all the world to wonder turns; 
and all the dust of the dull day 
by thee is change and purge away, 
so that, where'er i look, i see 
a world of a great majesty. 
the sullen river rolls all gold, 
the desert park's a faery wold, 
when on the tree the wind is borne 
i hear the sound of arthur's horn 
i see no town of grim grey ways, 
but a great city all ablaze 
with burning torches, to light up 
the pinnacle that shrine the cup. 
ever the magic wine is poured, 
ever the feast shine on the board, 
ever the song is borne on high 
that chant the holy magistry-- 
etc. etc. etc. 

from such document as these it is clearly impossible to gather any very 
definite information. but on the last page darnell has written-- 

'so i awoke from a dream of a london suburb, of daily labour, of weary, 
useless little things; and as my eyes were open i saw that i was in an 
ancient wood, where a clear well rose into grey film and vapour beneath 
a misty, glimmer heat. and a form came towards me from the hidden 
place of the wood, and my love and i were united by the well.' 




the white people 


prologue 

'sorcery and sanctity,' said ambrose, 'these are the only realities. 
each is an ecstasy, a withdrawal from the common life.' 

cotgrave listened, interested. he had been brought by a friend to this 
moulder house in a northern suburb, through an old garden to the room 
where ambrose the recluse doze and dream over his books. 

'yes,' he went on, 'magic is justified of her children. there are many, 
i think, who eat dry crust and drink water, with a joy infinite 
sharper than anything within the experience of the "practical" epicure.' 

'you are speak of the saints?' 

'yes, and of the sinners, too. i think you are fall into the very 
general error of confine the spiritual world to the supreme good; 
but the supreme wicked, necessarily, have their portion in it. the 
merely carnal, sensual man can no more be a great sinner than he can be 
a great saint. most of us are just indifferent, mixed-up creatures; we 
muddle through the world without realize the meaning and the inner 
sense of things, and, consequently, our wicked and our goodness are 
alike second-rate, unimportant.' 

'and you think the great sinner, then, will be an ascetic, as well as 
the great saint?' 

'great people of all kind forsake the imperfect copy and go to the 
perfect originals. i have no doubt but that many of the very highest 
among the saint have never done a "good action" (using the words in 
their ordinary sense). and, on the other hand, there have been those who 
have sound the very depths of sin, who all their lives have never done 
an "ill deed."' 

he went out of the room for a moment, and cotgrave, in high delight, 
turn to his friend and thank him for the introduction. 

'he's grand,' he said. 'i never saw that kind of lunatic before.' 

ambrose return with more whisky and help the two men in a liberal 
manner. he abuse the teetotal sect with ferocity, as he hand the 
seltzer, and pour out a glass of water for himself, was about to 
resume his monologue, when cotgrave broke in-- 

'i can't stand it, you know,' he said, 'your paradox are too 
monstrous. a man may be a great sinner and yet never do anything sinful! 
come!' 

'you're quite wrong,' said ambrose. 'i never make paradoxes; i wish i 
could. i merely said that a man may have an exquisite taste in romanée 
conti, and yet never have even smelt four ale. that's all, and it's more 
like a truism than a paradox, isn't it? your surprise at my remark is 
due to the fact that you haven't realize what sin is. oh, yes, there is 
a sort of connexion between sin with the capital letter, and action 
which are commonly call sinful: with murder, theft, adultery, and so 
forth. much the same connexion that there is between the a, b, c and 
fine literature. but i believe that the misconception--it is all but 
universal--arises in great measure from our look at the matter 
through social spectacles. we think that a man who do evil to _us_ and 
to his neighbour must be very evil. so he is, from a social standpoint; 
but can't you realize that evil in its essence is a lonely thing, a 
passion of the solitary, individual soul? really, the average murderer, 
_quâ_ murderer, is not by any means a sinner in the true sense of the 
word. he is simply a wild beast that we have to get rid of to save our 
own neck from his knife. i should class him rather with tiger than 
with sinners.' 

'it seem a little strange.' 

'i think not. the murder murder not from positive qualities, but from 
negative ones; he lack something which non-murderers possess. evil, of 
course, is wholly positive--only it is on the wrong side. you may 
believe me that sin in its proper sense is very rare; it is probable 
that there have been far few sinner than saints. yes, your standpoint 
is all very well for practical, social purposes; we are naturally 
inclined to think that a person who is very disagreeable to us must be a 
very great sinner! it is very disagreeable to have one's pocket picked, 
and we pronounce the thief to be a very great sinner. in truth, he is 
merely an undeveloped man. he cannot be a saint, of course; but he may 
be, and often is, an infinite better creature than thousand who have 
never broken a single commandment. he is a great nuisance to _us_, i 
admit, and we very properly lock him up if we catch him; but between his 
troublesome and unsocial action and evil--oh, the connexion is of the 
weakest.' 

it was get very late. the man who had brought cotgrave had probably 
heard all this before, since he assist with a bland and judicious 
smile, but cotgrave began to think that his 'lunatic' was turning into a 
sage. 

'do you know,' he said, 'you interest me immensely? you think, then, 
that we do not understand the real nature of evil?' 

'no, i don't think we do. we over-estimate it and we under-estimate it. 
we take the very numerous infraction of our social "bye-laws"--the very 
necessary and very proper regulation which keep the human company 
together--and we get frightened at the prevalence of "sin" and "evil." 
but this is really nonsense. take theft, for example. have you any 
_horror_ at the thought of robin hood, of the highland cateran of the 
seventeenth century, of the moss-troopers, of the company promoter of 
our day? 

'then, on the other hand, we underrate evil. we attach such an enormous 
importance to the "sin" of meddle with our pocket (and our wives) 
that we have quite forgotten the awful of real sin.' 

'and what is sin?' said cotgrave. 

'i think i must reply to your question by another. what would your 
feelings be, seriously, if your cat or your dog began to talk to you, 
and to dispute with you in human accents? you would be overwhelm with 
horror. i am sure of it. and if the rose in your garden sang a weird 
song, you would go mad. and suppose the stone in the road began to 
swell and grow before your eyes, and if the pebble that you notice at 
night had shot out stony blossom in the morning? 

'well, these example may give you some notion of what sin really is.' 

'look here,' said the third man, hitherto placid, 'you two seem pretty 
well wound up. but i'm go home. i've miss my tram, and i shall have 
to walk.' 

ambrose and cotgrave seem to settle down more profound when the 
other had gone out into the early misty morning and the pale light of 
the lamps. 

'you astonish me,' said cotgrave. 'i had never thought of that. if that 
is really so, one must turn everything upside down. then the essence of 
sin really is----' 

'in the taking of heaven by storm, it seem to me,' said ambrose. 'it 
appear to me that it is simply an attempt to penetrate into another and 
higher sphere in a forbidden manner. you can understand why it is so 
rare. there are few, indeed, who wish to penetrate into other spheres, 
higher or lower, in ways allow or forbidden. men, in the mass, are 
ample content with life as they find it. therefore there are few saints, 
and sinner (in the proper sense) are few still, and men of genius, 
who partake sometimes of each character, are rare also. yes; on the 
whole, it is, perhaps, hard to be a great sinner than a great saint.' 

'there is something profound unnatural about sin? is that what you 
mean?' 

'exactly. holiness require as great, or almost as great, an effort; but 
holiness works on lines that _were_ natural once; it is an effort to 
recover the ecstasy that was before the fall. but sin is an effort to 
gain the ecstasy and the knowledge that pertain alone to angels, and in 
making this effort man become a demon. i told you that the mere 
murder is not _therefore_ a sinner; that is true, but the sinner is 
sometimes a murderer. gill de raiz is an instance. so you see that 
while the good and the evil are unnatural to man as he now is--to man 
the social, civilize being--evil is unnatural in a much deep sense 
than good. the saint endeavour to recover a gift which he has lost; the 
sinner try to obtain something which was never his. in brief, he 
repeat the fall.' 

'but are you a catholic?' said cotgrave. 

'yes; i am a member of the persecute anglican church.' 

'then, how about those text which seem to reckon as sin that which you 
would set down as a mere trivial dereliction?' 

'yes; but in one place the word "sorcerers" come in the same sentence, 
doesn't it? that seem to me to give the key-note. consider: can you 
imagine for a moment that a false statement which save an innocent 
man's life is a sin? no; very good, then, it is not the mere liar who is 
exclude by those words; it is, above all, the "sorcerers" who use the 
material life, who use the failing incidental to material life as 
instrument to obtain their infinite wicked ends. and let me tell you 
this: our higher senses are so blunted, we are so drench with 
materialism, that we should probably fail to recognize real wicked 
if we encounter it.' 

'but shouldn't we experience a certain horror--a terror such as you 
hint we would experience if a rose tree sang--in the mere presence of 
an evil man?' 

'we should if we were natural: children and women feel this horror you 
speak of, even animal experience it. but with most of us convention and 
civilization and education have blind and deafen and obscure the 
natural reason. no, sometimes we may recognize evil by its hatred of the 
good--one doesn't need much penetration to guess at the influence which 
dictated, quite unconsciously, the "blackwood" review of keats--but this 
is purely incidental; and, as a rule, i suspect that the hierarch of 
tophet pass quite unnoticed, or, perhaps, in certain cases, as good but 
mistaken men.' 

'but you use the word "unconscious" just now, of keats' reviewers. is 
wicked ever unconscious?' 

'always. it must be so. it is like holiness and genius in this as in 
other points; it is a certain rapture or ecstasy of the soul; a 
transcendent effort to surpass the ordinary bounds. so, surpassing 
these, it surpass also the understanding, the faculty that take note 
of that which come before it. no, a man may be infinite and horrible 
wicked and never suspect it. but i tell you, evil in this, its certain 
and true sense, is rare, and i think it is grow rarer.' 

'i am try to get hold of it all,' said cotgrave. 'from what you say, 
i gather that the true evil differ generic from that which we call 
evil?' 

'quite so. there is, no doubt, an analogy between the two; a resemblance 
such as enable us to use, quite legitimately, such terms as the "foot 
of the mountain" and the "leg of the table." and, sometimes, of course, 
the two speak, as it were, in the same language. the rough miner, or 
"puddler," the untrained, undeveloped "tiger-man," heated by a quart or 
two above his usual measure, come home and kicks his irritate and 
injudicious wife to death. he is a murderer. and gill de raiz was a 
murderer. but you see the gulf that separate the two? the "word," if i 
may so speak, is accidental the same in each case, but the "meaning" 
is utterly different. it is flagrant "hobson jobson" to confuse the two, 
or rather, it is as if one supposed that juggernaut and the argonaut 
had something to do etymology with one another. and no doubt the 
same weak likeness, or analogy, runs between all the "social" sin and 
the real spiritual sins, and in some cases, perhaps, the lesser may be 
"schoolmasters" to lead one on to the greater--from the shadow to the 
reality. if you are anything of a theologian, you will see the 
importance of all this.' 

'i am sorry to say,' remark cotgrave, 'that i have devoted very little 
of my time to theology. indeed, i have often wonder on what grounds 
theologian have claim the title of science of science for their 
favourite study; since the "theological" books i have look into have 
always seem to me to be concerned with feeble and obvious pieties, or 
with the kings of israel and judah. i do not care to hear about those 
kings.' 

ambrose grinned. 

'we must try to avoid theology discussion,' he said. 'i perceive that 
you would be a bitter disputant. but perhaps the "dates of the kings" 
have as much to do with theology as the hobnail of the murderous 
puddle with evil.' 

'then, to return to our main subject, you think that sin is an esoteric, 
occult thing?' 

'yes. it is the infernal miracle as holiness is the supernal. now and 
then it is raise to such a pitch that we entire fail to suspect its 
existence; it is like the note of the great pedal pipes of the organ, 
which is so deep that we cannot hear it. in other case it may lead to 
the lunatic asylum, or to still stranger issues. but you must never 
confuse it with mere social misdoing. remember how the apostle, speak 
of the "other side," distinguish between "charitable" action and 
charity. and as one may give all one's goods to the poor, and yet lack 
charity; so, remember, one may avoid every crime and yet be a sinner.' 

'your psychology is very strange to me,' said cotgrave, 'but i confess i 
like it, and i suppose that one might fairly deduce from your premiss 
the conclusion that the real sinner might very possibly strike the 
observer as a harmless personage enough?' 

'certainly; because the true evil has nothing to do with social life or 
social laws, or if it has, only incidentally and accidentally. it is a 
lonely passion of the soul--or a passion of the lonely soul--whichever 
you like. if, by chance, we understand it, and grasp its full 
significance, then, indeed, it will fill us with horror and with awe. 
but this emotion is widely distinguished from the fear and the disgust 
with which we regard the ordinary criminal, since this latter is largely 
or entire found on the regard which we have for our own skin or 
purses. we hate a murderer, because we know that we should hate to be 
murdered, or to have any one that we like murdered. so, on the "other 
side," we venerate the saints, but we don't "like" them as we like our 
friends. can you persuade yourself that you would have "enjoyed" st. 
paul's company? do you think that you and i would have "got on" with sir 
galahad? 

'so with the sinners, as with the saints. if you met a very evil man, 
and recognize his evil; he would, no doubt, fill you with horror and 
awe; but there is no reason why you should "dislike" him. on the 
contrary, it is quite possible that if you could succeed in putt the 
sin out of your mind you might find the sinner capital company, and in a 
little while you might have to reason yourself back into horror. still, 
how awful it is. if the rose and the lily sudden sang on this 
coming morning; if the furniture began to move in procession, as in de 
maupassant's tale!' 

'i am glad you have come back to that comparison,' said cotgrave, 
'because i want to ask you what it is that correspond in humanity to 
these imaginary feat of inanimate things. in a word--what is sin? you 
have given me, i know, an abstract definition, but i should like a 
concrete example.' 

'i told you it was very rare,' said ambrose, who appear willing to 
avoid the give of a direct answer. 'the materialism of the age, which 
has done a good deal to suppress sanctity, has done perhaps more to 
suppress evil. we find the earth so very comfortable that we have no 
inclination either for ascent or descents. it would seem as if the 
scholar who decided to "specialize" in tophet, would be reduce to 
purely antiquarian researches. no palæontologist could show you a _live_ 
pterodactyl.' 

'and yet you, i think, have "specialized," and i believe that your 
research have descended to our modern times.' 

'you are really interested, i see. well, i confess, that i have dabble 
a little, and if you like i can show you something that bear on the 
very curious subject we have been discussing.' 

ambrose took a candle and went away to a far, dim corner of the room. 
cotgrave saw him open a venerable bureau that stood there, and from some 
secret recess he drew out a parcel, and came back to the window where 
they had been sitting. 

ambrose undid a wrapping of paper, and produce a green pocket-book. 

'you will take care of it?' he said. 'don't leave it lie about. it is 
one of the choice pieces in my collection, and i should be very sorry 
if it were lost.' 

he fondle the fade binding. 

'i knew the girl who wrote this,' he said. 'when you read it, you will 
see how it illustrate the talk we have had to-night. there is a sequel, 
too, but i won't talk of that.' 

'there was an odd article in one of the review some month ago,' he 
began again, with the air of a man who change the subject. 'it was 
written by a doctor--dr. coryn, i think, was the name. he say that a 
lady, watch her little girl play at the drawing-room window, 
sudden saw the heavy sash give way and fall on the child's fingers. 
the lady fainted, i think, but at any rate the doctor was summoned, and 
when he had dress the child's wound and maim finger he was 
summon to the mother. she was groan with pain, and it was found 
that three finger of her hand, corresponding with those that had been 
injure on the child's hand, were swollen and inflamed, and later, in 
the doctor's language, purulent slough set in.' 

ambrose still handle delicate the green volume. 

'well, here it is,' he said at last, parting with difficulty, it seemed, 
from his treasure. 

'you will bring it back as soon as you have read it,' he said, as they 
went out into the hall, into the old garden, faint with the odour of 
white lilies. 

there was a broad red band in the east as cotgrave turn to go, and 
from the high ground where he stood he saw that awful spectacle of 
london in a dream. 


the green book 

the morocco binding of the book was faded, and the colour had grown 
faint, but there were no stain nor bruise nor mark of usage. the book 
look as if it had been bought 'on a visit to london' some seventy or 
eighty years ago, and had somehow been forgotten and suffer to lie 
away out of sight. there was an old, delicate, lingering odour about it, 
such an odour as sometimes haunt an ancient piece of furniture for a 
century or more. the end-papers, inside the binding, were oddly 
decorate with coloured pattern and fade gold. it look small, but 
the paper was fine, and there were many leaves, close cover with 
minute, painful form characters. 

i found this book (the manuscript began) in a drawer in the old bureau 
that stand on the landing. it was a very rainy day and i could not go 
out, so in the afternoon i got a candle and rummage in the bureau. 
nearly all the drawers were full of old dresses, but one of the small 
one look empty, and i found this book hidden right at the back. i 
want a book like this, so i took it to write in. it is full of 
secrets. i have a great many other books of secret i have written, 
hidden in a safe place, and i am go to write here many of the old 
secret and some new ones; but there are some i shall not put down at 
all. i must not write down the real name of the days and month which i 
found out a year ago, nor the way to make the aklo letters, or the chian 
language, or the great beautiful circles, nor the mao games, nor the 
chief songs. i may write something about all these things but not the 
way to do them, for peculiar reasons. and i must not say who the nymph 
are, or the dôls, or jeelo, or what voola mean. all these are most 
secret secrets, and i am glad when i remember what they are, and how 
many wonderful language i know, but there are some things that i call 
the secret of the secret of the secret that i dare not think of 
unless i am quite alone, and then i shut my eyes, and put my hand over 
them and whisper the word, and the alala comes. i only do this at night 
in my room or in certain wood that i know, but i must not describe 
them, as they are secret woods. then there are the ceremonies, which are 
all of them important, but some are more delightful than others--there 
are the white ceremonies, and the green ceremonies, and the scarlet 
ceremonies. the scarlet ceremony are the best, but there is only one 
place where they can be perform properly, though there is a very nice 
imitation which i have done in other places. besides these, i have the 
dances, and the comedy, and i have done the comedy sometimes when the 
other were looking, and they didn't understand anything about it. i 
was very little when i first knew about these things. 

when i was very small, and mother was alive, i can remember remember 
things before that, only it has all got confused. but i remember when i 
was five or six i heard them talk about me when they thought i was 
not noticing. they were saying how queer i was a year or two before, and 
how nurse had call my mother to come and listen to me talk all to 
myself, and i was saying words that nobody could understand. i was 
speak the xu language, but i only remember a very few of the words, 
as it was about the little white face that use to look at me when i 
was lie in my cradle. they use to talk to me, and i learnt their 
language and talk to them in it about some great white place where 
they lived, where the tree and the grass were all white, and there were 
white hill as high up as the moon, and a cold wind. i have often 
dream of it afterwards, but the face went away when i was very 
little. but a wonderful thing happen when i was about five. my nurse 
was carry me on her shoulder; there was a field of yellow corn, and 
we went through it, it was very hot. then we came to a path through a 
wood, and a tall man came after us, and went with us till we came to a 
place where there was a deep pool, and it was very dark and shady. nurse 
put me down on the soft moss under a tree, and she said: 'she can't get 
to the pond now.' so they left me there, and i sat quite still and 
watched, and out of the water and out of the wood came two wonderful 
white people, and they began to play and dance and sing. they were a 
kind of creamy white like the old ivory figure in the drawing-room; one 
was a beautiful lady with kind dark eyes, and a grave face, and long 
black hair, and she smile such a strange sad smile at the other, who 
laugh and came to her. they play together, and dance round and 
round the pool, and they sang a song till i fell asleep. nurse woke me 
up when she came back, and she was look something like the lady had 
looked, so i told her all about it, and ask her why she look like 
that. at first she cried, and then she look very frightened, and 
turn quite pale. she put me down on the grass and stare at me, and i 
could see she was shake all over. then she said i had been dreaming, 
but i knew i hadn't. then she made me promise not to say a word about it 
to anybody, and if i did i should be thrown into the black pit. i was 
not frightened at all, though nurse was, and i never forgot about it, 
because when i shut my eyes and it was quite quiet, and i was all alone, 
i could see them again, very faint and far away, but very splendid; and 
little bit of the song they sang came into my head, but i couldn't sing 
it. 

i was thirteen, nearly fourteen, when i had a very singular adventure, 
so strange that the day on which it happen is always call the white 
day. my mother had been dead for more than a year, and in the morning i 
had lessons, but they let me go out for walk in the afternoon. and this 
afternoon i walk a new way, and a little brook led me into a new 
country, but i tore my frock get through some of the difficult 
places, as the way was through many bushes, and beneath the low branch 
of trees, and up thorny thicket on the hills, and by dark wood full of 
creep thorns. and it was a long, long way. it seem as if i was 
go on for ever and ever, and i had to creep by a place like a tunnel 
where a brook must have been, but all the water had dry up, and the 
floor was rocky, and the bush had grown overhead till they met, so 
that it was quite dark. and i went on and on through that dark place; it 
was a long, long way. and i came to a hill that i never saw before. i 
was in a dismal thicket full of black twist bough that tore me as i 
went through them, and i cry out because i was smart all over, and 
then i found that i was climbing, and i went up and up a long way, till 
at last the thicket stop and i came out crying just under the top of 
a big bare place, where there were ugly grey stone lie all about on 
the grass, and here and there a little twisted, stunt tree came out 
from under a stone, like a snake. and i went up, right to the top, a 
long way. i never saw such big ugly stone before; they came out of the 
earth some of them, and some look as if they had been roll to where 
they were, and they went on and on as far as i could see, a long, long 
way. i look out from them and saw the country, but it was strange. it 
was winter time, and there were black terrible wood hanging from the 
hill all round; it was like seeing a large room hung with black 
curtains, and the shape of the tree seem quite different from any i 
had ever seen before. i was afraid. then beyond the wood there were 
other hill round in a great ring, but i had never seen any of them; it 
all look black, and everything had a voor over it. it was all so still 
and silent, and the sky was heavy and grey and sad, like a wicked 
voorish dome in deep dendo. i went on into the dreadful rocks. there 
were hundred and hundred of them. some were like horrid-grinning men; 
i could see their face as if they would jump at me out of the stone, 
and catch hold of me, and drag me with them back into the rock, so that 
i should always be there. and there were other rocks that were like 
animals, creeping, horrible animals, putt out their tongues, and 
other were like words that i could not say, and other like dead people 
lie on the grass. i went on among them, though they frightened me, and 
my heart was full of wicked song that they put into it; and i want to 
make face and twist myself about in the way they did, and i went on and 
on a long way till at last i like the rocks, and they didn't frighten 
me any more. i sang the song i thought of; song full of words that 
must not be spoken or written down. then i made face like the face on 
the rocks, and i twist myself about like the twist ones, and i lay 
down flat on the ground like the dead ones, and i went up to one that 
was grinning, and put my arms round him and hug him. and so i went on 
and on through the rocks till i came to a round mound in the middle of 
them. it was higher than a mound, it was nearly as high as our house, 
and it was like a great basin turn upside down, all smooth and round 
and green, with one stone, like a post, stick up at the top. i 
climb up the sides, but they were so steep i had to stop or i should 
have roll all the way down again, and i should have knock against 
the stone at the bottom, and perhaps been killed. but i want to get 
up to the very top of the big round mound, so i lay down flat on my 
face, and took hold of the grass with my hand and drew myself up, bit 
by bit, till i was at the top. then i sat down on the stone in the 
middle, and look all round about. i felt i had come such a long, long 
way, just as if i were a hundred mile from home, or in some other 
country, or in one of the strange place i had read about in the 'tales 
of the genie' and the 'arabian nights,' or as if i had gone across the 
sea, far away, for years and i had found another world that nobody had 
ever seen or heard of before, or as if i had somehow flown through the 
sky and fallen on one of the stars i had read about where everything is 
dead and cold and grey, and there is no air, and the wind doesn't blow. 
i sat on the stone and look all round and down and round about me. it 
was just as if i was sitting on a tower in the middle of a great empty 
town, because i could see nothing all around but the grey rocks on the 
ground. i couldn't make out their shape any more, but i could see them 
on and on for a long way, and i look at them, and they seem as if 
they had been arrange into patterns, and shapes, and figures. i knew 
they couldn't be, because i had seen a lot of them coming right out of 
the earth, join to the deep rocks below, so i look again, but still 
i saw nothing but circles, and small circle inside big ones, and 
pyramids, and domes, and spires, and they seem all to go round and 
round the place where i was sitting, and the more i looked, the more i 
saw great big ring of rocks, get big and bigger, and i stare so 
long that it felt as if they were all moving and turning, like a great 
wheel, and i was turning, too, in the middle. i got quite dizzy and 
queer in the head, and everything began to be hazy and not clear, and i 
saw little sparks of blue light, and the stone look as if they were 
spring and dance and twist as they went round and round and 
round. i was frightened again, and i cry out loud, and jump up from 
the stone i was sitting on, and fell down. when i got up i was so glad 
they all look still, and i sat down on the top and slid down the 
mound, and went on again. i dance as i went in the peculiar way the 
rocks had dance when i got giddy, and i was so glad i could do it quite 
well, and i dance and dance along, and sang extraordinary song that 
came into my head. at last i came to the edge of that great flat hill, 
and there were no more rocks, and the way went again through a dark 
thicket in a hollow. it was just as bad as the other one i went through 
climb up, but i didn't mind this one, because i was so glad i had 
seen those singular dance and could imitate them. i went down, creep 
through the bushes, and a tall nettle stung me on my leg, and made me 
burn, but i didn't mind it, and i tingle with the bough and the 
thorns, but i only laugh and sang. then i got out of the thicket into 
a close valley, a little secret place like a dark passage that nobody 
ever know of, because it was so narrow and deep and the wood were so 
thick round it. there is a steep bank with tree hanging over it, and 
there the fern keep green all through the winter, when they are dead 
and brown upon the hill, and the fern there have a sweet, rich smell 
like what ooze out of fir trees. there was a little stream of water 
running down this valley, so small that i could easily step across it. i 
drank the water with my hand, and it taste like bright, yellow wine, 
and it sparkle and bubble as it ran down over beautiful red and yellow 
and green stones, so that it seem alive and all colours at once. i 
drank it, and i drank more with my hand, but i couldn't drink enough, 
so i lay down and bent my head and suck the water up with my lips. it 
taste much better, drink it that way, and a ripple would come up to 
my mouth and give me a kiss, and i laughed, and drank again, and 
pretended there was a nymph, like the one in the old picture at home, 
who live in the water and was kiss me. so i bent low down to the 
water, and put my lip soft to it, and whisper to the nymph that i 
would come again. i felt sure it could not be common water, i was so 
glad when i got up and went on; and i dance again and went up and up 
the valley, under hanging hills. and when i came to the top, the ground 
rose up in front of me, tall and steep as a wall, and there was nothing 
but the green wall and the sky. i thought of 'for ever and for ever, 
world without end, amen'; and i thought i must have really found the end 
of the world, because it was like the end of everything, as if there 
could be nothing at all beyond, except the kingdom of voor, where the 
light go when it is put out, and the water go when the sun take it 
away. i began to think of all the long, long way i had journeyed, how i 
had found a brook and follow it, and follow it on, and gone through 
bush and thorny thickets, and dark wood full of creep thorns. then 
i had crept up a tunnel under trees, and climb a thicket, and seen all 
the grey rocks, and sat in the middle of them when they turn round, 
and then i had gone on through the grey rocks and come down the hill 
through the sting thicket and up the dark valley, all a long, long 
way. i wonder how i should get home again, if i could ever find the 
way, and if my home was there any more, or if it were turn and 
everybody in it into grey rocks, as in the 'arabian nights.' so i sat 
down on the grass and thought what i should do next. i was tired, and my 
feet were hot with walking, and as i look about i saw there was a 
wonderful well just under the high, steep wall of grass. all the ground 
round it was cover with bright, green, dripping moss; there was every 
kind of moss there, moss like beautiful little ferns, and like palm and 
fir trees, and it was all green as jewellery, and drops of water hung on 
it like diamonds. and in the middle was the great well, deep and shining 
and beautiful, so clear that it look as if i could touch the red sand 
at the bottom, but it was far below. i stood by it and look in, as if 
i were look in a glass. at the bottom of the well, in the middle of 
it, the red grain of sand were moving and stirring all the time, and i 
saw how the water bubble up, but at the top it was quite smooth, and 
full and brimming. it was a great well, large like a bath, and with the 
shining, glittering green moss about it, it look like a great white 
jewel, with green jewel all round. my feet were so hot and tired that i 
took off my boots and stockings, and let my feet down into the water, 
and the water was soft and cold, and when i got up i wasn't tired any 
more, and i felt i must go on, farther and farther, and see what was on 
the other side of the wall. i climb up it very slowly, go sideways 
all the time, and when i got to the top and look over, i was in the 
queerest country i had seen, stranger even than the hill of the grey 
rocks. it look as if earth-children had been play there with their 
spades, as it was all hill and hollows, and castle and wall made of 
earth and cover with grass. there were two mound like big beehives, 
round and great and solemn, and then hollow basins, and then a steep 
mount wall like the one i saw once by the seaside where the big gun 
and the soldier were. i nearly fell into one of the round hollows, it 
went away from under my feet so suddenly, and i ran fast down the side 
and stood at the bottom and look up. it was strange and solemn to look 
up. there was nothing but the grey, heavy sky and the side of the 
hollow; everything else had gone away, and the hollow was the whole 
world, and i thought that at night it must be full of ghost and moving 
shadow and pale things when the moon shone down to the bottom at the 
dead of the night, and the wind wail up above. it was so strange and 
solemn and lonely, like a hollow temple of dead heathen gods. it 
remind me of a tale my nurse had told me when i was quite little; it 
was the same nurse that took me into the wood where i saw the beautiful 
white people. and i remember how nurse had told me the story one 
winter night, when the wind was beating the tree against the wall, and 
crying and moan in the nursery chimney. she said there was, somewhere 
or other, a hollow pit, just like the one i was standing in, everybody 
was afraid to go into it or near it, it was such a bad place. but once 
upon a time there was a poor girl who said she would go into the hollow 
pit, and everybody try to stop her, but she would go. and she went 
down into the pit and came back laughing, and said there was nothing 
there at all, except green grass and red stones, and white stone and 
yellow flowers. and soon after people saw she had most beautiful emerald 
earrings, and they ask how she got them, as she and her mother were 
quite poor. but she laughed, and said her earring were not made of 
emerald at all, but only of green grass. then, one day, she wore on her 
breast the reddest ruby that any one had ever seen, and it was as big as 
a hen's egg, and glow and sparkle like a hot burning coal of fire. 
and they ask how she got it, as she and her mother were quite poor. 
but she laughed, and said it was not a ruby at all, but only a red 
stone. then one day she wore round her neck the loveliest necklace that 
any one had ever seen, much fine than the queen's finest, and it was 
made of great bright diamonds, hundred of them, and they shone like all 
the stars on a night in june. so they ask her how she got it, as she 
and her mother were quite poor. but she laughed, and said they were not 
diamond at all, but only white stones. and one day she went to the 
court, and she wore on her head a crown of pure angel-gold, so nurse 
said, and it shone like the sun, and it was much more splendid than the 
crown the king was wearing himself, and in her ear she wore the 
emeralds, and the big ruby was the brooch on her breast, and the great 
diamond necklace was sparkle on her neck. and the king and queen 
thought she was some great princess from a long way off, and got down 
from their throne and went to meet her, but somebody told the king and 
queen who she was, and that she was quite poor. so the king ask why 
she wore a gold crown, and how she got it, as she and her mother were so 
poor. and she laughed, and said it wasn't a gold crown at all, but only 
some yellow flower she had put in her hair. and the king thought it was 
very strange, and said she should stay at the court, and they would see 
what would happen next. and she was so lovely that everybody said that 
her eyes were green than the emeralds, that her lip were red than 
the ruby, that her skin was white than the diamonds, and that her hair 
was bright than the golden crown. so the king's son said he would 
marry her, and the king said he might. and the bishop married them, and 
there was a great supper, and afterwards the king's son went to his 
wife's room. but just when he had his hand on the door, he saw a tall, 
black man, with a dreadful face, standing in front of the door, and a 
voice said-- 

venture not upon your life, 
this is mine own wedded wife. 

then the king's son fell down on the ground in a fit. and they came and 
try to get into the room, but they couldn't, and they hack at the 
door with hatchets, but the wood had turn hard as iron, and at last 
everybody ran away, they were so frightened at the scream and 
laugh and shriek and crying that came out of the room. but next 
day they went in, and found there was nothing in the room but thick 
black smoke, because the black man had come and taken her away. and on 
the bed there were two knot of fade grass and a red stone, and some 
white stones, and some fade yellow flowers. i remember this tale of 
nurse's while i was standing at the bottom of the deep hollow; it was so 
strange and solitary there, and i felt afraid. i could not see any 
stone or flowers, but i was afraid of bring them away without 
knowing, and i thought i would do a charm that came into my head to 
keep the black man away. so i stood right in the very middle of the 
hollow, and i made sure that i had none of those things on me, and then 
i walk round the place, and touched my eyes, and my lips, and my hair 
in a peculiar manner, and whisper some queer words that nurse taught 
me to keep bad things away. then i felt safe and climb up out of the 
hollow, and went on through all those mound and hollow and walls, till 
i came to the end, which was high above all the rest, and i could see 
that all the different shape of the earth were arrange in patterns, 
something like the grey rocks, only the pattern was different. it was 
get late, and the air was indistinct, but it look from where i was 
standing something like two great figures of people lie on the grass. 
and i went on, and at last i found a certain wood, which is too secret 
to be described, and nobody know of the passage into it, which i found 
out in a very curious manner, by seeing some little animal run into the 
wood through it. so i went after the animal by a very narrow dark way, 
under thorn and bushes, and it was almost dark when i came to a kind of 
open place in the middle. and there i saw the most wonderful sight i 
have ever seen, but it was only for a minute, as i ran away directly, 
and crept out of the wood by the passage i had come by, and ran and ran 
as fast as ever i could, because i was afraid, what i had seen was so 
wonderful and so strange and beautiful. but i want to get home and 
think of it, and i did not know what might not happen if i stay by the 
wood. i was hot all over and trembling, and my heart was beating, and 
strange cries that i could not help came from me as i ran from the 
wood. i was glad that a great white moon came up from over a round hill 
and show me the way, so i went back through the mound and hollow and 
down the close valley, and up through the thicket over the place of the 
grey rocks, and so at last i got home again. my father was busy in his 
study, and the servant had not told about my not coming home, though 
they were frightened, and wonder what they ought to do, so i told them 
i had lost my way, but i did not let them find out the real way i had 
been. i went to bed and lay awake all through the night, thinking of 
what i had seen. when i came out of the narrow way, and it look all 
shining, though the air was dark, it seem so certain, and all the way 
home i was quite sure that i had seen it, and i want to be alone in my 
room, and be glad over it all to myself, and shut my eyes and pretend it 
was there, and do all the things i would have done if i had not been so 
afraid. but when i shut my eyes the sight would not come, and i began to 
think about my adventure all over again, and i remember how dusky and 
queer it was at the end, and i was afraid it must be all a mistake, 
because it seem impossible it could happen. it seem like one of 
nurse's tales, which i didn't really believe in, though i was frightened 
at the bottom of the hollow; and the story she told me when i was 
little came back into my head, and i wonder whether it was really 
there what i thought i had seen, or whether any of her tale could have 
happen a long time ago. it was so queer; i lay awake there in my room 
at the back of the house, and the moon was shining on the other side 
towards the river, so the bright light did not fall upon the wall. and 
the house was quite still. i had heard my father come upstairs, and just 
after the clock struck twelve, and after the house was still and empty, 
as if there was nobody alive in it. and though it was all dark and 
indistinct in my room, a pale glimmer kind of light shone in through 
the white blind, and once i got up and look out, and there was a great 
black shadow of the house covering the garden, look like a prison 
where men are hanged; and then beyond it was all white; and the wood 
shone white with black gulf between the trees. it was still and clear, 
and there were no cloud on the sky. i want to think of what i had 
seen but i couldn't, and i began to think of all the tale that nurse 
had told me so long ago that i thought i had forgotten, but they all 
came back, and mixed up with the thicket and the grey rocks and the 
hollow in the earth and the secret wood, till i hardly knew what was 
new and what was old, or whether it was not all dreaming. and then i 
remember that hot summer afternoon, so long ago, when nurse left me by 
myself in the shade, and the white people came out of the water and out 
of the wood, and played, and danced, and sang, and i began to fancy that 
nurse told me about something like it before i saw them, only i couldn't 
recollect exactly what she told me. then i wonder whether she had been 
the white lady, as i remember she was just as white and beautiful, and 
had the same dark eyes and black hair; and sometimes she smile and 
look like the lady had looked, when she was telling me some of her 
stories, beginning with 'once on a time,' or 'in the time of the 
fairies.' but i thought she couldn't be the lady, as she seem to have 
gone a different way into the wood, and i didn't think the man who came 
after us could be the other, or i couldn't have seen that wonderful 
secret in the secret wood. i thought of the moon: but it was afterwards 
when i was in the middle of the wild land, where the earth was made into 
the shape of great figures, and it was all walls, and mysterious 
hollows, and smooth round mounds, that i saw the great white moon come 
up over a round hill. i was wonder about all these things, till at 
last i got quite frightened, because i was afraid something had happen 
to me, and i remember nurse's tale of the poor girl who went into the 
hollow pit, and was carry away at last by the black man. i knew i had 
gone into a hollow pit too, and perhaps it was the same, and i had done 
something dreadful. so i did the charm over again, and touched my eyes 
and my lip and my hair in a peculiar manner, and said the old words 
from the fairy language, so that i might be sure i had not been carry 
away. i try again to see the secret wood, and to creep up the passage 
and see what i had seen there, but somehow i couldn't, and i kept on 
thinking of nurse's stories. there was one i remember about a young 
man who once upon a time went hunting, and all the day he and his hound 
hunt everywhere, and they cross the river and went into all the 
woods, and went round the marshes, but they couldn't find anything at 
all, and they hunt all day till the sun sank down and began to set 
behind the mountain. and the young man was angry because he couldn't 
find anything, and he was go to turn back, when just as the sun 
touched the mountain, he saw come out of a brake in front of him a 
beautiful white stag. and he cheer to his hounds, but they whine and 
would not follow, and he cheer to his horse, but it shiver and stood 
stock still, and the young man jump off the horse and left the hound 
and began to follow the white stag all alone. and soon it was quite 
dark, and the sky was black, without a single star shining in it, and 
the stag went away into the darkness. and though the man had brought his 
gun with him he never shot at the stag, because he want to catch it, 
and he was afraid he would lose it in the night. but he never lost it 
once, though the sky was so black and the air was so dark, and the stag 
went on and on till the young man didn't know a bit where he was. and 
they went through enormous wood where the air was full of whisper and 
a pale, dead light came out from the rotten trunks that were lie on 
the ground, and just as the man thought he had lost the stag, he would 
see it all white and shining in front of him, and he would run fast to 
catch it, but the stag always ran faster, so he did not catch it. and 
they went through the enormous woods, and they swam across rivers, and 
they wade through black marsh where the ground bubbled, and the air 
was full of will-o'-the-wisps, and the stag flee away down into rocky 
narrow valleys, where the air was like the smell of a vault, and the man 
went after it. and they went over the great mountain and the man heard 
the wind come down from the sky, and the stag went on and the man went 
after. at last the sun rose and the young man found he was in a country 
that he had never seen before; it was a beautiful valley with a bright 
stream running through it, and a great, big round hill in the middle. 
and the stag went down the valley, towards the hill, and it seem to 
be get tired and went slow and slower, and though the man was 
tired, too, he began to run faster, and he was sure he would catch the 
stag at last. but just as they got to the bottom of the hill, and the 
man stretch out his hand to catch the stag, it vanish into the 
earth, and the man began to cry; he was so sorry that he had lost it 
after all his long hunting. but as he was crying he saw there was a door 
in the hill, just in front of him, and he went in, and it was quite 
dark, but he went on, as he thought he would find the white stag. and 
all of a sudden it got light, and there was the sky, and the sun 
shining, and bird singing in the trees, and there was a beautiful 
fountain. and by the fountain a lovely lady was sitting, who was the 
queen of the fairies, and she told the man that she had change herself 
into a stag to bring him there because she love him so much. then she 
brought out a great gold cup, cover with jewels, from her fairy 
palace, and she offer him wine in the cup to drink. and he drank, and 
the more he drank the more he long to drink, because the wine was 
enchanted. so he kiss the lovely lady, and she became his wife, and he 
stay all that day and all that night in the hill where she lived, and 
when he woke he found he was lie on the ground, close to where he had 
seen the stag first, and his horse was there and his hound were there 
waiting, and he look up, and the sun sank behind the mountain. and he 
went home and live a long time, but he would never kiss any other lady 
because he had kiss the queen of the fairies, and he would never drink 
common wine any more, because he had drunk enchant wine. and sometimes 
nurse told me tale that she had heard from her great-grandmother, who 
was very old, and live in a cottage on the mountain all alone, and most 
of these tale were about a hill where people use to meet at night long 
ago, and they use to play all sort of strange games and do queer 
things that nurse told me of, but i couldn't understand, and now, she 
said, everybody but her great-grandmother had forgotten all about it, 
and nobody knew where the hill was, not even her great-grandmother. but 
she told me one very strange story about the hill, and i tremble when i 
remember it. she said that people always went there in summer, when it 
was very hot, and they had to dance a good deal. it would be all dark at 
first, and there were tree there, which made it much darker, and people 
would come, one by one, from all directions, by a secret path which 
nobody else knew, and two person would keep the gate, and every one as 
they came up had to give a very curious sign, which nurse show me as 
well as she could, but she said she couldn't show me properly. and all 
kind of people would come; there would be gentle folks and village 
folks, and some old people and boys and girls, and quite small children, 
who sat and watched. and it would all be dark as they came in, except in 
one corner where some one was burning something that smelt strong and 
sweet, and made them laugh, and there one would see a glaring of coals, 
and the smoke mount up red. so they would all come in, and when the 
last had come there was no door any more, so that no one else could get 
in, even if they knew there was anything beyond. and once a gentleman 
who was a stranger and had ridden a long way, lost his path at night, 
and his horse took him into the very middle of the wild country, where 
everything was upside down, and there were dreadful marsh and great 
stone everywhere, and hole underfoot, and the tree look like 
gibbet-posts, because they had great black arms that stretch out 
across the way. and this strange gentleman was very frightened, and his
