horse began to shiver all over, and at last it stop and wouldn't go 
any farther, and the gentleman got down and try to lead the horse, but 
it wouldn't move, and it was all cover with a sweat, like death. so 
the gentleman went on all alone, go farther and farther into the wild 
country, till at last he came to a dark place, where he heard shouting 
and singing and crying, like nothing he had ever heard before. it all 
sound quite close to him, but he couldn't get in, and so he began to 
call, and while he was calling, something came behind him, and in a 
minute his mouth and arms and leg were all bound up, and he fell into a 
swoon. and when he came to himself, he was lie by the roadside, just 
where he had first lost his way, under a blasted oak with a black trunk, 
and his horse was tie beside him. so he rode on to the town and told 
the people there what had happened, and some of them were amazed; but 
other knew. so when once everybody had come, there was no door at all 
for anybody else to pass in by. and when they were all inside, round in 
a ring, touching each other, some one began to sing in the darkness, and 
some one else would make a noise like thunder with a thing they had on 
purpose, and on still nights people would hear the thundering noise far, 
far away beyond the wild land, and some of them, who thought they knew 
what it was, use to make a sign on their breast when they woke up in 
their bed at dead of night and heard that terrible deep noise, like 
thunder on the mountains. and the noise and the singing would go on and 
on for a long time, and the people who were in a ring sway a little to 
and fro; and the song was in an old, old language that nobody know now, 
and the tune was queer. nurse said her great-grandmother had known some 
one who remember a little of it, when she was quite a little girl, and 
nurse try to sing some of it to me, and it was so strange a tune that 
i turn all cold and my flesh crept as if i had put my hand on 
something dead. sometimes it was a man that sang and sometimes it was a 
woman, and sometimes the one who sang it did it so well that two or 
three of the people who were there fell to the ground shriek and 
tear with their hands. the singing went on, and the people in the 
ring kept sway to and fro for a long time, and at last the moon would 
rise over a place they call the tole deol, and came up and show them 
swinging and sway from side to side, with the sweet thick smoke 
curling up from the burning coals, and floating in circle all around 
them. then they had their supper. a boy and a girl brought it to them; 
the boy carry a great cup of wine, and the girl carry a cake of 
bread, and they pass the bread and the wine round and round, but they 
taste quite different from common bread and common wine, and change 
everybody that taste them. then they all rose up and danced, and secret 
things were brought out of some hiding place, and they play 
extraordinary games, and dance round and round and round in the 
moonlight, and sometimes people would sudden disappear and never be 
heard of afterwards, and nobody knew what had happen to them. and 
they drank more of that curious wine, and they made image and 
worship them, and nurse show me how the image were made one day 
when we were out for a walk, and we pass by a place where there was a 
lot of wet clay. so nurse ask me if i would like to know what those 
things were like that they made on the hill, and i said yes. then she 
ask me if i would promise never to tell a living soul a word about it, 
and if i did i was to be thrown into the black pit with the dead people, 
and i said i wouldn't tell anybody, and she said the same thing again 
and again, and i promised. so she took my wooden spade and dug a big 
lump of clay and put it in my tin bucket, and told me to say if any one 
met us that i was go to make pie when i went home. then we went on a 
little way till we came to a little brake grow right down into the 
road, and nurse stopped, and look up the road and down it, and then 
peep through the hedge into the field on the other side, and then she 
said, 'quick!' and we ran into the brake, and crept in and out among the 
bush till we had gone a good way from the road. then we sat down under 
a bush, and i want so much to know what nurse was go to make with 
the clay, but before she would begin she made me promise again not to 
say a word about it, and she went again and peep through the bush on 
every side, though the lane was so small and deep that hardly anybody 
ever went there. so we sat down, and nurse took the clay out of the 
bucket, and began to knead it with her hands, and do queer things with 
it, and turn it about. and she hid it under a big dock-leaf for a minute 
or two and then she brought it out again, and then she stood up and sat 
down, and walk round the clay in a peculiar manner, and all the time 
she was soft singing a sort of rhyme, and her face got very red. then 
she sat down again, and took the clay in her hand and began to shape it 
into a doll, but not like the doll i have at home, and she made the 
queerest doll i had ever seen, all out of the wet clay, and hid it under 
a bush to get dry and hard, and all the time she was making it she was 
singing these rhyme to herself, and her face got red and redder. so 
we left the doll there, hidden away in the bush where nobody would 
ever find it. and a few days later we went the same walk, and when we 
came to that narrow, dark part of the lane where the brake runs down to 
the bank, nurse made me promise all over again, and she look about, 
just as she had done before, and we crept into the bush till we got to 
the green place where the little clay man was hidden. i remember it all 
so well, though i was only eight, and it is eight years ago now as i am 
writing it down, but the sky was a deep violet blue, and in the middle 
of the brake where we were sitting there was a great elder tree cover 
with blossoms, and on the other side there was a clump of meadowsweet, 
and when i think of that day the smell of the meadowsweet and elder 
blossom seem to fill the room, and if i shut my eyes i can see the 
glaring blue sky, with little cloud very white floating across it, and 
nurse who went away long ago sitting opposite me and look like the 
beautiful white lady in the wood. so we sat down and nurse took out the 
clay doll from the secret place where she had hidden it, and she said we 
must 'pay our respects,' and she would show me what to do, and i must 
watch her all the time. so she did all sort of queer things with the 
little clay man, and i notice she was all stream with perspiration, 
though we had walk so slowly, and then she told me to 'pay my 
respects,' and i did everything she did because i like her, and it was 
such an odd game. and she said that if one love very much, the clay man 
was very good, if one did certain things with it, and if one hate very 
much, it was just as good, only one had to do different things, and we 
play with it a long time, and pretended all sort of things. nurse 
said her great-grandmother had told her all about these images, but what 
we did was no harm at all, only a game. but she told me a story about 
these image that frightened me very much, and that was what i 
remember that night when i was lie awake in my room in the pale, 
empty darkness, thinking of what i had seen and the secret wood. nurse 
said there was once a young lady of the high gentry, who live in a 
great castle. and she was so beautiful that all the gentlemen want to 
marry her, because she was the loveliest lady that anybody had ever 
seen, and she was kind to everybody, and everybody thought she was very 
good. but though she was polite to all the gentlemen who wish to marry 
her, she put them off, and said she couldn't make up her mind, and she 
wasn't sure she want to marry anybody at all. and her father, who was 
a very great lord, was angry, though he was so fond of her, and he ask 
her why she wouldn't choose a bachelor out of all the handsome young men 
who came to the castle. but she only said she didn't love any of them 
very much, and she must wait, and if they pester her, she said she 
would go and be a nun in a nunnery. so all the gentlemen said they 
would go away and wait for a year and a day, and when a year and a day 
were gone, they would come back again and ask her to say which one she 
would marry. so the day was appoint and they all went away; and the 
lady had promise that in a year and a day it would be her wedding day 
with one of them. but the truth was, that she was the queen of the 
people who dance on the hill on summer nights, and on the proper nights 
she would lock the door of her room, and she and her maid would steal 
out of the castle by a secret passage that only they knew of, and go 
away up to the hill in the wild land. and she knew more of the secret 
things than any one else, and more than any one knew before or after, 
because she would not tell anybody the most secret secrets. she knew how 
to do all the awful things, how to destroy young men, and how to put a 
curse on people, and other things that i could not understand. and her 
real name was the lady avelin, but the dance people call her cassap, 
which meant somebody very wise, in the old language. and she was white 
than any of them and taller, and her eyes shone in the dark like burning 
rubies; and she could sing song that none of the other could sing, and 
when she sang they all fell down on their face and worship her. and 
she could do what they call shib-show, which was a very wonderful 
enchantment. she would tell the great lord, her father, that she want 
to go into the wood to gather flowers, so he let her go, and she and 
her maid went into the wood where nobody came, and the maid would keep 
watch. then the lady would lie down under the tree and begin to sing a 
particular song, and she stretch out her arms, and from every part of 
the wood great serpent would come, hiss and gliding in and out among 
the trees, and shoot out their forked tongue as they crawl up to 
the lady. and they all came to her, and twist round her, round her 
body, and her arms, and her neck, till she was cover with writhe 
serpents, and there was only her head to be seen. and she whisper to 
them, and she sang to them, and they writhe round and round, fast and 
faster, till she told them to go. and they all went away directly, back 
to their holes, and on the lady's breast there would be a most curious, 
beautiful stone, shaped something like an egg, and coloured dark blue 
and yellow, and red, and green, marked like a serpent's scales. it was 
call a glame stone, and with it one could do all sort of wonderful 
things, and nurse said her great-grandmother had seen a glame stone with 
her own eyes, and it was for all the world shiny and scaly like a snake. 
and the lady could do a lot of other things as well, but she was quite 
fixed that she would not be married. and there were a great many 
gentlemen who want to marry her, but there were five of them who were 
chief, and their name were sir simon, sir john, sir oliver, sir 
richard, and sir rowland. all the other believe she spoke the truth, 
and that she would choose one of them to be her man when a year and a 
day was done; it was only sir simon, who was very crafty, who thought 
she was deceive them all, and he vow he would watch and try if he 
could find out anything. and though he was very wise he was very young, 
and he had a smooth, soft face like a girl's, and he pretended, as the 
rest did, that he would not come to the castle for a year and a day, and 
he said he was go away beyond the sea to foreign parts. but he 
really only went a very little way, and came back dress like a servant 
girl, and so he got a place in the castle to wash the dishes. and he 
wait and watched, and he listen and said nothing, and he hid in dark 
places, and woke up at night and look out, and he heard things and he 
saw things that he thought were very strange. and he was so sly that he 
told the girl that wait on the lady that he was really a young man, 
and that he had dress up as a girl because he love her so very much 
and want to be in the same house with her, and the girl was so pleased 
that she told him many things, and he was more than ever certain that 
the lady avelin was deceive him and the others. and he was so clever, 
and told the servant so many lies, that one night he manage to hide in 
the lady avelin's room behind the curtains. and he stay quite still 
and never moved, and at last the lady came. and she bent down under the 
bed, and raise up a stone, and there was a hollow place underneath, and 
out of it she took a waxen image, just like the clay one that i and 
nurse had made in the brake. and all the time her eyes were burning like 
rubies. and she took the little wax doll up in her arms and held it to 
her breast, and she whisper and she murmured, and she took it up and 
she laid it down again, and she held it high, and she held it low, and 
she laid it down again. and she said, 'happy is he that begat the 
bishop, that ordered the clerk, that married the man, that had the wife, 
that fashion the hive, that harbour the bee, that gather the wax 
that my own true love was made of.' and she brought out of an aumbry a 
great golden bowl, and she brought out of a closet a great jar of wine, 
and she pour some of the wine into the bowl, and she laid her mannikin 
very gently in the wine, and wash it in the wine all over. then she 
went to a cupboard and took a small round cake and laid it on the 
image's mouth, and then she bore it soft and cover it up. and sir 
simon, who was watch all the time, though he was terribly frightened, 
saw the lady bend down and stretch out her arms and whisper and sing, 
and then sir simon saw beside her a handsome young man, who kiss her 
on the lips. and they drank wine out of the golden bowl together, and 
they ate the cake together. but when the sun rose there was only the 
little wax doll, and the lady hid it again under the bed in the hollow 
place. so sir simon knew quite well what the lady was, and he wait and 
he watched, till the time she had said was nearly over, and in a week 
the year and a day would be done. and one night, when he was watch 
behind the curtains in her room, he saw her making more wax dolls. and 
she made five, and hid them away. and the next night she took one out, 
and held it up, and fill the golden bowl with water, and took the doll 
by the neck and held it under the water. then she said-- 

sir dickon, sir dickon, your day is done, 
you shall be drown in the water wan. 

and the next day news came to the castle that sir richard had been 
drown at the ford. and at night she took another doll and tie a 
violet cord round its neck and hung it up on a nail. then she said-- 

sir rowland, your life has end its span, 
high on a tree i see you hang. 

and the next day news came to the castle that sir rowland had been 
hang by robber in the wood. and at night she took another doll, and 
drove her bodkin right into its heart. then she said-- 

sir noll, sir noll, so cease your life, 
your heart piercèd with the knife. 

and the next day news came to the castle that sir olive had fought in a 
tavern, and a stranger had stab him to the heart. and at night she 
took another doll, and held it to a fire of charcoal till it was melted. 
then she said-- 

sir john, return, and turn to clay, 
in fire of fever you waste away. 

and the next day news came to the castle that sir john had die in a 
burning fever. so then sir simon went out of the castle and mount his 
horse and rode away to the bishop and told him everything. and the 
bishop sent his men, and they took the lady avelin, and everything she 
had done was found out. so on the day after the year and a day, when she 
was to have been married, they carry her through the town in her 
smock, and they tie her to a great stake in the market-place, and 
burn her alive before the bishop with her wax image hung round her 
neck. and people said the wax man scream in the burning of the flames. 
and i thought of this story again and again as i was lie awake in my 
bed, and i seem to see the lady avelin in the market-place, with the 
yellow flame eat up her beautiful white body. and i thought of it so 
much that i seem to get into the story myself, and i fancy i was the 
lady, and that they were coming to take me to be burnt with fire, with 
all the people in the town look at me. and i wonder whether she 
cared, after all the strange things she had done, and whether it hurt 
very much to be burn at the stake. i try again and again to forget 
nurse's stories, and to remember the secret i had seen that afternoon, 
and what was in the secret wood, but i could only see the dark and a 
glimmer in the dark, and then it went away, and i only saw myself 
running, and then a great moon came up white over a dark round hill. 
then all the old story came back again, and the queer rhyme that 
nurse use to sing to me; and there was one beginning 'halsy cumsy helen 
musty,' that she use to sing very soft when she want me to go to 
sleep. and i began to sing it to myself inside of my head, and i went to 
sleep. 

the next morning i was very tired and sleepy, and could hardly do my 
lessons, and i was very glad when they were over and i had had my 
dinner, as i want to go out and be alone. it was a warm day, and i 
went to a nice turfy hill by the river, and sat down on my mother's old 
shawl that i had brought with me on purpose. the sky was grey, like the 
day before, but there was a kind of white gleam behind it, and from 
where i was sitting i could look down on the town, and it was all still 
and quiet and white, like a picture. i remember that it was on that 
hill that nurse taught me to play an old game call 'troy town,' in 
which one had to dance, and wind in and out on a pattern in the grass, 
and then when one had dance and turn long enough the other person 
ask you questions, and you can't help answer whether you want to or 
not, and whatever you are told to do you feel you have to do it. nurse 
said there use to be a lot of games like that that some people knew of, 
and there was one by which people could be turn into anything you 
liked, and an old man her great-grandmother had seen had known a girl 
who had been turn into a large snake. and there was another very 
ancient game of dance and winding and turning, by which you could take 
a person out of himself and hide him away as long as you liked, and his 
body went walking about quite empty, without any sense in it. but i came 
to that hill because i want to think of what had happen the day 
before, and of the secret of the wood. from the place where i was 
sitting i could see beyond the town, into the opening i had found, where 
a little brook had led me into an unknown country. and i pretended i was 
following the brook over again, and i went all the way in my mind, and 
at last i found the wood, and crept into it under the bushes, and then 
in the dusk i saw something that made me feel as if i were fill with 
fire, as if i want to dance and sing and fly up into the air, because 
i was change and wonderful. but what i saw was not change at all, and 
had not grown old, and i wonder again and again how such things could 
happen, and whether nurse's story were really true, because in the 
daytime in the open air everything seem quite different from what it 
was at night, when i was frightened, and thought i was to be burn 
alive. i once told my father one of her little tales, which was about a 
ghost, and ask him if it was true, and he told me it was not true at 
all, and that only common, ignorant people believe in such rubbish. he 
was very angry with nurse for telling me the story, and scold her, 
and after that i promise her i would never whisper a word of what she 
told me, and if i did i should be bitten by the great black snake that 
live in the pool in the wood. and all alone on the hill i wonder what 
was true. i had seen something very amazing and very lovely, and i knew 
a story, and if i had really seen it, and not made it up out of the 
dark, and the black bough, and the bright shining that was mount up 
to the sky from over the great round hill, but had really seen it in 
truth, then there were all kind of wonderful and lovely and terrible 
things to think of, so i long and trembled, and i burn and got cold. 
and i look down on the town, so quiet and still, like a little white 
picture, and i thought over and over if it could be true. i was a long 
time before i could make up my mind to anything; there was such a 
strange flutter at my heart that seem to whisper to me all the time 
that i had not made it up out of my head, and yet it seem quite 
impossible, and i knew my father and everybody would say it was dreadful 
rubbish. i never dream of telling him or anybody else a word about it, 
because i knew it would be of no use, and i should only get laugh at 
or scolded, so for a long time i was very quiet, and went about thinking 
and wondering; and at night i use to dream of amazing things, and 
sometimes i woke up in the early morning and held out my arms with a 
cry. and i was frightened, too, because there were dangers, and some 
awful thing would happen to me, unless i took great care, if the story 
were true. these old tale were always in my head, night and morning, 
and i went over them and told them to myself over and over again, and 
went for walk in the place where nurse had told them to me; and when 
i sat in the nursery by the fire in the evenings i use to fancy nurse 
was sitting in the other chair, and telling me some wonderful story in a 
low voice, for fear anybody should be listening. but she use to like 
best to tell me about things when we were right out in the country, far 
from the house, because she said she was telling me such secrets, and 
wall have ears. and if it was something more than ever secret, we had 
to hide in brake or woods; and i use to think it was such fun creep 
along a hedge, and go very softly, and then we would get behind the 
bush or run into the wood all of a sudden, when we were sure that none 
was watch us; so we knew that we had our secret quite all to 
ourselves, and nobody else at all knew anything about them. now and 
then, when we had hidden ourselves as i have described, she use to show 
me all sort of odd things. one day, i remember, we were in a hazel 
brake, overlook the brook, and we were so snug and warm, as though it 
was april; the sun was quite hot, and the leaves were just coming out. 
nurse said she would show me something funny that would make me laugh, 
and then she show me, as she said, how one could turn a whole house 
upside down, without anybody being able to find out, and the pot and 
pan would jump about, and the china would be broken, and the chair 
would tumble over of themselves. i try it one day in the kitchen, and 
i found i could do it quite well, and a whole row of plate on the 
dresser fell off it, and cook's little work-table tilt up and turn 
right over 'before her eyes,' as she said, but she was so frightened and 
turn so white that i didn't do it again, as i like her. and 
afterwards, in the hazel copse, when she had shown me how to make 
things tumble about, she show me how to make rap noises, and i 
learnt how to do that, too. then she taught me rhyme to say on certain 
occasions, and peculiar mark to make on other occasions, and other 
things that her great-grandmother had taught her when she was a little 
girl herself. and these were all the things i was thinking about in 
those days after the strange walk when i thought i had seen a great 
secret, and i wish nurse were there for me to ask her about it, but 
she had gone away more than two years before, and nobody seem to know 
what had become of her, or where she had gone. but i shall always 
remember those days if i live to be quite old, because all the time i 
felt so strange, wonder and doubting, and feeling quite sure at one 
time, and making up my mind, and then i would feel quite sure that such 
things couldn't happen really, and it began all over again. but i took 
great care not to do certain things that might be very dangerous. so i 
wait and wonder for a long time, and though i was not sure at all, i 
never dare to try to find out. but one day i became sure that all that 
nurse said was quite true, and i was all alone when i found it out. i 
tremble all over with joy and terror, and as fast as i could i ran into 
one of the old brake where we use to go--it was the one by the lane, 
where nurse made the little clay man--and i ran into it, and i crept 
into it; and when i came to the place where the elder was, i cover up 
my face with my hand and lay down flat on the grass, and i stay there 
for two hour without moving, whisper to myself delicious, terrible 
things, and saying some words over and over again. it was all true and 
wonderful and splendid, and when i remember the story i knew and 
thought of what i had really seen, i got hot and i got cold, and the air 
seem full of scent, and flowers, and singing. and first i want to 
make a little clay man, like the one nurse had made so long ago, and i 
had to invent plan and stratagems, and to look about, and to think of 
things beforehand, because nobody must dream of anything that i was 
do or go to do, and i was too old to carry clay about in a tin 
bucket. at last i thought of a plan, and i brought the wet clay to the 
brake, and did everything that nurse had done, only i made a much fine 
image than the one she had made; and when it was finished i did 
everything that i could imagine and much more than she did, because it 
was the likeness of something far better. and a few days later, when i 
had done my lesson early, i went for the second time by the way of the 
little brook that had led me into a strange country. and i follow the 
brook, and went through the bushes, and beneath the low branch of 
trees, and up thorny thicket on the hill, and by dark wood full of 
creep thorns, a long, long way. then i crept through the dark tunnel 
where the brook had been and the ground was stony, till at last i came 
to the thicket that climb up the hill, and though the leaves were 
coming out upon the trees, everything look almost as black as it was 
on the first day that i went there. and the thicket was just the same, 
and i went up slow till i came out on the big bare hill, and began to 
walk among the wonderful rocks. i saw the terrible voor again on 
everything, for though the sky was brighter, the ring of wild hill all 
around was still dark, and the hanging wood look dark and dreadful, 
and the strange rocks were as grey as ever; and when i look down on 
them from the great mound, sitting on the stone, i saw all their amazing 
circle and rounds within rounds, and i had to sit quite still and watch 
them as they began to turn about me, and each stone dance in its place, 
and they seem to go round and round in a great whirl, as if one were 
in the middle of all the stars and heard them rush through the air. 
so i went down among the rocks to dance with them and to sing 
extraordinary songs; and i went down through the other thicket, and 
drank from the bright stream in the close and secret valley, putt my 
lip down to the bubble water; and then i went on till i came to the 
deep, brim well among the glittering moss, and i sat down. i look 
before me into the secret dark of the valley, and behind me was the 
great high wall of grass, and all around me there were the hanging wood 
that made the valley such a secret place. i knew there was nobody here 
at all besides myself, and that no one could see me. so i took off my 
boots and stockings, and let my feet down into the water, saying the 
words that i knew. and it was not cold at all, as i expected, but warm 
and very pleasant, and when my feet were in it i felt as if they were in 
silk, or as if the nymph were kiss them. so when i had done, i said 
the other words and made the signs, and then i dry my feet with a 
towel i had brought on purpose, and put on my stocking and boots. then 
i climb up the steep wall, and went into the place where there are the 
hollows, and the two beautiful mounds, and the round ridge of land, and 
all the strange shapes. i did not go down into the hollow this time, 
but i turn at the end, and made out the figures quite plainly, as it 
was lighter, and i had remember the story i had quite forgotten 
before, and in the story the two figures are call adam and eve, and 
only those who know the story understand what they mean. so i went on 
and on till i came to the secret wood which must not be described, and i 
crept into it by the way i had found. and when i had gone about halfway 
i stopped, and turn round, and got ready, and i bound the handkerchief 
tight round my eyes, and made quite sure that i could not see at all, 
not a twig, nor the end of a leaf, nor the light of the sky, as it was 
an old red silk handkerchief with large yellow spots, that went round 
twice and cover my eyes, so that i could see nothing. then i began to 
go on, step by step, very slowly. my heart beat fast and faster, and 
something rose in my throat that choke me and made me want to cry out, 
but i shut my lips, and went on. bough caught in my hair as i went, and 
great thorn tore me; but i went on to the end of the path. then i 
stopped, and held out my arms and bowed, and i went round the first 
time, feeling with my hands, and there was nothing. i went round the 
second time, feeling with my hands, and there was nothing. then i went 
round the third time, feeling with my hands, and the story was all true, 
and i wish that the years were gone by, and that i had not so long a 
time to wait before i was happy for ever and ever. 

nurse must have been a prophet like those we read of in the bible. 
everything that she said began to come true, and since then other things 
that she told me of have happened. that was how i came to know that her 
story were true and that i had not made up the secret myself out of my 
own head. but there was another thing that happen that day. i went a 
second time to the secret place. it was at the deep brim well, and 
when i was standing on the moss i bent over and look in, and then i 
knew who the white lady was that i had seen come out of the water in the 
wood long ago when i was quite little. and i tremble all over, because 
that told me other things. then i remember how sometime after i had 
seen the white people in the wood, nurse ask me more about them, and i 
told her all over again, and she listened, and said nothing for a long, 
long time, and at last she said, 'you will see her again.' so i 
understood what had happen and what was to happen. and i understood 
about the nymphs; how i might meet them in all kind of places, and they 
would always help me, and i must always look for them, and find them in 
all sort of strange shape and appearances. and without the nymph i 
could never have found the secret, and without them none of the other 
things could happen. nurse had told me all about them long ago, but she 
call them by another name, and i did not know what she meant, or what 
her tale of them were about, only that they were very queer. and there 
were two kinds, the bright and the dark, and both were very lovely and 
very wonderful, and some people saw only one kind, and some only the 
other, but some saw them both. but usually the dark appear first, and 
the bright one came afterwards, and there were extraordinary tale 
about them. it was a day or two after i had come home from the secret 
place that i first really knew the nymphs. nurse had shown me how to 
call them, and i had tried, but i did not know what she meant, and so i 
thought it was all nonsense. but i made up my mind i would try again, so 
i went to the wood where the pool was, where i saw the white people, and 
i try again. the dark nymph, alanna, came, and she turn the pool of 
water into a pool of fire.... 


epilogue 

'that's a very queer story,' said cotgrave, hand back the green book 
to the recluse, ambrose. 'i see the drift of a good deal, but there are 
many things that i do not grasp at all. on the last page, for example, 
what do she mean by "nymphs"?' 

'well, i think there are reference throughout the manuscript to certain 
"processes" which have been hand down by tradition from age to age. 
some of these process are just beginning to come within the purview of 
science, which has arrive at them--or rather at the steps which lead to 
them--by quite different paths. i have interpret the reference to 
"nymphs" as a reference to one of these processes.' 

'and you believe that there are such things?' 

'oh, i think so. yes, i believe i could give you convincing evidence on 
that point. i am afraid you have neglect the study of alchemy? it is a 
pity, for the symbolism, at all events, is very beautiful, and moreover 
if you were acquaint with certain books on the subject, i could recall 
to your mind phrase which might explain a good deal in the manuscript 
that you have been reading.' 

'yes; but i want to know whether you seriously think that there is any 
foundation of fact beneath these fancies. is it not all a department of 
poetry; a curious dream with which man has indulge himself?' 

'i can only say that it is no doubt better for the great mass of people 
to dismiss it all as a dream. but if you ask my veritable belief--that 
go quite the other way. no; i should not say belief, but rather 
knowledge. i may tell you that i have known case in which men have 
stumble quite by accident on certain of these "processes," and have 
been astonish by wholly unexpected results. in the case i am thinking 
of there could have been no possibility of "suggestion" or sub-conscious 
action of any kind. one might as well suppose a schoolboy "suggesting" 
the existence of Æschylus to himself, while he plod mechanical 
through the declensions. 

'but you have notice the obscurity,' ambrose went on, 'and in this 
particular case it must have been dictate by instinct, since the writer 
never thought that her manuscript would fall into other hands. but the 
practice is universal, and for most excellent reasons. powerful and 
sovereign medicines, which are, of necessity, virulent poison also, are 
kept in a lock cabinet. the child may find the key by chance, and 
drink herself dead; but in most case the search is educational, and the 
phial contain precious elixir for him who has patient fashion the 
key for himself.' 

'you do not care to go into details?' 

'no, frankly, i do not. no, you must remain unconvinced. but you saw how 
the manuscript illustrate the talk we had last week?' 

'is this girl still alive?' 

'no. i was one of those who found her. i knew the father well; he was a 
lawyer, and had always left her very much to herself. he thought of 
nothing but deed and leases, and the news came to him as an awful 
surprise. she was missing one morning; i suppose it was about a year 
after she had written what you have read. the servant were called, and 
they told things, and put the only natural interpretation on them--a 
perfectly erroneous one. 

'they discover that green book somewhere in her room, and i found her 
in the place that she describe with so much dread, lie on the ground 
before the image.' 

'it was an image?' 

'yes, it was hidden by the thorn and the thick undergrowth that had 
surround it. it was a wild, lonely country; but you know what it was 
like by her description, though of course you will understand that the 
colours have been heightened. a child's imagination always make the 
heights higher and the depths deep than they really are; and she had, 
unfortunately for herself, something more than imagination. one might 
say, perhaps, that the picture in her mind which she succeed in a 
measure in putt into words, was the scene as it would have appear 
to an imaginative artist. but it is a strange, desolate land.' 

'and she was dead?' 

'yes. she had poison herself--in time. no; there was not a word to be 
said against her in the ordinary sense. you may recollect a story i told 
you the other night about a lady who saw her child's finger crush by 
a window?' 

'and what was this statue?' 

'well, it was of roman workmanship, of a stone that with the century 
had not blackened, but had become white and luminous. the thicket had 
grown up about it and conceal it, and in the middle age the follower 
of a very old tradition had known how to use it for their own purposes. 
in fact it had been incorporated into the monstrous mythology of the 
sabbath. you will have noted that those to whom a sight of that shining 
white had been vouchsafe by chance, or rather, perhaps, by apparent 
chance, were require to blindfold themselves on their second approach. 
that is very significant.' 

'and is it there still?' 

'i sent for tools, and we hammer it into dust and fragments.' 

'the persistence of tradition never surprise me,' ambrose went on after 
a pause. 'i could name many an english parish where such tradition as 
that girl had listen to in her childhood are still existent in occult 
but unabated vigour. no, for me, it is the "story" not the "sequel," 
which is strange and awful, for i have always believe that wonder is of 
the soul.' 




the great god pan 


i 

the experiment 

'i am glad you came, clarke; very glad indeed. i was not sure you could 
spare the time.' 

'i was able to make arrangement for a few days; things are not very 
lively just now. but have you no misgivings, raymond? is it absolutely 
safe?' 

the two men were slow pace the terrace in front of dr. raymond's 
house. the sun still hung above the western mountain-line, but it shone 
with a dull red glow that cast no shadows, and all the air was quiet; a 
sweet breath came from the great wood on the hillside above, and with 
it, at intervals, the soft murmur call of the wild doves. below, in 
the long lovely valley, the river wound in and out between the lonely 
hills, and, as the sun hover and vanish into the west, a faint mist, 
pure white, began to rise from the banks. dr. raymond turn sharp to 
his friend. 

'safe? of course it is. in itself the operation is a perfectly simple 
one; any surgeon could do it.' 

'and there is no danger at any other stage?' 

'none; absolutely no physical danger whatever, i give you my word. you 
are always timid, clarke, always; but you know my history. i have 
devoted myself to transcendental medicine for the last twenty years. i 
have heard myself call quack and charlatan and impostor, but all the 
while i knew i was on the right path. five years ago i reach the goal, 
and since then every day has been a preparation for what we shall do 
to-night.' 

'i should like to believe it is all true.' clarke knit his brows, and 
look doubtful at dr. raymond. 'are you perfectly sure, raymond, that 
your theory is not a phantasmagoria--a splendid vision, certainly, but a 
mere vision after all?' 

dr. raymond stop in his walk and turn sharply. he was a middle-aged 
man, gaunt and thin, of a pale yellow complexion, but as he answer 
clarke and face him, there was a flush on his cheek. 

'look about you, clarke. you see the mountain, and hill following after 
hill, as wave on wave, you see the wood and orchards, the field of 
ripe corn, and the meadow reach to the reed-beds by the river. you 
see me standing here beside you, and hear my voice; but i tell you that 
all these things--yes, from that star that has just shone out in the sky 
to the solid ground beneath our feet--i say that all these are but 
dream and shadows: the shadow that hide the real world from our eyes. 
there _is_ a real world, but it is beyond this glamour and this vision, 
beyond these "chases in arras, dream in a career," beyond them all as 
beyond a veil. i do not know whether any human being has ever lift 
that veil; but i do know, clarke, that you and i shall see it lift 
this very night from before another's eyes. you may think all this 
strange nonsense; it may be strange, but it is true, and the ancients 
knew what lift the veil means. they call it seeing the god pan.' 

clarke shivered; the white mist gathering over the river was chilly. 

'it is wonderful indeed,' he said. 'we are standing on the brink of a 
strange world, raymond, if what you say is true. i suppose the knife is 
absolutely necessary?' 

'yes; a slight lesion in the grey matter, that is all; a trifling 
rearrange of certain cells, a microscopic alteration that would 
escape the attention of ninety-nine brain specialist out of a hundred. 
i don't want to bother you with "shop," clarke; i might give you a mass 
of technical detail which would sound very imposing, and would leave you 
as enlightened as you are now. but i suppose you have read, casually, in 
out-of-the-way corner of your paper, that immense stride have been 
made recently in the physiology of the brain. i saw a paragraph the 
other day about digby's theory, and browne faber's discoveries. theory 
and discoveries! where they are standing now, i stood fifteen years ago, 
and i need not tell you that i have not been standing still for the last 
fifteen years. it will be enough if i say that five years ago i made the 
discovery to which i allude when i said that then i reach the goal. 
after years of labour, after years of toil and grope in the dark, 
after days and nights of disappointment and sometimes of despair, in 
which i use now and then to tremble and grow cold with the thought that 
perhaps there were other seek for what i sought, at last, after so 
long, a pang of sudden joy thrill my soul, and i knew the long journey 
was at an end. by what seem then and still seem a chance, the 
suggestion of a moment's idle thought follow up upon familiar lines 
and path that i had track a hundred times already, the great truth 
burst upon me, and i saw, map out in lines of light, a whole world, a 
sphere unknown; continent and islands, and great ocean in which no 
ship has sail (to my belief) since a man first lift up his eyes and 
beheld the sun, and the stars of heaven, and the quiet earth beneath. 
you will think all this high-flown language, clarke, but it is hard to 
be literal. and yet; i do not know whether what i am hint at cannot 
be set forth in plain and homely terms. for instance, this world of ours 
is pretty well gird now with the telegraph wire and cables; thought, 
with something less than the speed of thought, flash from sunrise to 
sunset, from north to south, across the flood and the desert places. 
suppose that an electrician of to-day were sudden to perceive that he 
and his friends have merely been play with pebble and mistake them 
for the foundations of the world; suppose that such a man saw uttermost 
space lie open before the current, and words of men flash forth to the 
sun and beyond the sun into the system beyond, and the voice of 
articulate-speaking men echo in the waste void that bounds our thought. 
as analogy go, that is a pretty good analogy of what i have done; you 
can understand now a little of what i felt as i stood here one evening; 
it was a summer evening, and the valley look much as it do now; i 
stood here, and saw before me the unutterable, the unthinkable gulf that 
yawn profound between two worlds, the world of matter and the world of 
spirit; i saw the great empty deep stretch dim before me, and in that 
instant a bridge of light leapt from the earth to the unknown shore, 
and the abyss was spanned. you may look in browne faber's book, if you 
like, and you will find that to the present day men of science are 
unable to account for the presence, or to specify the function of a 
certain group of nerve-cells in the brain. that group is, as it were, 
land to let, a mere waste place for fanciful theories. i am not in the 
position of browne faber and the specialists, i am perfectly instruct 
as to the possible function of those nerve-centers in the scheme of 
things. with a touch i can bring them into play, with a touch, i say, i 
can set free the current, with a touch i can complete the communication 
between this world of sense and----we shall be able to finish the 
sentence later on. yes, the knife is necessary; but think what that 
knife will effect. it will level utterly the solid wall of sense, and 
probably, for the first time since man was made, a spirit will gaze on a 
spirit-world. clarke, mary will see the god pan!' 

'but you remember what you wrote to me? i thought it would be requisite 
that she----' 

he whisper the rest into the doctor's ear. 

'not at all, not at all. that is nonsense, i assure you. indeed, it is 
better as it is; i am quite certain of that.' 

'consider the matter well, raymond. it's a great responsibility. 
something might go wrong; you would be a miserable man for the rest of 
your days.' 

'no, i think not, even if the worst happened. as you know, i rescue 
mary from the gutter, and from almost certain starvation, when she was a 
child; i think her life is mine, to use as i see fit. come, it is 
get late; we had better go in.' 

dr. raymond led the way into the house, through the hall, and down a 
long dark passage. he took a key from his pocket and open a heavy 
door, and motion clarke into his laboratory. it had once been a 
billiard-room, and was light by a glass dome in the centre of the 
ceiling, whence there still shone a sad grey light on the figure of the 
doctor as he lit a lamp with a heavy shade and placed it on a table in 
the middle of the room. 

clarke look about him. scarcely a foot of wall remain bare; there 
were shelves all around laden with bottle and phial of all shape and 
colours, and at one end stood a little chippendale bookcase. raymond 
pointed to this. 

'you see that parchment oswald crollius? he was one of the first to show 
me the way, though i don't think he ever found it himself. that is a 
strange saying of his: "in every grain of wheat there lie hidden the 
soul of a star."' 

there was not much of furniture in the laboratory. the table in the 
centre, a stone slab with a drain in one corner, the two armchair on 
which raymond and clarke were sitting; that was all, except an 
odd-looking chair at the furthest end of the room. clarke look at it, 
and raise his eyebrows. 

'yes, that is the chair,' said raymond. 'we may as well place it in 
position,' he got up and wheel the chair to the light, and began 
raise and lower it, letting down the seat, setting the back at 
various angles, and adjust the foot-rest. it look comfortable 
enough, and clarke pass his hand over the soft green velvet, as the 
doctor manipulate the levers. 

'now, clarke, make yourself quite comfortable. i have a couple of 
hours' work before me; i was oblige to leave certain matter to the 
last.' 

raymond went to the stone slab, and clarke watch him dreary as he 
bent over a row of phial and lit the flame under the crucible. the 
doctor had a small hand-lamp, shade as the large one, on a ledge above 
his apparatus, and clarke, who sat in the shadows, look down the great 
dreary room, wonder at the bizarre effects of brilliant light and 
undefined dark contrast with one another. soon he became 
conscious of an odd odour, at first the merest suggestion of odour, in 
the room; and as it grew more decided he felt surprise that he was not 
remind of the chemist's shop or the surgery. clarke found himself idle 
endeavour to analyse the sensation, and, half conscious, he began to 
think of a day, fifteen years ago, that he had spent in roam through 
the wood and meadow near his old home. it was a burning day at the 
beginning of august, the heat had dim the outline of all things and 
all distance with a faint mist, and people who observe the thermometer 
spoke of an abnormal register, of a temperature that was almost 
tropical. strange that wonderful hot day of the 'fifties rose up in 
clarke's imagination; the sense of dazzle all-pervading sunlight 
seem to blot out the shadow and the lights of the laboratory, and he 
felt again the heated air beating in gust about his face, saw the 
shimmer rising from the turf, and heard the myriad murmur of the summer. 

'i hope the smell doesn't annoy you, clarke; there's nothing unwholesome 
about it. it may make you a bit sleepy, that's all.' 

clarke heard the words quite distinctly, and knew that raymond was 
speak to him, but for the life of him he could not rouse himself from 
his lethargy. he could only think of the lonely walk he had taken 
fifteen years ago; it was his last look at the field and wood he had 
known since he was a child, and now it all stood out in brilliant light, 
as a picture, before him. above all there came to his nostril the scent 
of summer, the smell of flower mingled, and the odour of the woods, of 
cool shade places, deep in the green depths, drawn forth by the sun's 
heat; and the scent of the good earth, lie as it were with arms 
stretch forth, and smile lips, overpower all. his fancies made him 
wander, as he had wander long ago, from the field into the wood, 
track a little path between the shining undergrowth of beech-trees; 
and the trickle of water drop from the limestone rock sound as a 
clear melody in the dream. thought began to go astray and to mingle 
with other recollections; the beech alley was transform to a path 
beneath ilex-trees, and here and there a vine climb from bough to 
bough, and sent up wave tendril and droop with purple grapes, and 
the sparse grey-green leaves of a wild olive-tree stood out against the 
dark shadow of the ilex. clarke, in the deep fold of dream, was 
conscious that the path from his father's house had led him into an 
undiscovered country, and he was wonder at the strange of it all, 
when suddenly, in place of the hum and murmur of the summer, an infinite 
silence seem to fall on all things, and the wood was hushed, and for a 
moment of time he stood face to face there with a presence, that was 
neither man nor beast, neither the living nor the dead, but all things 
mingled, the form of all things but devoid of all form. and in that 
moment, the sacrament of body and soul was dissolved, and a voice seem 
to cry 'let us go hence,' and then the dark of dark beyond the 
stars, the dark of everlasting. 

when clarke woke up with a start he saw raymond pour a few drops of 
some oily fluid into a green phial, which he stopper tightly. 

'you have been dozing,' he said; 'the journey must have tired you out. 
it is done now. i am go to fetch mary; i shall be back in ten 
minutes.' 

clarke lay back in his chair and wondered. it seem as if he had but 
pass from one dream into another. he half expect to see the wall of 
the laboratory melt and disappear, and to awake in london, shudder at 
his own sleep fancies. but at last the door opened, and the doctor 
returned, and behind him came a girl of about seventeen, dress all in 
white. she was so beautiful that clarke did not wonder at what the 
doctor had written to him. she was blush now over face and neck and 
arms, but raymond seem unmoved. 

'mary,' he said, 'the time has come. you are quite free. are you willing 
to trust yourself to me entirely?' 

'yes, dear.' 

'you hear that, clarke? you are my witness. here is the chair, mary. it 
is quite easy. just sit in it and lean back. are you ready?' 

'yes, dear, quite ready. give me a kiss before you begin.' 

the doctor stoop and kiss her mouth, kindly enough. 'now shut your 
eyes,' he said. the girl closed her eyelids, as if she were tired, and 
long for sleep, and raymond held the green phial to her nostrils. her 
face grew white, white than her dress; she struggle faintly, and then 
with the feeling of submission strong within her, cross her arms upon 
her breast as a little child about to say her prayers. the bright light 
of the lamp beat full upon her, and clarke watch change fleeting over 
that face as the change of the hill when the summer cloud float 
across the sun. and then she lay all white and still, and the doctor 
turn up one of her eyelids. she was quite unconscious. raymond pressed 
hard on one of the lever and the chair instantly sank back. clarke saw 
him cutting away a circle, like a tonsure, from her hair, and the lamp 
was move nearer. raymond took a small glittering instrument from a 
little case, and clarke turn away shuddering. when he look again the 
doctor was binding up the wound he had made. 

'she will awake in five minutes.' raymond was still perfectly cool. 
'there is nothing more to be done; we can only wait.' 

the minutes pass slowly; they could hear a slow, heavy ticking. there 
was an old clock in the passage. clarke felt sick and faint; his knee 
shook beneath him, he could hardly stand. 

suddenly, as they watched, they heard a long-drawn sigh, and sudden 
did the colour that had vanish return to the girl's cheeks, and 
sudden her eyes opened. clarke quail before them. they shone with an 
awful light, look far away, and a great wonder fell upon her face, 
and her hand stretch out as if to touch what was invisible; but in an 
instant the wonder faded, and gave place to the most awful terror. the 
muscle of her face were hideous convulsed, she shook from head to 
foot; the soul seem struggle and shudder within the house of 
flesh. it was a horrible sight, and clarke rush forward, as she fell 
shriek to the floor. 

three days later raymond took clarke to mary's bedside. she was lie 
wide-awake, rolling her head from side to side, and grin vacantly. 

'yes,' said the doctor, still quite cool, 'it is a great pity; she is a 
hopeless idiot. however, it could not be helped; and, after all, she has 
seen the great god pan.' 


ii 

mr. clarke's memoirs 

mr. clarke, the gentleman chosen by dr. raymond to witness the strange 
experiment of the god pan, was a person in whose character caution and 
curiosity were oddly mingled; in his sober moments he thought of the 
unusual and the eccentric with undisguised aversion, and yet, deep in 
his heart, there was a wide-eyed inquisitive with respect to all the 
more recondite and esoteric elements in the nature of men. the latter 
tendency had prevail when he accept raymond's invitation, for though 
his considered judgment had always repudiate the doctor's theory as 
the wildest nonsense, yet he secret hug a belief in fantasy, and 
would have rejoice to see that belief confirmed. the horrors that he 
witness in the dreary laboratory were to a certain extent salutary; he 
was conscious of being involved in an affair not altogether reputable, 
and for many years afterwards he clung brave to the commonplace, and 
reject all occasion of occult investigation. indeed, on some 
hom[oe]opathic principle, he for some time attend the see of 
distinguished mediums, hope that the clumsy trick of these gentlemen 
would make him altogether disgust with mysticism of every kind, but 
the remedy, though caustic, was not efficacious. clarke knew that he 
still pine for the unseen, and little by little, the old passion began 
to reassert itself, as the face of mary, shudder and convulse with 
an unknowable terror, fade slow from his memory. occupy all day in 
pursuit both serious and lucrative, the temptation to relax in the 
evening was too great, especially in the winter months, when the fire 
cast a warm glow over his snug bachelor apartment, and a bottle of some 
choice claret stood ready by his elbow. his dinner digested, he would 
make a brief pretence of reading the evening paper, but the mere 
catalogue of news soon pall upon him, and clarke would find himself 
casting glance of warm desire in the direction of an old japan 
bureau, which stood at a pleasant distance from the hearth. like a boy 
before a jam-closet, for a few minutes he would hover indecisive, but 
lust always prevailed, and clarke end by drawing up his chair, 
lighting a candle, and sitting down before the bureau. its pigeonhole 
and drawers teem with document on the most morbid subjects, and in 
the well repose a large manuscript volume, in which he had painful 
enter the gem of his collection. clarke had a fine contempt for 
publish literature; the most ghostly story cease to interest him if 
it happen to be printed; his sole pleasure was in the reading, 
compiling, and rearrange what he call his 'memoirs to prove the 
existence of the devil,' and engaged in this pursuit the evening seem 
to fly and the night appear too short. 

on one particular evening, an ugly december night, black with fog, and 
raw with frost, clarke hurried over his dinner, and scarcely deign to 
observe his customary ritual of taking up the paper and lay it down 
again. he pace two or three times up and down the room, and open the 
bureau, stood still a moment, and sat down. he leant back, absorb in 
one of those dream to which he was subject, and at length drew out his 
book, and open it at the last entry. there were three or four page 
dense cover with clarke's round, set penmanship, and at the 
beginning he had written in a somewhat large hand: 

singular narrative told me by my friend, dr. phillips. he assure me 
that all the fact related therein are strict and wholly true, but 
refuse to give either the surname of the person concerned, or the 
place where these extraordinary event occurred. 

mr. clarke began to read over the account for the tenth time, glancing 
now and then at the pencil note he had made when it was told him by his 
friend. it was one of his humour to pride himself on a certain literary 
ability; he thought well of his style, and took pains in arrange the 
circumstances in dramatic order. he read the following story:-- 

the person concerned in this statement are helen v., who, if she is 
still alive, must now be a woman of twenty-three, rachel m., since 
deceased, who was a year younger than the above, and trevor w., an 
imbecile, age eighteen. these person were at the period of the story 
inhabitant of a village on the border of wales, a place of some 
importance in the time of the roman occupation, but now a scattered 
hamlet, of not more than five hundred souls. it is situated on rising 
ground, about six mile from the sea, and is sheltered by a large and 
picturesque forest. 

some eleven years ago, helen v. came to the village under rather 
peculiar circumstances. it is understood that she, being an orphan, was 
adopt in her infancy by a distant relative, who brought her up in his 
own house till she was twelve years old. thinking, however, that it 
would be better for the child to have playmate of her own age, he 
advertise in several local papers for a good home in a comfortable 
farmhouse for a girl of twelve, and this advertisement was answer by 
mr. r., a well-to-do farmer in the above-mentioned village. his 
reference prove satisfactory, the gentleman sent his adopt daughter 
to mr. r., with a letter, in which he stipulate that the girl should 
have a room to herself, and state that her guardian need be at no 
trouble in the matter of education, as she was already sufficient 
educated for the position in life which she would occupy. in fact, mr. 
r. was given to understand that the girl was to be allow to find her 
own occupations, and to spend her time almost as she liked. mr. r. duly 
met her at the nearest station, a town some seven mile away from his 
house, and seem to have remark nothing extraordinary about the 
child, except that she was reticent as to her former life and her 
adopt father. she was, however, of a very different type from the 
inhabitant of the village; her skin was a pale, clear olive, and her 
features were strong marked, and of a somewhat foreign character. she 
appear to have settled down easily enough into farmhouse life, and 
became a favourite with the children, who sometimes went with her on her 
ramble in the forest, for this was her amusement. mr. r. states that he 
has known her go out by herself directly after their early breakfast, 
and not return till after dusk, and that, feeling uneasy at a young girl 
being out alone for so many hours, he communicate with her adopt 
father, who reply in a brief note that helen must do as she chose. in 
the winter, when the forest path are impassable, she spent most of her 
time in her bedroom, where she slept alone, according to the 
instruction of her relative. it was on one of these expedition to the 
forest that the first of the singular incident with which this girl is 
connected occurred, the date being about a year after her arrival at the 
village. the preceding winter had been remarkably severe, the snow 
drift to a great depth, and the frost continue for an unexampled 
period, and the summer following was as noteworthy for its extreme heat. 
on one of the very hottest days in this summer, helen v. left the 
farmhouse for one of her long ramble in the forest, taking with her, as 
usual, some bread and meat for lunch. she was seen by some men in the 
field making for the old roman road, a green causeway which traverse 
the highest part of the wood, and they were astonish to observe that 
the girl had taken off her hat, though the heat of the sun was already 
almost tropical. as it happened, a labourer, joseph w. by name, was 
working in the forest near the roman road, and at twelve o'clock his 
little son, trevor, brought the man his dinner of bread and cheese. 
after the meal, the boy, who was about seven years old at the time, left 
his father at work, and, as he said, went to look for flower in the 
wood, and the man, who could hear him shouting with delight over his 
discoveries, felt no uneasiness. suddenly, however, he was horrify at 
hearing the most dreadful screams, evidently the result of great terror, 
proceeding from the direction in which his son had gone, and he hasty 
threw down his tool and ran to see what had happened. tracing his path 
by the sound, he met the little boy, who was running headlong, and was 
evidently terribly frightened, and on questioning him the man at last 
elicit that after pick a posy of flower he felt tired, and lay 
down on the grass and fell asleep. he was sudden awakened, as he 
stated, by a peculiar noise, a sort of singing he call it, and on 
peep through the branch he saw helen v. play on the grass with a 
'strange naked man,' whom he seem unable to describe more fully. he 
said he felt dreadfully frightened, and ran away crying for his father. 
joseph w. proceed in the direction indicate by his son, and found 
helen v. sitting on the grass in the middle of a glade or open space 
left by charcoal burners. he angry charged her with frighten his 
little boy, but she entire deny the accusation and laugh at the 
child's story of a 'strange man,' to which he himself did not attach 
much credence. joseph w. came to the conclusion that the boy had woke up 
with a sudden fright, as children sometimes do, but trevor persist in 
his story, and continue in such evident distress that at last his 
father took him home, hope that his mother would be able to soothe 
him. for many weeks, however, the boy gave his parent much anxiety; he 
became nervous and strange in his manner, refuse to leave the cottage 
by himself, and constant alarm the household by waking in the night 
with cries of 'the man in the wood! father! father!' 

in course of time, however, the impression seem to have worn off, and 
about three month later he accompany his father to the house of a 
gentleman in the neighbourhood, for whom joseph w. occasional did 
work. the man was shown into the study, and the little boy was left 
sitting in the hall, and a few minutes later, while the gentleman was 
give w. his instructions, they were both horrify by a piercing 
shriek and the sound of a fall, and rush out they found the child 
lie senseless on the floor, his face contort with terror. the doctor 
was immediately summoned, and after some examination he pronounced the 
child to be suffering from a kind of fit, apparently produce by a 
sudden shock. the boy was taken to one of the bedrooms, and after some 
time recover consciousness, but only to pass into a condition 
describe by the medical man as one of violent hysteria. the doctor 
exhibit a strong sedative, and in the course of two hour pronounced 
him fit to walk home, but in passing through the hall the paroxysm of 
fright return and with additional violence. the father perceive that 
the child was point at some object, and heard the old cry, 'the man 
in the wood,' and look in the direction indicate saw a stone head of 
grotesque appearance, which had been built into the wall above one of 
the doors. it seem that the owner of the house had recently made 
alteration in his premises, and on digging the foundation for some 
offices, the men had found a curious head, evidently of the roman 
period, which had been placed in the hall in the manner described. the 
head is pronounced by the most experienced archaeologist of the 
district to be that of a faun or satyr.[1] 

[1] dr. phillip tell me that he has seen the head in question, and 
assure me that he has never received such a vivid present of 
intense evil. 

from whatever cause arising, this second shock seem too severe for the 
boy trevor, and at the present date he suffer from a weakness of 
intellect, which give but little promise of amending. the matter cause 
a good deal of sensation at the time, and the girl helen was close 
question by mr. r., but to no purpose, she steadfast deny that 
she had frightened or in any way molest trevor. 

the second event with which this girl's name is connected took place 
about six years ago, and is of a still more extraordinary character. 

at the beginning of the summer of 1882 helen contract a friendship of 
a peculiarly intimate character with rachel m., the daughter of a 
prosperous farmer in the neighbourhood. this girl, who was a year 
younger than helen, was considered by most people to be the pretty of 
the two, though helen's features had to a great extent soften as she 
became older. the two girls, who were together on every available 
opportunity, present a singular contrast, the one with her clear, 
olive skin and almost italy appearance, and the other of the 
proverbial red and white of our rural districts. it must be state that 
the payment made to mr. r. for the maintenance of helen were known in 
the village for their excessive liberality, and the impression was 
general that she would one day inherit a large sum of money from her 
relative. the parent of rachel were therefore not averse from their 
daughter's friendship with the girl, and even encourage the intimacy, 
though they now bitter regret have done so. helen still retain her 
extraordinary fond for the forest, and on several occasion rachel 
accompany her, the two friends setting out early in the morning, and 
remain in the wood till dusk. once or twice after these excursion 
mrs. m. thought her daughter's manner rather peculiar; she seem 
languid and dreamy, and as it has been expressed, 'different from 
herself,' but these peculiarity seem to have been thought too trifling 
for remark. one evening, however, after rachel had come home, her mother 
heard a noise which sound like suppress weeping in the girl's room, 
and on go in found her lying, half undressed, upon the bed, evidently 
in the greatest distress. as soon as she saw her mother, she exclaimed, 
'ah, mother, mother, why did you let me go to the forest with helen?' 
mrs. m. was astonish at so strange a question, and proceed to make 
inquiries. rachel told her a wild story. she said-- 

clarke closed the book with a snap, and turn his chair towards the 
fire. when his friend sat one evening in that very chair, and told his 
story, clarke had interrupt him at a point a little subsequent to 
this, had cut short his words in a paroxysm of horror. 'my god!' he had 
exclaimed, 'think, think what you are saying. it is too incredible, too 
monstrous; such things can never be in this quiet world, where men and 
women live and die, and struggle, and conquer, or maybe fail, and fall 
down under sorrow, and grieve and suffer strange fortune for many a 
year; but not this, phillips, not such things as this. there must be 
some explanation, some way out of the terror. why, man, if such a case 
were possible, our earth would be a nightmare.' 

but phillip had told his story to the end, concluding: 

'her flight remains a mystery to this day; she vanish in broad 
sunlight; they saw her walking in a meadow, and a few moments later she 
was not there.' 

clarke try to conceive the thing again, as he sat by the fire, and 
again his mind shudder and shrank back, appall before the sight of 
such awful, unspeakable elements enthroned as it were, and triumphant in 
human flesh. before him stretch the long dim vista of the green 
causeway in the forest, as his friend had describe it; he saw the 
sway leaves and the quiver shadow on the grass, he saw the 
sunlight and the flowers, and far away, far in the long distance, the 
two figures move toward him. one was rachel, but the other? 

clarke had try his best to disbelieve it all, but at the end of the 
account, as he had written it in his book, he had placed the 
inscription: 

et diabolu incarnatu est. et homo factu est. 


iii 

the city of resurrection 

'herbert! good god! is it possible?' 

'yes, my name's herbert. i think i know your face too, but i don't 
remember your name. my memory is very queer.' 

'don't you recollect villier of wadham?' 

'so it is, so it is. i beg your pardon, villiers, i didn't think i was 
beg of an old college friend. good-night.' 

'my dear fellow, this haste is unnecessary. my rooms are close by, but 
we won't go there just yet. suppose we walk up shaftesbury avenue a 
little way? but how in heaven's name have you come to this pass, 
herbert?' 

'it's a long story, villiers, and a strange one too, but you can hear it 
if you like.' 

'come on, then. take my arm, you don't seem very strong.' 

the ill-assorted pair move slow up rupert street; the one in dirty, 
evil-looking rags, and the other attire in the regulation uniform of a 
man about town, trim, glossy, and eminently well-to-do. villier had 
emerge from his restaurant after an excellent dinner of many courses, 
assist by an ingratiating little flask of chianti, and, in that frame 
of mind which was with him almost chronic, had delay a moment by the 
door, peer round in the dimly-lighted street in search of those 
mysterious incident and person with which the street of london teem 
in every quarter and at every hour. villier pride himself as a 
practised explore of such obscure maze and byways of london life, and 
in this unprofitable pursuit he display an assiduity which was worthy 
of more serious employment. thus he stood beside the lamp-post survey 
the passers-by with undisguised curiosity, and with that gravity only 
known to the systematic diner, had just enunciate in his mind the 
formula: 'london has been call the city of encounters; it is more than 
that, it is the city of resurrections,' when these reflection were 
sudden interrupt by a piteous whine at his elbow, and a deplorable 
appeal for alms. he look around in some irritation, and with a sudden 
shock found himself confront with the embody proof of his somewhat 
stilted fancies. there, close beside him, his face alter and 
disfigure by poverty and disgrace, his body barely cover by greasy 
ill-fitting rags, stood his old friend charles herbert, who had 
matriculate on the same day as himself, with whom he had been merry and 
wise for twelve revolving terms. different occupation and vary 
interests had interrupt the friendship, and it was six years since 
villier had seen herbert; and now he look upon this wreck of a man 
with grief and dismay, mingle with a certain inquisitive as to what 
dreary chain of circumstance had drag him down to such a doleful 
pass. villier felt together with compassion all the relish of the 
amateur in mysteries, and congratulate himself on his leisurely 
speculation outside the restaurant. 

they walk on in silence for some time, and more than one passer-by 
stare in astonishment at the unaccustomed spectacle of a well-dressed 
man with an unmistakable beggar hanging on to his arm, and, observing 
this, villier led the way to an obscure street in soho. here he 
repeated his question. 

'how on earth has it happened, herbert? i always understood you would 
succeed to an excellent position in dorsetshire. did your father 
disinherit you? surely not?' 

'no, villiers; i came into all the property at my poor father's death; 
he die a year after i left oxford. he was a very good father to me, and 
i mourn his death sincerely enough. but you know what young men are; a 
few month later i came up to town and went a good deal into society. of 
course i had excellent introductions, and i manage to enjoy myself very 
much in a harmless sort of way. i play a little, certainly, but never 
for heavy stakes, and the few bet i made on races brought me in 
money--only a few pounds, you know, but enough to pay for cigar and 
such petty pleasures. it was in my second season that the tide turned. 
of course you have heard of my marriage?' 

'no, i never heard anything about it.' 

'yes, i married, villiers. i met a girl, a girl of the most wonderful 
and most strange beauty, at the house of some people whom i knew. i 
cannot tell you her age; i never knew it, but, so far as i can guess, i 
should think she must have been about nineteen when i made her 
acquaintance. my friends had come to know her at florence; she told 
them she was an orphan, the child of an english father and an italy 
mother, and she charm them as she charm me. the first time i saw her 
was at an evening party. i was standing by the door talk to a friend, 
when sudden above the hum and babble of conversation i heard a voice 
which seem to thrill to my heart. she was singing an italy song. i 
was introduce to her that evening, and in three month i married helen. 
villiers, that woman, if i can call her woman, corrupt my soul. the 
night of the wedding i found myself sitting in her bedroom in the hotel, 
listen to her talk. she was sitting up in bed, and i listen to her 
as she spoke in her beautiful voice, spoke of things which even now i 
would not dare whisper in blackest night, though i stood in the midst of 
a wilderness. you, villiers, you may think you know life, and london, 
and what go on day and night in this dreadful city; for all i can say 
you may have heard the talk of the vilest, but i tell you you can have 
no conception of what i know, not in your most fantastic, hideous dream 
can you have image forth the faintest shadow of what i have heard--and 
seen. yes, seen. i have seen the incredible, such horrors that even i 
myself sometimes stop in the middle of the street, and ask whether it is 
possible for a man to behold such things and live. in a year, villiers, 
i was a ruin man, in body and soul--in body and soul.' 

'but your property, herbert? you had land in dorset.' 

'i sold it all; the field and woods, the dear old house--everything.' 

'and the money?' 

'she took it all from me.' 

'and then left you?' 

'yes; she disappear one night. i don't know where she went, but i am 
sure if i saw her again it would kill me. the rest of my story is of no 
interest; sordid misery, that is all. you may think, villiers, that i 
have exaggerate and talk for effect; but i have not told you half. i 
could tell you certain things which would convince you, but you would 
never know a happy day again. you would pass the rest of your life, as i 
pass mine, a haunt man, a man who has seen hell.' 

villier took the unfortunate man to his rooms, and gave him a meal. 
herbert could eat little, and scarcely touched the glass of wine set 
before him. he sat moody and silent by the fire, and seem relieved 
when villier sent him away with a small present of money. 

'by the way, herbert,' said villiers, as they part at the door, 'what 
was your wife's name? you said helen, i think? helen what?' 

'the name she pass under when i met her was helen vaughan, but what 
her real name was i can't say. i don't think she had a name. no, no, not 
in that sense. only human being have names, villiers; i can't say any 
more. good-bye; yes, i will not fail to call if i see any way in which 
you can help me. good-night.' 

the man went out into the bitter night, and villier return to his 
fireside. there was something about herbert which shock him 
inexpressibly; not his poor rag nor the mark which poverty had set 
upon his face, but rather an indefinite terror which hung about him 
like a mist. he had acknowledge that he himself was not devoid of 
blame; the woman, he had avowed, had corrupt him body and soul, and 
villier felt that this man, once his friend, had been an actor in 
scene evil beyond the power of words. his story need no confirmation: 
he himself was the embody proof of it. villier muse curious over 
the story he had heard, and wonder whether he had heard both the first 
and the last of it. 'no,' he thought, 'certainly not the last, probably 
only the beginning. a case like this is like a nest of china boxes; 
you open one after another and find a quaint workmanship in every box. 
most likely poor herbert is merely one of the outside boxes; there are 
stranger one to follow.' 

villier could not take his mind away from herbert and his story, which 
seem to grow wild as the night wore on. the fire began to burn low, 
and the chilly air of the morning crept into the room; villier got up 
with a glance over his shoulder, and shiver slightly, went to bed. 

a few days later he saw at his club a gentleman of his acquaintance, 
name austin, who was famous for his intimate knowledge of london life, 
both in its tenebrous and luminous phases. villiers, still full of his 
encounter in soho and its consequences, thought austin might possibly be 
able to shed some light on herbert's history, and so after some casual 
talk he sudden put the question: 

'do you happen to know anything of a man name herbert--charles 
herbert?' 

austin turn round sharp and stare at villier with some 
astonishment. 

'charles herbert? weren't you in town three years ago? no; then you have 
not heard of the paul street case? it cause a good deal of sensation at 
the time.' 

'what was the case?' 

'well, a gentleman, a man of very good position, was found dead, stark 
dead, in the area of a certain house in paul street, off tottenham court 
road. of course the police did not make the discovery; if you happen to 
be sitting up all night and have a light in your window, the constable 
will ring the bell, but if you happen to be lie dead in somebody's 
area, you will be left alone. in this instance as in many other the 
alarm was raise by some kind of vagabond; i don't mean a common tramp, 
or a public-house loafer, but a gentleman, whose business or pleasure, 
or both, made him a spectator of the london street at five o'clock in 
the morning. this individual was, as he said, "going home," it did not 
appear whence or whither, and had occasion to pass through paul street 
between four and five a. m. something or other caught his eye at number 
20; he said, absurd enough, that the house had the most unpleasant 
physiognomy he had ever observed, but, at any rate, he glance down the 
area, and was a good deal astonish to see a man lie on the stones, 
his limb all huddle together, and his face turn up. our gentleman 
thought his face look peculiarly ghastly, and so set off at a run in
