search of the nearest policeman. the constable was at first inclined to 
treat the matter lightly, suspect common drunkenness; however, he 
came, and after look at the man's face, change his tone, quick 
enough. the early bird, who had picked up this fine worm, was sent off 
for a doctor, and the policeman rang and knock at the door till a 
slattern servant girl came down look more than half asleep. the 
constable pointed out the contents of the area to the maid, who scream 
loud enough to wake up the street, but she knew nothing of the man; 
had never seen him at the house, and so forth. meanwhile the original 
discover had come back with a medical man, and the next thing was to 
get into the area. the gate was open, so the whole quartet stump down 
the steps. the doctor hardly need a moment's examination; he said the 
poor fellow had been dead for several hours, and it was then the case 
began to get interesting. the dead man had not been robbed, and in one 
of his pocket were papers identify him as--well, as a man of good 
family and means, a favourite in society, and nobody's enemy, so far as 
could be known. i don't give his name, villiers, because it has nothing 
to do with the story, and because it's no good rake up these affair 
about the dead when there are no relations living. the next curious 
point was that the medical men couldn't agree as to how he met his 
death. there were some slight bruise on his shoulders, but they were so 
slight that it look as if he had been pushed roughly out of the 
kitchen door, and not thrown over the railing from the street or even 
drag down the steps. but there were positively no other mark of 
violence about him, certainly none that would account for his death; and 
when they came to the autopsy there wasn't a trace of poison of any 
kind. of course the police want to know all about the people at number 
20, and here again, so i have heard from private sources, one or two 
other very curious points came out. it appear that the occupant of the 
house were a mr. and mrs. charles herbert; he was said to be a landed 
proprietor, though it struck most people that paul street was not 
exactly the place to look for county gentry. as for mrs. herbert, nobody 
seem to know who or what she was, and, between ourselves, i fancy the 
divers after her history found themselves in rather strange waters. of 
course they both deny knowing anything about the deceased, and in 
default of any evidence against them they were discharged. but some very 
odd things came out about them. though it was between five and six in 
the morning when the dead man was removed, a large crowd had collected, 
and several of the neighbour ran to see what was go on. they were 
pretty free with their comments, by all accounts, and from these it 
appear that number 20 was in very bad odour in paul street. the 
detective try to trace down these rumour to some solid foundation of 
fact, but could not get hold of anything. people shook their head and 
raise their eyebrow and thought the herbert rather "queer," "would 
rather not be seen go into their house," and so on, but there was 
nothing tangible. the authority were morally certain that the man met 
his death in some way or another in the house and was thrown out by the 
kitchen door, but they couldn't prove it, and the absence of any 
indication of violence or poison left them helpless. an odd case, 
wasn't it? but curious enough, there's something more that i haven't 
told you. i happen to know one of the doctor who was consult as to 
the cause of death, and some time after the inquest i met him, and ask 
him about it. "do you really mean to tell me," i said, "that you were 
baffle by the case, that you actually don't know what the man die of?" 
"pardon me," he replied, "i know perfectly well what cause death. blank 
die of fright, of sheer, awful terror; i never saw features so 
hideous contort in the entire course of my practice, and i have seen 
the face of a whole host of dead." the doctor was usually a cool 
customer enough, and a certain vehemence in his manner struck me, but i 
couldn't get anything more out of him. i suppose the treasury didn't see 
their way to prosecute the herbert for frighten a man to death; at 
any rate, nothing was done, and the case drop out of men's minds. do 
you happen to know anything of herbert?' 

'well,' reply villiers, 'he was an old college friend of mine.' 

'you don't say so? have you ever seen his wife?' 

'no, i haven't. i have lost sight of herbert for many years.' 

'it's queer, isn't it, parting with a man at the college gate or at 
paddington, seeing nothing of him for years, and then finding him pop up 
his head in such an odd place. but i should like to have seen mrs. 
herbert; people said extraordinary things about her.' 

'what sort of things?' 

'well, i hardly know how to tell you. every one who saw her at the 
police court said she was at once the most beautiful woman and the most 
repulsive they had ever set eyes on. i have spoken to a man who saw her, 
and i assure you he positively shudder as he try to describe the 
woman, but he couldn't tell why. she seem to have been a sort of 
enigma; and i expect if that one dead man could have told tales, he 
would have told some uncommonly queer ones. and there you are again in 
another puzzle; what could a respectable country gentleman like mr. 
blank (we'll call him that if you don't mind) want in such a very queer 
house as number 20? it's altogether a very odd case, isn't it?' 

'it is indeed, austin; an extraordinary case. i didn't think, when i 
ask you about my old friend, i should strike on such strange metal. 
well, i must be off; good-day.' 

villier went away, thinking of his own conceit of the china boxes; 
here was quaint workmanship indeed. 


iv 

the discovery in paul street 

a few month after villiers's meeting with herbert, mr. clarke was 
sitting, as usual, by his after-dinner hearth, resolute guard his 
fancies from wandering in the direction of the bureau. for more than a 
week he had succeed in keeping away from the 'memoirs,' and he 
cherish hope of a complete self-reformation; but, in spite of his 
endeavours, he could not hush the wonder and the strange curiosity that 
that last case he had written down had excited within him. he had put 
the case, or rather the outline of it, conjectural to a scientific 
friend, who shook his head, and thought clarke get queer, and on 
this particular evening clarke was making an effort to rationalize the 
story, when a sudden knock at his door rouse him from his meditations. 

'mr. villier to see you, sir.' 

'dear me, villiers, it is very kind of you to look me up; i have not 
seen you for many months; i should think nearly a year. come in, come 
in. and how are you, villiers? want any advice about investments?' 

'no, thanks, i fancy everything i have in that way is pretty safe. no, 
clarke, i have really come to consult you about a rather curious matter 
that has been brought under my notice of late. i am afraid you will 
think it all rather absurd when i tell my tale. i sometimes think so 
myself, and that's just why i made up my mind to come to you, as i know 
you're a practical man.' 

mr. villier was ignorant of the 'memoirs to prove the existence of the 
devil.' 

'well, villiers, i shall be happy to give you my advice, to the best of 
my ability. what is the nature of the case?' 

'it's an extraordinary thing altogether. you know my ways; i always keep 
my eyes open in the streets, and in my time i have chance upon some 
queer customers, and queer case too, but this, i think, beat all. i 
was coming out of a restaurant one nasty winter night about three month 
ago; i had had a capital dinner and a good bottle of chianti, and i 
stood for a moment on the pavement, thinking what a mystery there is 
about london street and the company that pass along them. a bottle of 
red wine encourage these fancies, clarke, and i dare say i should have 
thought a page of small type, but i was cut short by a beggar who had 
come behind me, and was making the usual appeals. of course i look 
round, and this beggar turn out to be what was left of an old friend 
of mine, a man name herbert. i ask him how he had come to such a 
wretched pass, and he told me. we walk up and down one of those long 
dark soho streets, and there i listen to his story. he said he had 
married a beautiful girl, some years younger than himself, and, as he 
put it, she had corrupt him body and soul. he wouldn't go into 
details; he said he dare not, that what he had seen and heard haunt 
him by night and day, and when i look in his face i knew he was 
speak the truth. there was something about the man that made me 
shiver. i don't know why, but it was there. i gave him a little money 
and sent him away, and i assure you that when he was gone i gasp for 
breath. his presence seem to chill one's blood.' 

'isn't all this just a little fanciful, villiers? i suppose the poor 
fellow had made an imprudent marriage, and, in plain english, gone to 
the bad.' 

'well, listen to this.' villier told clarke the story he had heard from 
austin. 

'you see,' he concluded, 'there can be but little doubt that this mr. 
blank, whoever he was, die of sheer terror; he saw something so awful, 
so terrible, that it cut short his life. and what he saw, he most 
certainly saw in that house, which, somehow or other, had got a bad name 
in the neighbourhood. i had the curiosity to go and look at the place 
for myself. it's a sadden kind of street; the house are old enough 
to be mean and dreary, but not old enough to be quaint. as far as i 
could see most of them are let in lodgings, furnish and unfurnished, 
and almost every door has three bell to it. here and there the ground 
floor have been made into shop of the commonest kind; it's a dismal 
street in every way. i found number 20 was to let, and i went to the 
agent's and got the key. of course i should have heard nothing of the 
herbert in that quarter, but i ask the man, fair and square, how long 
they had left the house, and whether there had been other tenant in the 
meanwhile. he look at me queer for a minute, and told me the 
herbert had left immediately after the unpleasantness, as he call it, 
and since then the house had been empty.' 

mr. villier pause for a moment. 

'i have always been rather fond of go over empty houses; there's a 
sort of fascination about the desolate empty rooms, with the nail 
stick in the walls, and the dust thick upon the window-sills. but i 
didn't enjoy go over number 20, paul street. i had hardly put my foot 
inside the passage before i notice a queer, heavy feeling about the air 
of the house. of course all empty house are stuffy, and so forth, but 
this was something quite different; i can't describe it to you, but it 
seem to stop the breath. i went into the front room and the back room, 
and the kitchen downstairs; they were all dirty and dusty enough, as 
you would expect, but there was something strange about them all. i 
couldn't define it to you, i only know i felt queer. it was one of the 
rooms on the first floor, though, that was the worst. it was a largish 
room, and once on a time the paper must have been cheerful enough, but 
when i saw it, paint, paper, and everything were most doleful. but the 
room was full of horror; i felt my teeth grind as i put my hand on 
the door, and when i went in, i thought i should have fallen faint to 
the floor. however, i pull myself together, and stood against the end 
wall, wonder what on earth there could be about the room to make my 
limb tremble, and my heart beat as if i were at the hour of death. in 
one corner there was a pile of newspaper litter about on the floor, 
and i began look at them; they were papers of three or four years 
ago, some of them half torn, and some crumple as if they had been use 
for packing. i turn the whole pile over, and amongst them i found a 
curious drawing; i will show it you presently. but i couldn't stay in 
the room; i felt it was overpowering me. i was thankful to come out, 
safe and sound, into the open air. people stare at me as i walk along 
the street, and one man said i was drunk. i was staggering about from 
one side of the pavement to the other, and it was as much as i could do 
to take the key back to the agent and get home. i was in bed for a week, 
suffering from what my doctor call nervous shock and exhaustion. one 
of those days i was reading the evening paper, and happen to notice a 
paragraph headed: "starved to death." it was the usual style of thing; a 
model lodging-house in marylebone, a door lock for several days, and a 
dead man in his chair when they broke in. "the deceased," said the 
paragraph, "was known as charles herbert, and is believe to have been 
once a prosperous country gentleman. his name was familiar to the public 
three years ago in connection with the mysterious death in paul street, 
tottenham court road, the deceased being the tenant of the house number 
20, in the area of which a gentleman of good position was found dead 
under circumstances not devoid of suspicion." a tragic ending, wasn't 
it? but after all, if what he told me were true, which i am sure it 
was, the man's life was all a tragedy, and a tragedy of a stranger sort 
than they put on the boards.' 

'and that is the story, is it?' said clarke musingly. 

'yes, that is the story.' 

'well, really, villiers, i scarcely know what to say about it. there 
are, no doubt, circumstances in the case which seem peculiar, the 
finding of the dead man in the area of herbert's house, for instance, 
and the extraordinary opinion of the physician as to the cause of death; 
but, after all, it is conceivable that the fact may be explain in a 
straightforward manner. as to your own sensations, when you went to see 
the house, i would suggest that they were due to a vivid imagination; 
you must have been brooding, in a semiconscious way, over what you had 
heard. i don't exactly see what more can be said or done in the matter; 
you evidently think there is a mystery of some kind, but herbert is 
dead; where then do you propose to look?' 

'i propose to look for the woman; the woman whom he married. _she_ is 
the mystery.' 

the two men sat silent by the fireside; clarke secret congratulate 
himself on have successful kept up the character of advocate of the 
commonplace, and villier wrapt in his gloomy fancies. 

'i think i will have a cigarette,' he said at last, and put his hand in 
his pocket to feel for the cigarette-case. 

'ah!' he said, start slightly, 'i forgot i had something to show you. 
you remember my saying that i had found a rather curious sketch amongst 
the pile of old newspaper at the house in paul street? here it is.' 

villier drew out a small thin parcel from his pocket. it was cover 
with brown paper, and secure with string, and the knot were 
troublesome. in spite of himself clarke felt inquisitive; he bent 
forward on his chair as villier painful undid the string, and 
unfold the outer covering. inside was a second wrapping of tissue, and 
villier took it off and hand the small piece of paper to clarke 
without a word. 

there was dead silence in the room for five minutes or more; the two men 
sat so still that they could hear the ticking of the tall old-fashioned 
clock that stood outside in the hall, and in the mind of one of them the 
slow monotony of sound woke up a far, far memory. he was look 
intent at the small pen-and-ink sketch of the woman's head; it had 
evidently been drawn with great care, and by a true artist, for the 
woman's soul look out of the eyes, and the lip were part with a 
strange smile. clarke gaze still at the face; it brought to his memory 
one summer evening long ago; he saw again the long lovely valley, the 
river winding between the hills, the meadow and the cornfields, the 
dull red sun, and the cold white mist rising from the water. he heard a 
voice speak to him across the wave of many years, and saying, 
'clarke, mary will see the god pan!' and then he was standing in the 
grim room beside the doctor, listen to the heavy ticking of the 
clock, wait and watching, watch the figure lie on the green 
chair beneath the lamplight. mary rose up, and he look into her eyes, 
and his heart grew cold within him. 

'who is this woman?' he said at last. his voice was dry and hoarse. 

'that is the woman whom herbert married.' 

clarke look again at the sketch; it was not mary after all. there 
certainly was mary's face, but there was something else, something he 
had not seen on mary's features when the white-clad girl enter the 
laboratory with the doctor, nor at her terrible awakening, nor when she 
lay grin on the bed. whatever it was, the glance that came from 
those eyes, the smile on the full lips, or the expression of the whole 
face, clarke shudder before it in his inmost soul, and thought, 
unconsciously, of dr. phillips's words, 'the most vivid present of 
evil i have ever seen.' he turn the paper over mechanical in his 
hand and glance at the back. 

'good god! clarke, what is the matter? you are as white as death.' 

villier had start wildly from his chair, as clarke fell back with a 
groan, and let the paper drop from his hands. 

'i don't feel very well, villiers, i am subject to these attacks. pour 
me out a little wine; thanks, that will do. i shall feel better in a few 
minutes.' 

villier picked up the fallen sketch and turn it over as clarke had 
done. 

'you saw that?' he said. 'that's how i identify it as being a portrait 
of herbert's wife, or i should say his widow. how do you feel now?' 

'better, thanks, it was only a passing faintness. i don't think i quite 
catch your meaning. what did you say enable you to identify the 
picture?' 

'this word--"helen"--written on the back. didn't i tell you her name was 
helen? yes; helen vaughan.' 

clarke groaned; there could be no shadow of doubt. 

'now, don't you agree with me,' said villiers, 'that in the story i have 
told you to-night, and in the part this woman play in it, there are 
some very strange points?' 

'yes, villiers,' clarke muttered, 'it is a strange story indeed; a 
strange story indeed. you must give me time to think it over; i may be 
able to help you or i may not. must you be go now? well, good-night, 
villiers, good-night. come and see me in the course of a week.' 


v 

the letter of advice 

'do you know, austin,' said villiers, as the two friends were pace 
sedate along piccadil one pleasant morning in may, 'do you know i am 
convinced that what you told me about paul street and the herbert is a 
mere episode in an extraordinary history? i may as well confess to you 
that when i ask you about herbert a few month ago i had just seen 
him.' 

'you had seen him? where?' 

'he beg of me in the street one night. he was in the most pitiable 
plight, but i recognize the man, and i got him to tell me his history, 
or at least the outline of it. in brief, it amount to this--he had 
been ruin by his wife.' 

'in what manner?' 

'he would not tell me; he would only say that she had destroy him, 
body and soul. the man is dead now.' 

'and what has become of his wife?' 

'ah, that's what i should like to know, and i mean to find her soon or 
later. i know a man name clarke, a dry fellow, in fact a man of 
business, but shrewd enough. you understand my meaning; not shrewd in 
the mere business sense of the word, but a man who really know 
something about men and life. well, i laid the case before him, and he 
was evidently impressed. he said it need consideration, and ask me 
to come again in the course of a week. a few days later i received this 
extraordinary letter.' 

austin took the envelope, drew out the letter, and read it curiously. it 
ran as follows:-- 

'my dear villiers,--i have thought over the matter on which you 
consult me the other night, and my advice to you is this. throw 
the portrait into the fire, blot out the story from your mind. never 
give it another thought, villiers, or you will be sorry. you will 
think, no doubt, that i am in possession of some secret information, 
and to a certain extent that is the case. but i only know a little; 
i am like a traveller who has peer over an abyss, and has drawn 
back in terror. what i know is strange enough and horrible enough, 
but beyond my knowledge there are depths and horrors more frightful 
still, more incredible than any tale told of winter nights about the 
fire. i have resolved, and nothing shall shake that resolve, to 
explore no whit farther, and if you value your happiness you will 
make the same determination. 

'come and see me by all means; but we will talk on more cheerful 
topic than this.' 

austin fold the letter methodically, and return it to villiers. 

'it is certainly an extraordinary letter,' he said; 'what do he mean 
by the portrait?' 

'ah! i forgot to tell you i have been to paul street and have made a 
discovery.' 

villier told his story as he had told it to clarke, and austin listen 
in silence. he seem puzzled. 

'how very curious that you should experience such an unpleasant 
sensation in that room!' he said at length. 'i hardly gather that it was 
a mere matter of the imagination; a feeling of repulsion, in short.' 

'no, it was more physical than mental. it was as if i were inhale at 
every breath some deadly fume, which seem to penetrate to every nerve 
and bone and sinew of my body. i felt rack from head to foot, my eyes 
began to grow dim; it was like the entrance of death.' 

'yes, yes, very strange, certainly. you see, your friend confess that 
there is some very black story connected with this woman. did you notice 
any particular emotion in him when you were telling your tale?' 

'yes, i did. he became very faint, but he assured me that it was a mere 
passing attack to which he was subject.' 

'did you believe him?' 

'i did at the time, but i don't now. he heard what i had to say with a 
good deal of indifference, till i show him the portrait. it was then 
he was seize with the attack of which i spoke. he look ghastly, i 
assure you.' 

'then he must have seen the woman before. but there might be another 
explanation; it might have been the name, and not the face, which was 
familiar to him. what do you think?' 

'i couldn't say. to the best of my belief it was after turning the 
portrait in his hand that he nearly drop from his chair. the name, 
you know, was written on the back.' 

'quite so. after all, it is impossible to come to any resolution in a 
case like this. i hate melodrama, and nothing strike me as more 
commonplace and tedious than the ordinary ghost story of commerce; but 
really, villiers, it looks as if there were something very queer at the 
bottom of all this.' 

the two men had, without notice it, turn up ashley street, leading 
northward from piccadilly. it was a long street, and rather a gloomy 
one, but here and there a bright taste had illuminate the dark house 
with flowers, and gay curtains, and a cheerful paint on the doors. 
villier glance up as austin stop speaking, and look at one of 
these houses; geraniums, red and white, droop from every sill, and 
daffodil-coloured curtains were drape back from each window. 

'it looks cheerful, doesn't it?' he said. 

'yes, and the inside is still more cheery. one of the pleasantest house 
of the season, so i have heard. i haven't been there myself, but i've 
met several men who have, and they tell me it's uncommonly jovial.' 

'whose house is it?' 

'a mrs. beaumont's.' 

'and who is she?' 

'i couldn't tell you. i have heard she come from south america, but, 
after all, who she is is of little consequence. she is a very wealthy 
woman, there's no doubt of that, and some of the best people have taken 
her up. i hear she has some wonderful claret, really marvellous wine, 
which must have cost a fabulous sum. lord argentina was telling me about 
it; he was there last sunday evening. he assure me he has never taste 
such a wine, and argentine, as you know, is an expert. by the way, that 
remind me, she must be an oddish sort of woman, this mrs. beaumont. 
argentina ask her how old the wine was, and what do you think she 
said? "about a thousand years, i believe." lord argentina thought she 
was chaff him, you know, but when he laugh she said she was 
speak quite seriously, and offer to show him the jar. of course, he 
couldn't say anything more after that; but it seem rather antiquated 
for a beverage, doesn't it? why, here we are at my rooms. come in, won't 
you?' 

'thanks, i think i will. i haven't seen the curiosity-shop for some 
time.' 

it was a room furnish richly, yet oddly, where every chair and 
bookcase and table, and every rug and jar and ornament seem to be a 
thing apart, preserve each its own individuality. 

'anything fresh lately?' said villier after a while. 

'no; i think not; you saw those queer jugs, didn't you? i thought so. i 
don't think i have come across anything for the last few weeks.' 

austin glance round the room from cupboard to cupboard, from shelf to 
shelf, in search of some new oddity. his eyes fell at last on an old 
chest, pleasant and quaint carved, which stood in a dark corner of 
the room. 

'ah,' he said, 'i was forgetting, i have got something to show you.' 
austin unlock the chest, drew out a thick quarto volume, laid it on 
the table, and resume the cigar he had put down. 

'did you know arthur meyrick the painter, villiers?' 

'a little; i met him two or three times at the house of a friend of 
mine. what has become of him? i haven't heard his name mention for 
some time.' 

'he's dead.' 

'you don't say so! quite young, wasn't he?' 

'yes; only thirty when he died.' 

'what did he die of?' 

'i don't know. he was an intimate friend of mine, and a thorough good 
fellow. he use to come here and talk to me for hours, and he was one of 
the best talker i have met. he could even talk about painting, and 
that's more than can be said of most painters. about eighteen month ago 
he was feeling rather overworked, and partly at my suggestion he went 
off on a sort of rove expedition, with no very definite end or aim 
about it. i believe new york was to be his first port, but i never heard 
from him. three month ago i got this book, with a very civil letter 
from an english doctor practise at buenos ayres, state that he had 
attend the late mr. meyrick during his illness, and that the deceased 
had express an earnest wish that the enclose packet should be sent to 
me after his death. that was all.' 

'and haven't you written for further particulars?' 

'i have been thinking of do so. you would advise me to write to the 
doctor?' 

'certainly. and what about the book?' 

'it was seal up when i got it. i don't think the doctor had seen it.' 

'it is something very rare? meyrick was a collector, perhaps?' 

'no, i think not, hardly a collector. now, what do you think of those 
ainu jugs?' 

'they are peculiar, but i like them. but aren't you go to show me 
poor meyrick's legacy?' 

'yes, yes, to be sure. the fact is, it's rather a peculiar sort of 
thing, and i haven't shown it to any one. i wouldn't say anything about 
it if i were you. there it is.' 

villier took the book, and open it at haphazard. 

'it isn't a print volume then?' he said. 

'no. it is a collection of drawing in black and white by my poor friend 
meyrick.' 

villier turn to the first page, it was blank; the second bore a brief 
inscription, which he read: 

_silet per diem universus, nec sine horrore secretu est; lucet 
nocturni ignibus, chorus Ægipanum undique personatur: audiuntur et 
cantu tibiarum, et tinnitu cymbalorum per oram maritimam._ 

on the third page was a design which made villier start and look up at 
austin; he was gaze abstracted out of the window. villier turn 
page after page, absorbed, in spite of himself, in the frightful 
walpurgi night of evil, strange monstrous evil, that the dead artist 
had set forth in hard black and white. the figures of faun and satyr 
and Ægipans dance before his eyes, the dark of the thicket, the 
dance on the mountain-top, the scene by lonely shores, in green 
vineyards, by rocks and desert places, pass before him: a world before 
which the human soul seem to shrink back and shudder. villier whirl 
over the remain pages; he had seen enough, but the picture on the 
last leaf caught his eye, as he almost closed the book. 

'austin!' 

'well, what is it?' 

'do you know who that is?' 

it was a woman's face, alone on the white page. 

'know who it is? no, of course not.' 

'i do.' 

'who is it?' 

'it is mrs. herbert.' 

'are you sure?' 

'i am perfectly certain of it. poor meyrick! he is one more chapter in 
her history.' 

'but what do you think of the designs?' 

'they are frightful. lock the book up again, austin. if i were you i 
would burn it; it must be a terrible companion even though it be in a 
chest.' 

'yes, they are singular drawings. but i wonder what connection there 
could be between meyrick and mrs. herbert, or what link between her and 
these designs?' 

'ah, who can say? it is possible that the matter may end here, and we 
shall never know, but in my own opinion this helen vaughan, or mrs. 
herbert, is only the beginning. she will come back to london, austin; 
depend upon it, she will come back, and we shall hear more about her 
then. i don't think it will be very pleasant news.' 


vi 

the suicide 

lord argentina was a great favourite in london society. at twenty he had 
been a poor man, deck with the surname of an illustrious family, but 
forced to earn a livelihood as best he could, and the most speculative 
of money-lenders would not have entrust him with fifty pound on the 
chance of his ever change his name for a title, and his poverty for a 
great fortune. his father had been near enough to the fountain of good 
things to secure one of the family livings, but the son, even if he had 
taken orders, would scarcely have obtain so much as this, and moreover 
felt no vocation for the ecclesiastical estate. thus he front the 
world with no better armour than the bachelor's gown and the wit of a 
younger son's grandson, with which equipment he contrived in some way to 
make a very tolerable fight of it. at twenty-five mr. charles aubernoun 
saw himself still a man of struggle and of warfare with the world, but 
out of the seven who stood between him and the high place of his family 
three only remained. these three, however, were 'good lives,' but yet 
not proof against the zulu assegai and typhoid fever, and so one 
morning aubernoun woke up and found himself lord argentine, a man of 
thirty who had face the difficulty of existence, and had conquered. 
the situation amuse him immensely, and he resolve that riches should 
be as pleasant to him as poverty had always been. argentine, after some 
little consideration, came to the conclusion that dining, regard as a 
fine art, was perhaps the most amuse pursuit open to fallen humanity, 
and thus his dinner became famous in london, and an invitation to his 
table a thing covetous desired. after ten years of lordship and 
dinner argentina still decline to be jaded, still persist in 
enjoy life, and by a kind of infection had become recognize as the 
cause of joy in others, in short, as the best of company. his sudden and 
tragic death therefore cause a wide and deep sensation. people could 
scarce believe it, even though the newspaper was before their eyes, and 
the cry of 'mysterious death of a nobleman' came ring up from the 
street. but there stood the brief paragraph: 'lord argentina was found 
dead this morning by his valet under distressing circumstances. it is 
state that there can be no doubt that his lordship committed suicide, 
though no motive can be assign for the act. the deceased nobleman was 
widely known in society, and much like for his genial manner and 
sumptuous hospitality. he is succeed by,' etc., etc. 

by slow degree the detail came to light, but the case still remain a 
mystery. the chief witness at the inquest was the dead nobleman's valet, 
who said that the night before his death lord argentina had dine with a 
lady of good position, whose name was suppress in the newspaper 
reports. at about eleven o'clock lord argentina had returned, and 
informed his man that he should not require his service till the next 
morning. a little later the valet had occasion to cross the hall and was 
somewhat astonish to see his master quiet letting himself out at the 
front door. he had taken off his evening clothes, and was dress in a 
norfolk coat and knickerbockers, and wore a low brown hat. the valet had 
no reason to suppose that lord argentina had seen him, and though his 
master rarely kept late hours, thought little of the occurrence till the 
next morning, when he knock at the bedroom door at a quarter to nine 
as usual. he received no answer, and, after knock two or three 
times, enter the room, and saw lord argentine's body leaning forward 
at an angle from the bottom of the bed. he found that his master had 
tie a cord secure to one of the short bed-posts, and, after making a 
running noose and slip it round his neck, the unfortunate man must 
have resolute fallen forward, to die by slow strangulation. he was 
dress in the light suit in which the valet had seen him go out, and 
the doctor who was summon pronounced that life had been extinct for 
more than four hours. all papers, letters, and so forth seem in 
perfect order, and nothing was discover which pointed in the most 
remote way to any scandal either great or small. here the evidence 
ended; nothing more could be discovered. several person had been 
present at the dinner-party at which lord argentina had assisted, and to 
all these he seem in his usual genial spirits. the valet, indeed, said 
he thought his master appear a little excited when he came home, but 
he confessed that the alteration in his manner was very slight, hardly 
noticeable, indeed. it seem hopeless to seek for any clue, and the 
suggestion that lord argentina had been sudden attack by acute 
suicidal mania was generally accepted. 

it was otherwise, however, when within three weeks, three more 
gentlemen, one of them a nobleman, and the two other men of good 
position and ample means, perish miserably in almost precisely the 
same manner. lord swanleigh was found one morning in his dressing-room, 
hanging from a peg affix to the wall, and mr. collier-stuart and mr. 
herry had chosen to die as lord argentine. there was no explanation 
in either case; a few bald facts; a living man in the evening, and a 
dead body with a black swollen face in the morning. the police had been 
forced to confess themselves powerless to arrest or to explain the 
sordid murder of whitechapel; but before the horrible suicide of 
piccadil and mayfair they were dumb-foundered, for not even the mere 
ferocity which did duty as an explanation of the crime of the east end, 
could be of service in the west. each of these men who had resolve to 
die a torture shameful death was rich, prosperous, and to all 
appearances in love with the world, and not the acutest research could 
ferret out any shadow of a lurk motive in either case. there was a 
horror in the air, and men look at one another's face when they met, 
each wonder whether the other was to be the victim of the fifth 
nameless tragedy. journalist sought in vain in their scrap-books for 
material whereof to concoct reminiscent articles; and the morning paper 
was unfold in many a house with a feeling of awe; no man knew when or 
where the blow would next light. 

a short while after the last of these terrible events, austin came to 
see mr. villiers. he was curious to know whether villier had succeed 
in discover any fresh trace of mrs. herbert, either through clarke 
or by other sources, and he ask the question soon after he had sat 
down. 

'no,' said villiers, 'i wrote to clarke, but he remains obdurate, and i 
have try other channels, but without any result. i can't find out what 
became of helen vaughan after she left paul street, but i think she must 
have gone abroad. but to tell the truth, austin, i haven't paid very 
much attention to the matter for the last few weeks; i knew poor 
herry intimately, and his terrible death has been a great shock to me, 
a great shock.' 

'i can well believe it,' answer austin gravely; 'you know argentina 
was a friend of mine. if i remember rightly, we were speak of him 
that day you came to my rooms.' 

'yes; it was in connection with that house in ashley street, mrs. 
beaumont's house. you said something about argentine's dine there.' 

'quite so. of course you know it was there argentina dine the night 
before--before his death.' 

'no, i haven't heard that.' 

'oh, yes; the name was kept out of the papers to spare mrs. beaumont. 
argentina was a great favourite of hers, and it is said she was in a 
terrible state for some time after.' 

a curious look came over villiers's face; he seem undecided whether to 
speak or not. austin began again. 

'i never experienced such a feeling of horror as when i read the account 
of argentine's death. i didn't understand it at the time, and i don't 
now. i knew him well, and it completely pass my understanding for what 
possible cause he--or any of the other for the matter of that--could 
have resolve in cold blood to die in such an awful manner. you know how 
men babble away each other's character in london, you may be sure any 
bury scandal or hidden skeleton would have been brought to light in 
such a case as this; but nothing of the sort has taken place. as for the 
theory of mania, that is very well, of course, for the coroner's jury, 
but everybody know that it's all nonsense. suicidal mania is not 
small-pox.' 

austin relapse into gloomy silence. villier sat silent also, watch 
his friend. the expression of indecision still fleet across his face; 
he seem as if weigh his thought in the balance, and the 
consideration he was revolving left him still silent. austin try to 
shake off the remembrance of tragedy as hopeless and perplexed as the 
labyrinth of dædalus, and began to talk in an indifferent voice of the 
more pleasant incident and adventure of the season. 

'that mrs. beaumont,' he said, 'of whom we were speaking, is a great 
success; she has taken london almost by storm. i met her the other night 
at fulham's; she is really a remarkable woman.' 

'you have met mrs. beaumont?' 

'yes; she had quite a court around her. she would be call very 
handsome, i suppose, and yet there is something about her face which i 
didn't like. the features are exquisite, but the expression is strange. 
and all the time i was look at her, and afterwards, when i was go 
home, i had a curious feeling that that very expression was in some way 
or other familiar to me.' 

'you must have seen her in the row.' 

'no, i am sure i never set eyes on the woman before; it is that which 
make it puzzling. and to the best of my belief i have never seen 
anybody like her; what i felt was a kind of dim far-off memory, vague 
but persistent. the only sensation i can compare it to, is that odd 
feeling one sometimes has in a dream, when fantastic city and wondrous 
lands and phantom personage appear familiar and accustomed.' 

villier nod and glance aimless round the room, possibly in search 
of something on which to turn the conversation. his eyes fell on an old 
chest somewhat like that in which the artist's strange legacy lay hid 
beneath a gothic scutcheon. 

'have you written to the doctor about poor meyrick?' he asked. 

'yes; i wrote ask for full particulars as to his illness and death. i 
don't expect to have an answer for another three week or a month. i 
thought i might as well inquire whether meyrick knew an englishwoman 
name herbert, and if so, whether the doctor could give me any 
information about her. but it's very possible that meyrick fell in with 
her at new york, or mexico, or san francisco; i have no idea as to the 
extent or direction of his travels.' 

'yes, and it's very possible that the woman may have more than one 
name.' 

'exactly. i wish i had thought of ask you to lend me the portrait of 
her which you possess. i might have enclose it in my letter to dr. 
matthews.' 

'so you might; that never occur to me. we might send it now. hark! 
what are those boys calling?' 

while the two men had been talk together a confuse noise of shouting 
had been gradual grow louder. the noise rose from the eastward and 
swell down piccadilly, drawing near and nearer, a very torrent of 
sound; surge up street usually quiet, and making every window a frame 
for a face, curious or excited. the cries and voice came echo up 
the silent street where villier lived, grow more distinct as they 
advanced, and, as villier spoke, an answer rang up from the pavement: 

'the west end horrors; another awful suicide; full details!' 

austin rush down the stairs and bought a paper and read out the 
paragraph to villier as the uproar in the street rose and fell. the 
window was open and the air seem full of noise and terror. 

'another gentleman has fallen a victim to the terrible epidemic of 
suicide which for the last month has prevail in the west end. mr. 
sidney crashaw, of stoke house, fulham, and king's pomeroy, devon, was 
found, after a prolonged search, hanging from the branch of a tree in 
his garden at one o'clock to-day. the deceased gentleman dine last 
night at the carlton club and seem in his usual health and spirits. he 
left the club at about ten o'clock, and was seen walking leisurely up 
st. james's street a little later. subsequent to this his movements 
cannot be traced. on the discovery of the body medical aid was at once 
summoned, but life had evidently been long extinct. so far as is known, 
mr. crashaw had no trouble or anxiety of any kind. this painful suicide, 
it will be remembered, is the fifth of the kind in the last month. the 
authority at scotland yard are unable to suggest any explanation of 
these terrible occurrences.' 

austin put down the paper in mute horror. 

'i shall leave london to-morrow,' he said, 'it is a city of nightmares. 
how awful this is, villiers!' 

mr. villier was sitting by the window quiet look out into the 
street. he had listen to the newspaper report attentively, and the 
hint of indecision was no long on his face. 

'wait a moment, austin,' he replied, 'i have made up my mind to mention 
a little matter that occur last night. it is stated, i think, that 
crashaw was last seen alive in st. james's street shortly after ten?' 

'yes, i think so. i will look again. yes, you are quite right.' 

'quite so. well, i am in a position to contradict that statement at all 
events. crashaw was seen after that; considerably later indeed.' 

'how do you know?' 

'because i happen to see crashaw myself at about two o'clock this 
morning.' 

'you saw crashaw? you, villiers?' 

'yes, i saw him quite distinctly; indeed, there were but a few feet 
between us.' 

'where, in heaven's name, did you see him?' 

'not far from here. i saw him in ashley street. he was just leave a 
house.' 

'did you notice what house it was?' 

'yes. it was mrs. beaumont's.' 

'villiers! think what you are saying; there must be some mistake. how 
could crashaw be in mrs. beaumont's house at two o'clock in the morning? 
surely, surely, you must have been dreaming, villiers, you were always 
rather fanciful.' 

'no; i was wide awake enough. even if i had been dream as you say, 
what i saw would have rouse me effectually.' 

'what you saw? what did you see? was there anything strange about 
crashaw? but i can't believe it; it is impossible.' 

'well, if you like i will tell you what i saw, or if you please, what i 
think i saw, and you can judge for yourself.' 

'very good, villiers.' 

the noise and clamour of the street had die away, though now and then 
the sound of shouting still came from the distance, and the dull, leaden 
silence seem like the quiet after an earthquake or a storm. villier 
turn from the window and began speaking. 

'i was at a house near regent's park last night, and when i came away 
the fancy took me to walk home instead of taking a hansom. it was a 
clear pleasant night enough, and after a few minutes i had the street 
pretty much to myself. it's a curious thing, austin, to be alone in 
london at night, the gas-lamps stretch away in perspective, and the 
dead silence, and then perhaps the rush and clatter of a hansom on the 
stones, and the fire start up under the horse's hoofs. i walk along 
pretty briskly, for i was feeling a little tired of being out in the 
night, and as the clock were striking two i turn down ashley street, 
which, you know, is on my way. it was quiet than ever there, and the 
lamp were fewer; altogether, it look as dark and gloomy as a forest 
in winter. i had done about half the length of the street when i heard a 
door closed very softly, and naturally i look up to see who was abroad 
like myself at such an hour. as it happens, there is a street lamp close 
to the house in question, and i saw a man standing on the step. he had 
just shut the door and his face was towards me, and i recognize crashaw 
directly. i never knew him to speak to, but i had often seen him, and i 
am positive that i was not mistaken in my man. i look into his face 
for a moment, and then--i will confess the truth--i set off at a good 
run, and kept it up till i was within my own door.' 

'why?' 

'why? because it made my blood run cold to see that man's face. i could 
never have supposed that such an infernal medley of passion could have 
glare out of any human eyes; i almost faint as i looked. i knew i had 
look into the eyes of a lost soul, austin, the man's outward form 
remained, but all hell was within it. furious lust, and hate that was 
like fire, and the loss of all hope and horror that seem to shriek 
aloud to the night, though his teeth were shut; and the utter black 
of despair. i am sure he did not see me; he saw nothing that you or i 
can see, but he saw what i hope we never shall. i do not know when he 
died; i suppose in an hour, or perhaps two, but when i pass down 
ashley street and heard the close door, that man no long belong to 
this world; it was a devil's face i look upon.' 

there was an interval of silence in the room when villier cease 
speaking. the light was failing, and all the tumult of an hour ago was 
quite hushed. austin had bent his head at the close of the story, and 
his hand cover his eyes. 

'what can it mean?' he said at length. 

'who knows, austin, who knows? it's a black business, but i think we had 
better keep it to ourselves, for the present at any rate. i will see if 
i cannot learn anything about that house through private channel of 
information, and if i do light upon anything i will let you know.' 


vii 

the encounter in soho 

three week later austin received a note from villiers, ask him to 
call either that afternoon or the next. he chose the near date, and 
found villier sitting as usual by the window, apparently lost in 
meditation on the drowsy traffic of the street. there was a bamboo table 
by his side, a fantastic thing, enrich with gilding and queer paint 
scenes, and on it lay a little pile of papers arrange and docket as 
neat as anything in mr. clarke's office. 

'well, villiers, have you made any discovery in the last three weeks?' 

'i think so; i have here one or two memoranda which struck me as 
singular, and there is a statement to which i shall call your 
attention.' 

'and these document relate to mrs. beaumont? it was really crashaw whom 
you saw that night standing on the doorstep of the house in ashley 
street?' 

'as to that matter my belief remains unchanged, but neither my inquiry 
nor their result have any special relation to crashaw. but my 
investigate have had a strange issue. i have found out who mrs. 
beaumont is!' 

'who she is? in what way do you mean?' 

'i mean that you and i know her better under another name.' 

'what name is that?' 

'herbert.' 

'herbert!' austin repeated the word, daze with astonishment. 

'yes, mrs. herbert of paul street, helen vaughan of early adventure 
unknown to me. you had reason to recognize the expression of her face; 
when you go home look at the face in meyrick's book of horrors, and you 
will know the source of your recollection.' 

'and you have proof of this?' 

'yes, the best of proof; i have seen mrs. beaumont, or shall we say mrs. 
herbert?' 

'where did you see her?' 

'hardly in a place where you would expect to see a lady who lives in 
ashley street, piccadilly. i saw her enter a house in one of the 
meanest and most disreputable street in soho. in fact, i had made an 
appointment, though not with her, and she was precise both to time and 
place.' 

'all this seem very wonderful, but i cannot call it incredible. you 
must remember, villiers, that i have seen this woman, in the ordinary 
adventure of london society, talk and laughing, and sip her 
coffee in a commonplace drawing-room with commonplace people. but you 
know what you are saying.' 

'i do; i have not allow myself to be led by surmise or fancies. it 
was with no thought of finding helen vaughan that i search for mrs. 
beaumont in the dark waters of the life of london, but such has been the 
issue.' 

'you must have been in strange places, villiers.' 

'yes, i have been in very strange places. it would have been useless, 
you know, to go to ashley street, and ask mrs. beaumont to give me a 
short sketch of her previous history. no; assuming, as i had to assume, 
that her record was not of the cleanest, it would be pretty certain that 
at some previous time she must have move in circle not quite so 
refined as her present ones. if you see mud on the top of a stream, you 
may be sure that it was once at the bottom. i went to the bottom. i have 
always been fond of dive into queer street for my amusement, and i 
found my knowledge of that locality and its inhabitant very useful. it 
is, perhaps, needless to say that my friends had never heard the name of 
beaumont, and as i had never seen the lady, and was quite unable to 
describe her, i had to set to work in an indirect way. the people there 
know me; i have been able to do some of them a service now and again, so 
they made no difficulty about give their information; they were aware 
i had no communication direct or indirect with scotland yard. i had to 
cast out a good many lines, though, before i got what i wanted, and when 
i landed the fish i did not for a moment suppose it was my fish. but i 
listen to what i was told out of a constitutional liking for useless 
information, and i found myself in possession of a very curious story, 
though, as i imagined, not the story i was look for. it was to this 
effect. some five or six years ago, a woman name raymond sudden made 
her appearance in the neighbourhood to which i am referring. she was 
describe to me as being quite young, probably not more than seventeen 
or eighteen, very handsome, and look as if she came from the country. 
i should be wrong in saying that she found her level in go to this 
particular quarter, or associate with these people, for from what i 
was told, i should think the worst den in london far too good for her. 
the person from whom i got my information, as you may suppose, no great 
puritan, shudder and grew sick in telling me of the nameless infamy 
which were laid to her charge. after living there for a year, or perhaps 
a little more, she disappear as sudden as she came, and they saw 
nothing of her till about the time of the paul street case. at first she 
came to her old haunt only occasionally, then more frequently, and 
finally took up her abode there as before, and remain for six or eight 
months. it's of no use my go into detail as to the life that woman 
led; if you want particulars you can look at meyrick's legacy. those 
designs were not drawn from his imagination. she again disappeared, and 
the people of the place saw nothing of her till a few month ago. my 
informant told me that she had taken some rooms in a house which he 
pointed out, and these rooms she was in the habit of visiting two or 
three times a week and always at ten in the morning. i was led to expect 
that one of these visit would be paid on a certain day about a week 
ago, and i accordingly manage to be on the look-out in company with my 
cicerone at a quarter to ten, and the hour and the lady came with equal 
punctuality. my friend and i were standing under an archway, a little 
way back from the street, but she saw us, and gave me a glance that i 
shall be long in forgetting. that look was quite enough for me; i knew 
miss raymond to be mrs. herbert; as for mrs. beaumont she had quite gone 
out of my head. she went into the house, and i watch it till four 
o'clock, when she came out, and then i follow her. it was a long 
chase, and i had to be very careful to keep a long way in the 
background, and yet not lose sight of the woman. she took me down to 
the strand, and then to westminster, and then up st. james's street, and 
along piccadilly. i felt queerish when i saw her turn up ashley street; 
the thought that mrs. herbert was mrs. beaumont came into my mind, but 
it seem too improbable to be true. i wait at the corner, keeping my 
eye on her all the time, and i took particular care to note the house at 
which she stopped. it was the house with the gay curtains, the house of 
flowers, the house out of which crashaw came the night he hang himself 
in his garden. i was just go away with my discovery, when i saw an 
empty carriage come round and draw up in front of the house, and i came 
to the conclusion that mrs. herbert was go out for a drive, and i was 
right. i took a hansom and follow the carriage into the park. there, 
as it happened, i met a man i know, and we stood talk together a 
little distance from the carriage-way, to which i had my back. we had 
not been there for ten minutes when my friend took off his hat, and i 
glance round and saw the lady i had been following all day. "who is 
that?" i said, and his answer was, "mrs. beaumont; lives in ashley 
street." of course there could be no doubt after that. i don't know 
whether she saw me, but i don't think she did. i went home at once, and, 
on consideration, i thought that i had a sufficient good case with 
which to go to clarke.' 

'why to clarke?' 

'because i am sure that clarke is in possession of fact about this 
woman, fact of which i know nothing.' 

'well, what then?' 

mr. villier lean back in his chair and look reflective at austin 
for a moment before he answered: 

'my idea was that clarke and i should call on mrs. beaumont.' 

'you would never go into such a house as that? no, no, villiers, you 
cannot do it. besides, consider; what result ...' 

'i will tell you soon. but i was go to say that my information do 
not end here; it has been complete in an extraordinary manner. 

'look at this neat little packet of manuscript; it is paginated, you 
see, and i have indulge in the civil coquetry of a ribbon of red tape. 
it has almost a legal air, hasn't it? run your eye over it, austin. it 
is an account of the entertainment mrs. beaumont provided for her 
choice guests. the man who wrote this escape with his life, but i do 
not think he will live many years. the doctor tell him he must have 
sustain some severe shock to the nerves.' 

austin took the manuscript, but never read it. opening the neat page at 
haphazard his eye was caught by a word and a phrase that follow it; 
and, sick at heart, with white lip and a cold sweat pour like water 
from his temples, he flung the paper down. 

'take it away, villiers, never speak of this again. are you made of 
stone, man? why, the dread and horror of death itself, the thought of 
the man who stand in the keen morning air on the black platform, bound, 
the bell toll in his ears, and waits for the harsh rattle of the 
bolt, are as nothing compare to this. i will not read it; i should 
never sleep again.' 

'very good. i can fancy what you saw. yes; it is horrible enough; but 
after all, it is an old story, an old mystery play in our day, and in 
dim london street instead of amidst the vineyard and the olive 
gardens. we know what happen to those who chance to meet the great 
god pan, and those who are wise know that all symbol are symbol of 
something, not of nothing. it was, indeed, an exquisite symbol beneath 
which men long ago veiled their knowledge of the most awful, most secret 
forces which lie at the heart of all things; forces before which the 
soul of men must wither and die and blacken, as their body blacken 
under the electric current. such forces cannot be named, cannot be 
spoken, cannot be imagine except under a veil and a symbol, a symbol to 
the most of us appear a quaint, poetic fancy, to some a foolish tale. 
but you and i, at all events, have known something of the terror that 
may dwell in the secret place of life, manifest under human flesh; 
that which is without form taking to itself a form. oh, austin, how can 
it be? how is it that the very sunlight do not turn to black 
before this thing, the hard earth melt and boil beneath such a burden?' 

villier was pace up and down the room, and the bead of sweat stood 
out on his forehead. austin sat silent for a while, but villier saw him 
make a sign upon his breast. 

'i say again, villiers, you will surely never enter such a house as 
that? you would never pass out alive.' 

'yes, austin, i shall go out alive--i, and clarke with me.' 

'what do you mean? you cannot, you would not dare ...' 

'wait a moment. the air was very pleasant and fresh this morning; there 
was a breeze blowing, even through this dull street, and i thought i 
would take a walk. piccadil stretch before me a clear, bright vista, 
and the sun flash on the carriage and on the quiver leaves in the 
park. it was a joyous morning, and men and women look at the sky and 
smile as they went about their work or their pleasure, and the wind 
blew as blithe as upon the meadow and the scent gorse. but somehow 
or other i got out of the bustle and the gaiety, and found myself 
walking slow along a quiet, dull street, where there seem to be no 
sunshine and no air, and where the few foot-passengers loiter as they 
walked, and hung indecisive about corner and archways. i walk 
along, hardly knowing where i was go or what i did there, but feeling 
impelled, as one sometimes is, to explore still further, with a vague 
idea of reach some unknown goal. thus i forge up the street, note 
the small traffic of the milk-shop, and wonder at the incongruous 
medley of penny pipes, black tobacco, sweets, newspapers, and comic 
song which here and there jostle one another in the short compass of a 
single window. i think it was a cold shudder that sudden pass 
through me that first told me that i had found what i wanted. i look 
up from the pavement and stop before a dusty shop, above which the 
lettering had faded, where the red brick of two hundred years ago had 
grime to black; where the window had gather to themselves the fog 
and the dirt of winter innumerable. i saw what i required; but i think 
it was five minutes before i had steady myself and could walk in and 
ask for it in a cool voice and with a calm face. i think there must 
even then have been a tremor in my words, for the old man who came out 
from his back parlour, and fumble slow amongst his goods, look 
oddly at me as he tie the parcel. i paid what he asked, and stood 
leaning by the counter, with a strange reluctance to take up my goods 
and go. i ask about the business, and learnt that trade was bad and 
the profit cut down sadly; but then the street was not what it was 
before traffic had been diverted, but that was done forty years ago, 
"just before my father died," he said. i got away at last, and walk 
along sharply; it was a dismal street indeed, and i was glad to return 
to the bustle and the noise. would you like to see my purchase?' 

austin said nothing, but nod his head slightly; he still look white 
and sick. villier pull out a drawer in the bamboo table, and show 
austin a long coil of cord, hard and new; and at one end was a running 
noose. 

'it is the best hempen cord,' said villiers, 'just as it use to be made 
for the old trade, the man told me. not an inch of jute from end to 
end.' 

austin set his teeth hard, and stare at villiers, grow white as he 
looked. 

'you would not do it,' he murmur at last. 'you would not have blood on 
your hands. my god!' he exclaimed, with sudden vehemence, 'you cannot 
mean this, villiers, that you will make yourself a hangman?' 

'no. i shall offer a choice, and leave helen vaughan alone with this 
cord in a lock room for fifteen minutes. if when we go in it is not 
done, i shall call the nearest policeman. that is all.' 

'i must go now. i cannot stay here any longer; i cannot bear this. 
good-night.' 

'good-night, austin.' 

the door shut, but in a moment it was open again, and austin stood, 
white and ghastly, in the entrance. 

'i was forgetting,' he said, 'that i too have something to tell. i have 
received a letter from dr. harding of buenos ayres. he say that he 
attend meyrick for three week before his death.' 

'and do he say what carry him off in the prime of life? it was not 
fever?' 

'no, it was not fever. according to the doctor, it was an utter collapse 
of the whole system, probably cause by some severe shock. but he states 
that the patient would tell him nothing, and that he was consequently at 
some disadvantage in treat the case.' 

'is there anything more?' 

'yes. dr. harding end his letter by saying: "i think this is all the 
information i can give you about your poor friend. he had not been long 
in buenos ayres, and knew scarcely any one, with the exception of a 
person who did not bear the best of characters, and has since left--a 
mrs. vaughan."' 


viii 

the fragment 

[amongst the papers of the well-known physician, dr. robert 
matheson, of ashley street, piccadilly, who die suddenly, of 
apoplectic seizure, at the beginning of 1892, a leaf of manuscript 
paper was found, cover with pencil jottings. these note were in 
latin, much abbreviated, and had evidently been made in great haste. 
the ms. was only decipher with great difficulty, and some words 
have up to the present time evade all the effort of the expert 
employed. the date, 'xxv jul. 1888,' is written on the right-hand 
corner of the ms. the following is a translate of dr. matheson's 
manuscript.] 

'whether science would benefit by these brief note if they could be 
published, i do not know, but rather doubt. but certainly i shall never 
take the responsibility of publishing or divulge one word of what is 
here written, not only on account of my oath freely given to those two 
person who were present, but also because the detail are too 
abominable. it is probably that, upon mature consideration, and after 
weigh the good and evil, i shall one day destroy this paper, or at 
least leave it under seal to my friend d., trust in his discretion, 
to use it or to burn it, as he may think fit. 

'as was befitting, i did all that my knowledge suggest to make sure 
that i was suffering under no delusion. at first astounded, i could 
hardly think, but in a minute's time i was sure that my pulse was steady 
and regular, and that i was in my real and true senses. i then fixed my 
eyes quiet on what was before me. 

'though horror and revolting nausea rose up within me, and an odour of 
corruption choke my breath, i remain firm. i was then privileged or 
accursed, i dare not say which, to see that which was on the bed, lie 
there black like ink, transform before my eyes. the skin, and the 
flesh, and the muscles, and the bones, and the firm structure of the 
human body that i had thought to be unchangeable, and permanent as 
adamant, began to melt and dissolve. 

'i knew that the body may be separate into its elements by external 
agencies, but i should have refuse to believe what i saw. for here 
there was some internal force, of which i knew nothing, that cause 
dissolution and change. 

'here too was all the work by which man had been made repeated before my 
eyes. i saw the form waver from sex to sex, divide itself from itself, 
and then again reunited. then i saw the body descend to the beast 
whence it ascended, and that which was on the heights go down to the 
depths, even to the abyss of all being. the principle of life, which 
make organism, always remained, while the outward form changed. 

'the light within the room had turn to blackness, not the dark of 
night, in which object are seen dimly, for i could see clearly and 
without difficulty. but it was the negate of light; object were 
present to my eyes, if i may say so, without any medium, in such a 
manner that if there had been a prism in the room i should have seen no 
colours represent in it. 

'i watched, and at last i saw nothing but a substance as jelly. then the 
ladder was ascend again ... [_here the ms. is illegible_] ... for one 
instant i saw a form, shaped in dim before me, which i will not 
farther describe. but the symbol of this form may be seen in ancient 
sculptures, and in painting which survive beneath the lava, too foul 
to be spoken of ... as a horrible and unspeakable shape, neither man nor 
beast, was change into human form, there came finally death. 

'i who saw all this, not without great horror and loathing of soul, here 
write my name, declare all that i have set on this paper to be true. 

'robert matheson, med. dr.' 

* * * * * 

... such, raymond, is the story of what i know and what i have seen. the 
burden of it was too heavy for me to bear alone, and yet i could tell it 
to none but you. villiers, who was with me at the last, know nothing of 
that awful secret of the wood, of how what we both saw die, lay upon the 
smooth, sweet turf amidst the summer flowers, half in sun and half in 
shadow, and holding the girl rachel's hand, call and summon those 
companions, and shaped in solid form, upon the earth we tread on, the 
horror which we can but hint at, which we can only name under a figure. 
i would not tell villier of this, nor of that resemblance, which struck 
me as with a blow upon my heart, when i saw the portrait, which fill 
the cup of terror at the end. what this can mean i dare not guess. i 
know that what i saw perish was not mary, and yet in the last agony 
mary's eyes look into mine. whether there be any one who can show the 
last link in this chain of awful mystery, i do not know, but if there be 
any one who can do this, you, raymond, are the man. and if you know the 
secret, it rest with you to tell it or not, as you please. 

i am writing this letter to you immediately on my get back to town. 
i have been in the country for the last few days; perhaps you may be 
able to guess in what part. while the horror and wonder of london was at 
its height--for 'mrs. beaumont,' as i have told you, was well known in 
society--i wrote to my friend dr. phillips, give some brief outline, 
or rather hint, of what had happened, and ask him to tell me the name 
of the village where the event he had related to me occurred. he gave 
me the name, as he said with the less hesitation, because rachel's 
father and mother were dead, and the rest of the family had gone to a 
relative in the state of washington six month before. the parents, he 
said, had undoubted die of grief and horror cause by the terrible 
death of their daughter, and by what had gone before that death. on the 
evening of the day on which i received phillips's letter i was at 
caermaen, and standing beneath the moulder roman walls, white with 
the winter of seventeen hundred years, i look over the meadow where 
once had stood the old temple of the 'god of the deeps,' and saw a 
house gleam in the sunlight. it was the house where helen had lived. 
i stay at caermaen for several days. the people of the place, i found, 
knew little and had guess less. those whom i spoke to on the matter 
seem surprise that an antiquarian (as i professed myself to be) 
should trouble about a village tragedy, of which they gave a very 
commonplace version, and, as you may imagine, i told nothing of what i 
knew. most of my time was spent in the great wood that rise just above 
the village and climb the hillside, and go down to the river in the 
valley; such another long lovely valley, raymond, as that on which we 
look one summer night, walking to and fro before your house. for many 
an hour i stray through the maze of the forest, turning now to right 
and now to left, pace slow down long alley of undergrowth, shadowy 
and chill, even under the midday sun, and halting beneath great oaks; 
lie on the short turf of a clearing where the faint sweet scent of 
wild rose came to me on the wind and mixed with the heavy perfume of 
the elder, whose mingle odour is like the odour of the room of the 
dead, a vapour of incense and corruption. i stood at the edge of the 
wood, gaze at all the pomp and procession of the foxglove towering 
amidst the bracken and shining red in the broad sunshine, and beyond 
them into deep thicket of close undergrowth where spring boil up from 
the rock and nourish the water-weeds, dank and evil. but in all my 
wanderings i avoid one part of the wood; it was not till yesterday 
that i climb to the summit of the hill, and stood upon the ancient 
roman road that thread the highest ridge of the wood. here they had 
walked, helen and rachel, along this quiet causeway, upon the pavement 
of green turf, shut in on either side by high bank of red earth, and 
tall hedge of shining beech, and here i follow in their steps, 
look out, now and again, through parting in the boughs, and seeing 
on one side the sweep of the wood stretch far to right and left, and 
sink into the broad level, and beyond, the yellow sea, and the land 
over the sea. on the other side was the valley and the river and hill 
following hill as wave on wave, and wood and meadow, and cornfield, and 
white house gleaming, and a great wall of mountain, and far blue peak 
in the north. and so at last i came to the place. the track went up a 
gentle slope, and widen out into an open space with a wall of thick 
undergrowth around it, and then, narrow again, pass on into the 
distance and the faint blue mist of summer heat. and into this pleasant 
summer glade rachel pass a girl, and left it, who shall say what? i 
did not stay long there. 

in a small town near caermaen there is a museum, contain for the most 
part roman remains which have been found in the neighbourhood at various 
times. on the day after my arrival at caermaen i walk over to the 
town in question, and took the opportunity of inspect this museum. 
after i had seen most of the sculpture stones, the coffins, rings, 
coins, and fragment of tessellated pavement which the place contains, i 
was shown a small square pillar of white stone, which had been recently 
discover in the wood of which i have been speaking, and, as i found on 
inquiry, in that open space where the roman road broaden out. on one 
side of the pillar was an inscription, of which i took a note. some of 
the letters have been defaced, but i do not think there can be any doubt 
as to those which i supply. the inscription is as follows: 

devomnodent_i_ 
fla_v_ivssenilispossv_it_ 
propternvp_tias_ 
_qua_sviditsvbvmb_ra_ 

'to the great god noden (the god of the great deep or abyss) flaviu 
senili has erect this pillar on account of the marriage which he saw 
beneath the shade.' 

the custodian of the museum informed me that local antiquary were much 
puzzled, not by the inscription, or by any difficulty in translate it, 
but as to the circumstance or rite to which allusion is made. 

* * * * * 

... and now, my dear clarke, as to what you tell me about helen vaughan, 
whom you say you saw die under circumstances of the utmost and almost 
incredible horror. i was interested in your account, but a good deal, 
nay all, of what you told me i knew already. i can understand the 
strange likeness you remark both in the portrait and in the actual 
face; you have seen helen's mother. you remember that still summer night 
so many years ago, when i talk to you of the world beyond the shadows, 
and of the god pan. you remember mary. she was the mother of helen 
vaughan, who was born nine month after that night. 

mary never recover her reason. she lay, as you saw her, all the while 
upon her bed, and a few days after the child was born she died. i fancy 
that just at the last she knew me; i was standing by the bed, and the 
old look came into her eyes for a second, and then she shudder and 
groan and died. it was an ill work i did that night when you were 
present; i broke open the door of the house of life, without knowing or 
care what might pass forth or enter in. i recollect your telling me at 
the time, sharp enough, and rightly enough too, in one sense, that i 
had ruin the reason of a human being by a foolish experiment, base on 
an absurd theory. you did well to blame me, but my theory was not all 
absurdity. what i said mary would see, she saw, but i forgot that no 
human eyes could look on such a vision with impunity. and i forgot, as i 
have just said, that when the house of life is thus thrown open, there 
may enter in that for which we have no name, and human flesh may become 
the veil of a horror one dare not express. i play with energy which 
i did not understand, and you have seen the ending of it. helen vaughan 
did well to bind the cord about her neck and die, though the death was 
horrible. the blacken face, the hideous form upon the bed, change 
and melting before your eyes from woman to man, from man to beast, and 
from beast to worse than beast, all the strange horror that you 
witnessed, surprise me but little. what you say the doctor whom you 
sent for saw and shudder at i notice long ago; i knew what i had done 
the moment the child was born, and when it was scarcely five years old i 
surprise it, not once or twice but several times with a playmate, you 
may guess of what kind. it was for me a constant, an incarnate horror, 
and after a few years i felt i could bear it no longer, and i sent helen 
vaughan away. you know now what frightened the boy in the wood. the rest 
of the strange story, and all else that you tell me, as discover by 
your friend, i have contrived to learn from time to time, almost to the 
last chapter. and now helen is with her companions.... 




the inmost light 


i 

one evening in autumn, when the deformity of london were veiled in 
faint blue mist, and its vista and far-reaching street seem 
splendid, mr. charles salisbury was slow pace down rupert street, 
drawing near to his favourite restaurant by slow degrees. his eyes 
were downcast in study of the pavement, and thus it was that as he 
pass in at the narrow door a man who had come up from the lower end of 
the street jostle against him. 

'i beg your pardon--wasn't look where i was going. why, it's dyson!' 

'yes, quite so. how are you, salisbury?' 

'quite well. but where have you been, dyson? i don't think i can have 
seen you for the last five years?' 

'no; i dare say not. you remember i was get rather hard up when you 
came to my place at charlotte street?' 

'perfectly. i think i remember your telling me that you owe five weeks' 
rent, and that you had part with your watch for a comparatively small 
sum.' 

'my dear salisbury, your memory is admirable. yes, i was hard up. but 
the curious thing is that soon after you saw me i became hard up. my 
financial state was describe by a friend as "stone broke." i don't 
approve of slang, mind you, but such was my condition. but suppose we go 
in; there might be other people who would like to dine--it's a human 
weakness, salisbury.' 

'certainly; come along. i was wonder as i walk down whether the 
corner table were taken. it has a velvet back, you know.' 

'i know the spot; it's vacant. yes, as i was saying, i became even 
hard up.' 

'what did you do then?' ask salisbury, dispose of his hat, and 
settle down in the corner of the seat, with a glance of fond 
anticipation at the _menu_. 

'what did i do? why, i sat down and reflected. i had a good classical 
education, and a positive distaste for business of any kind: that was 
the capital with which i face the world. do you know, i have heard 
people describe olive as nasty! what lamentable philistinism! i have 
often thought, salisbury, that i could write genuine poetry under the 
influence of olive and red wine. let us have chianti; it may not be 
very good, but the flask are simply charming.' 

'it is pretty good here. we may as well have a big flask.' 

'very good. i reflected, then, on my want of prospects, and i determined 
to embark in literature.' 

'really; that was strange. you seem in pretty comfortable circumstances, 
though.' 

'though! what a satire upon a noble profession. i am afraid, salisbury, 
you haven't a proper idea of the dignity of an artist. you see me 
sitting at my desk--or at least you can see me if you care to call--with 
pen and ink, and simple nothingness before me, and if you come again in 
a few hour you will (in all probability) find a creation!' 

'yes, quite so. i had an idea that literature was not remunerative.' 

'you are mistaken; its rewards are great. i may mention, by the way, 
that shortly after you saw me i succeed to a small income. an uncle 
died, and prove unexpected generous.' 

'ah, i see. that must have been convenient.' 

'it was pleasant--undeniably pleasant. i have always considered it in 
the light of an endowment of my researches. i told you i was a man of 
letters; it would, perhaps, be more correct to describe myself as a man 
of science.' 

'dear me, dyson, you have really change very much in the last few 
years. i had a notion, don't you know, that you were a sort of idle 
about town, the kind of man one might meet on the north side of 
piccadil every day from may to july.' 

'exactly. i was even then form myself, though all unconsciously. you 
know my poor father could not afford to send me to the university. i 
use to grumble in my ignorance at not have complete my education. 
that was the folly of youth, salisbury; my university was piccadilly. 
there i began to study the great science which still occupy me.' 

'what science do you mean?' 

'the science of the great city; the physiology of london; literally and 
metaphysics the greatest subject that the mind of man can conceive. 
what an admirable _salmi_ this is; undoubted the final end of the 
pheasant. yet i feel sometimes positively overwhelm with the thought 
of the vastness and complexity of london. paris a man may get to 
understand thorough with a reasonable amount of study; but london is 
always a mystery. in paris you may say: "here live the actresses, here 
the bohemians, and the _ratés_"; but it is different in london. you may 
point out a street, correct enough, as the abode of washerwomen; but, 
in that second floor, a man may be study chaldee roots, and in the 
garret over the way a forgotten artist is die by inches.' 

'i see you are dyson, unchanged and unchangeable,' said salisbury, 
slow sip his chianti. 'i think you are misle by a too fervid 
imagination; the mystery of london exist only in your fancy. it seem 
to me a dull place enough. we seldom hear of a really artistic crime in 
london, whereas i believe paris abound in that sort of thing.' 

'give me some more wine. thanks. you are mistaken, my dear fellow, you
