are really mistaken. london has nothing to be ashamed of in the way of 
crime. where we fail is for want of homers, not agamemnons. _carent quia 
vate sacro_, you know.' 

'i recall the quotation. but i don't think i quite follow you.' 

'well, in plain language, we have no good writer in london who make a 
speciality of that kind of thing. our common reporter is a dull dog; 
every story that he has to tell is spoilt in the telling. his idea of 
horror and of what excite horror is so lamentable deficient. nothing 
will content the fellow but blood, vulgar red blood, and when he can get 
it he lay it on thick, and consider that he has produce a telling 
article. it's a poor notion. and, by some curious fatality, it is the 
most commonplace and brutal murder which always attract the most 
attention and get written up the most. for instance, i dare say that 
you never heard of the harlesden case?' 

'no; no, i don't remember anything about it.' 

'of course not. and yet the story is a curious one. i will tell it you 
over our coffee. harlesden, you know, or i expect you don't know, is 
quite on the out-quarters of london; something curious different from 
your fine old crust suburb like norwood or hampstead, different as 
each of these is from the other. hampstead, i mean, is where you look 
for the head of your great china house with his three acre of land and 
pine-houses, though of late there is the artistic substratum; while 
norwood is the home of the prosperous middle-class family who took the 
house "because it was near the palace," and sicken of the palace six 
month afterwards; but harlesden is a place of no character. it's too 
new to have any character as yet. there are the row of red house and 
the row of white house and the bright green venetians, and the 
blistering doorways, and the little backyard they call gardens, and a 
few feeble shops, and then, just as you think you're go to grasp the 
physiognomy of the settlement, it all melt away.' 

'how the dickens is that? the house don't tumble down before one's 
eyes, i suppose!' 

'well, no, not exactly that. but harlesden as an entity disappears. your 
street turn into a quiet lane, and your staring house into elm trees, 
and the back-gardens into green meadows. you pass instantly from town to 
country; there is no transition as in a small country town, no soft 
gradation of wide lawn and orchards, with house gradual becoming 
less dense, but a dead stop. i believe the people who live there mostly 
go into the city. i have seen once or twice a laden 'bus bound 
thitherwards. but however that may be, i can't conceive a greater 
lonely in a desert at midnight than there is there at midday. it is 
like a city of the dead; the street are glaring and desolate, and as 
you pass it sudden strike you that this too is part of london. well, 
a year or two ago there was a doctor living there; he had set up his 
brass plate and his red lamp at the very end of one of those shining 
streets, and from the back of the house, the field stretch away to 
the north. i don't know what his reason was in settle down in such an 
out-of-the-way place, perhaps dr. black, as we will call him, was a 
far-seeing man and look ahead. his relations, so it appear 
afterwards, had lost sight of him for many years and didn't even know he 
was a doctor, much less where he lived. however, there he was settled in 
harlesden, with some fragment of a practice, and an uncommonly pretty 
wife. people use to see them walking out together in the summer 
evenings soon after they came to harlesden, and, so far as could be 
observed, they seem a very affectionate couple. these walk went on 
through the autumn, and then ceased; but, of course, as the days grew 
dark and the weather cold, the lane near harlesden might be expect to 
lose many of their attractions. all through the winter nobody saw 
anything of mrs. black; the doctor use to reply to his patients' 
inquiry that she was a "little out of sorts, would be better, no 
doubt, in the spring." but the spring came, and the summer, and no mrs. 
black appeared, and at last people began to rumour and talk amongst 
themselves, and all sort of queer things were said at "high teas," 
which you may possibly have heard are the only form of entertainment 
known in such suburbs. dr. black began to surprise some very odd looks 
cast in his direction, and the practice, such as it was, fell off before 
his eyes. in short, when the neighbour whisper about the matter, they 
whisper that mrs. black was dead, and that the doctor had made away 
with her. but this wasn't the case; mrs. black was seen alive in june. 
it was a sunday afternoon, one of those few exquisite days that an 
english climate offers, and half london had stray out into the fields, 
north, south, east, and west to smell the scent of the white may, and to 
see if the wild rose were yet in blossom in the hedges. i had gone out 
myself early in the morning, and had had a long ramble, and somehow or 
other as i was steer homeward i found myself in this very harlesden 
we have been talk about. to be exact, i had a glass of beer in the 
"general gordon," the most flourish house in the neighbourhood, and 
as i was wandering rather aimless about, i saw an uncommonly tempt 
gap in a hedgerow, and resolve to explore the meadow beyond. soft grass 
is very grateful to the feet after the infernal grit strewn on suburban 
sidewalks, and after walking about for some time i thought i should like 
to sit down on a bank and have a smoke. while i was get out my 
pouch, i look up in the direction of the houses, and as i look i 
felt my breath caught back, and my teeth began to chatter, and the stick 
i had in one hand snap in two with the grip i gave it. it was as if i 
had had an electric current down my spine, and yet for some moment of 
time which seem long, but which must have been very short, i caught 
myself wonder what on earth was the matter. then i knew what had 
made my very heart shudder and my bone grind together in an agony. as i 
glance up i had look straight towards the last house in the row 
before me, and in an upper window of that house i had seen for some 
short fraction of a second a face. it was the face of a woman, and yet 
it was not human. you and i, salisbury, have heard in our time, as we 
sat in our seat in church in sober english fashion, of a lust that 
cannot be satiate and of a fire that is unquenchable, but few of us 
have any notion what these words mean. i hope you never may, for as i 
saw that face at the window, with the blue sky above me and the warm air 
play in gust about me, i knew i had look into another 
world--looked through the window of a commonplace, brand-new house, and 
seen hell open before me. when the first shock was over, i thought once 
or twice that i should have fainted; my face stream with a cold sweat, 
and my breath came and went in sobs, as if i had been half drowned. i 
manage to get up at last, and walk round to the street, and there i 
saw the name "dr. black" on the post by the front gate. as fate or my 
luck would have it, the door open and a man came down the steps as i 
pass by. i had no doubt it was the doctor himself. he was of a type 
rather common in london; long and thin, with a pasty face and a dull 
black moustache. he gave me a look as we pass each other on the 
pavement, and though it was merely the casual glance which one 
foot-passenger bestow on another, i felt convinced in my mind that here 
was an ugly customer to deal with. as you may imagine, i went my way a 
good deal puzzle and horrify too by what i had seen; for i had paid 
another visit to the "general gordon," and had got together a good deal 
of the common gossip of the place about the blacks. i didn't mention the 
fact that i had seen a woman's face in the window; but i heard that mrs. 
black had been much admire for her beautiful golden hair, and round 
what had struck me with such a nameless terror, there was a mist of 
flowing yellow hair, as it were an aureole of glory round the visage of 
a satyr. the whole thing bother me in an indescribable manner; and 
when i got home i try my best to think of the impression i had 
received as an illusion, but it was no use. i knew very well i had seen 
what i have try to describe to you, and i was morally certain that i 
had seen mrs. black. and then there was the gossip of the place, the 
suspicion of foul play, which i knew to be false, and my own conviction 
that there was some deadly mischief or other go on in that bright red 
house at the corner of devon road: how to construct a theory of a 
reasonable kind out of these two elements. in short, i found myself in a 
world of mystery; i puzzle my head over it and fill up my leisure 
moments by gathering together odd thread of speculation, but i never 
move a step towards any real solution, and as the summer days went on 
the matter seem to grow misty and indistinct, shadow some vague 
terror, like a nightmare of last month. i suppose it would before long 
have fade into the background of my brain--i should not have forgotten 
it, for such a thing could never be forgotten--but one morning as i was 
look over the paper my eye was caught by a heading over some two 
dozen lines of small type. the words i had seen were simply, "the 
harlesden case," and i knew what i was go to read. mrs. black was 
dead. black had call in another medical man to certify as to cause of 
death, and something or other had arouse the strange doctor's 
suspicion and there had been an inquest and _post-mortem_. and the 
result? that, i will confess, did astonish me considerably; it was the 
triumph of the unexpected. the two doctor who made the autopsy were 
oblige to confess that they could not discover the faintest trace of 
any kind of foul play; their most exquisite test and reagent fail to 
detect the presence of poison in the most infinitesimal quantity. death, 
they found, had been cause by a somewhat obscure and scientific 
interesting form of brain disease. the tissue of the brain and the 
molecule of the grey matter had undergone a most extraordinary series 
of changes; and the younger of the two doctors, who has some reputation, 
i believe, as a specialist in brain trouble, made some remark in give 
his evidence which struck me deep at the time, though i did not then 
grasp their full significance. he said: "at the commencement of the 
examination i was astonish to find appearances of a character entire 
new to me, notwithstanding my somewhat large experience. i need not 
specify these appearances at present, it will be sufficient for me to 
state that as i proceed in my task i could scarcely believe that the 
brain before me was that of a human being at all." there was some 
surprise at this statement, as you may imagine, and the coroner ask 
the doctor if he meant to say that the brain resemble that of an 
animal. "no," he replied, "i should not put it in that way. some of the 
appearances i notice seem to point in that direction, but others, and 
these were the more surprising, indicate a nervous organization of a 
wholly different character from that either of man or the lower 
animals." it was a curious thing to say, but of course the jury brought 
in a verdict of death from natural causes, and, so far as the public was 
concerned, the case came to an end. but after i had read what the doctor 
said i made up my mind that i should like to know a good deal more, and 
i set to work on what seem likely to prove an interesting 
investigation. i had really a good deal of trouble, but i was successful 
in a measure. though why--my dear fellow, i had no notion at the time. 
are you aware that we have been here nearly four hours? the waiter are 
staring at us. let's have the bill and be gone.' 

the two men went out in silence, and stood a moment in the cool air, 
watch the hurry traffic of coventry street pass before them to the 
accompaniment of the ring bell of hansom and the cries of the 
newsboys; the deep far murmur of london surge up ever and again from 
beneath these loud noises. 

'it is a strange case, isn't it?' said dyson at length. 'what do you 
think of it?' 

'my dear fellow, i haven't heard the end, so i will reserve my opinion. 
when will you give me the sequel?' 

'come to my rooms some evening; say next thursday. here's the address. 
good-night; i want to get down to the strand.' dyson hail a passing 
hansom, and salisbury turn northward to walk home to his lodgings. 


ii 

mr. salisbury, as may have been gather from the few remark which he 
had found it possible to introduce in the course of the evening, was a 
young gentleman of a peculiarly solid form of intellect, coy and 
retiring before the mysterious and the uncommon, with a constitutional 
dislike of paradox. during the restaurant dinner he had been forced to 
listen in almost absolute silence to a strange tissue of improbability 
strung together with the ingenuity of a born meddle in plot and 
mysteries, and it was with a feeling of weary that he cross 
shaftesbury avenue, and dive into the recess of soho, for his 
lodgings were in a modest neighbourhood to the north of oxford street. 
as he walk he speculate on the probable fate of dyson, rely on 
literature, unbefriended by a thoughtful relative, and could not help 
conclude that so much subtlety united to a too vivid imagination would 
in all likelihood have been reward with a pair of sandwich-boards or a 
super's banner. absorb in this train of thought, and admire the 
perverse dexterity which could transmute the face of a sickly woman and 
a case of brain disease into the crude elements of romance, salisbury 
stray on through the dimly-lighted streets, not notice the gusty 
wind which drove sharp round corner and whirl the stray rubbish of 
the pavement into the air in eddies, while black cloud gather over 
the sickly yellow moon. even a stray drop or two of rain blown into his 
face did not rouse him from his meditations, and it was only when with 
a sudden rush the storm tore down upon the street that he began to 
consider the expediency of finding some shelter. the rain, driven by the 
wind, pelt down with the violence of a thunderstorm, dashing up from 
the stone and hiss through the air, and soon a perfect torrent of 
water course along the kennels and accumulate in pools over the 
choked-up drains. the few stray passenger who had been loaf rather 
than walking about the street had scuttere away, like frightened 
rabbits, to some invisible place of refuge, and though salisbury 
whistle loud and long for a hansom, no hansom appeared. he look about 
him, as if to discover how far he might be from the haven of oxford 
street, but strolling careless along, he had turn out of his way, 
and found himself in an unknown region, and one to all appearance devoid 
even of a public-house where shelter could be bought for the modest sum 
of twopence. the street lamp were few and at long intervals, and burn 
behind grimy glasses with the sickly light of oil, and by this waver 
glimmer salisbury could make out the shadowy and vast old house of 
which the street was composed. as he pass along, hurrying, and 
shrink from the full sweep of the rain, he notice the innumerable 
bell-handles, with name that seem about to vanish of old age graven 
on brass plate beneath them, and here and there a richly carve 
penthouse overhung the door, blacken with the grime of fifty years. 
the storm seem to grow more and more furious; he was wet through, and 
a new hat had become a ruin, and still oxford street seem as far off 
as ever; it was with deep relief that the dripping man caught sight of a 
dark archway which seem to promise shelter from the rain if not from 
the wind. salisbury took up his position in the driest corner and look 
about him; he was standing in a kind of passage contrived under part of 
a house, and behind him stretch a narrow footway leading between blank 
wall to regions unknown. he had stood there for some time, vain 
endeavour to rid himself of some of his superfluous moisture, and 
listen for the passing wheel of a hansom, when his attention was 
arouse by a loud noise coming from the direction of the passage behind, 
and grow loud as it drew nearer. in a couple of minutes he could 
make out the shrill, raucous voice of a woman, threaten and 
renouncing, and making the very stone echo with her accents, while now 
and then a man grumble and expostulated. though to all appearance 
devoid of romance, salisbury had some relish for street rows, and was, 
indeed, somewhat of an amateur in the more amuse phase of 
drunkenness; he therefore compose himself to listen and observe with 
something of the air of a subscriber to grand opera. to his annoyance, 
however, the tempest seem sudden to be composed, and he could hear 
nothing but the impatient steps of the woman and the slow lurch of the 
man as they came towards him. keeping back in the shadow of the wall, he 
could see the two drawing nearer; the man was evidently drunk, and had 
much ado to avoid frequent collision with the wall as he tack across 
from one side to the other, like some bark beating up against a wind. 
the woman was look straight in front of her, with tear stream 
from her eyes, but sudden as they went by the flame blaze up again, 
and she burst forth into a torrent of abuse, facing round upon her 
companion. 

'you low rascal, you mean, contemptible cur,' she went on, after an 
incoherent storm of curses, 'you think i'm to work and slave for you 
always, i suppose, while you're after that green street girl and 
drink every penny you've got? but you're mistaken, sam--indeed, i'll 
bear it no longer. damn you, you dirty thief, i've done with you and 
your master too, so you can go your own errands, and i only hope they'll 
get you into trouble.' 

the woman tore at the bosom of her dress, and taking something out that 
look like paper, crumple it up and flung it away. it fell at 
salisbury's feet. she ran out and disappear in the darkness, while the 
man lurch slow into the street, grumbling indistinct to himself in 
a perplexed tone of voice. salisbury look out after him and saw him 
maunder along the pavement, halting now and then and sway 
indecisively, and then start off at some fresh tangent. the sky had 
cleared, and white fleecy cloud were fleeting across the moon, high in 
the heaven. the light came and went by turns, as the cloud pass by, 
and, turning round as the clear, white ray shone into the passage, 
salisbury saw the little ball of crumple paper which the woman had cast 
down. oddly curious to know what it might contain, he picked it up and 
put it in his pocket, and set out afresh on his journey. 


iii 

salisbury was a man of habit. when he got home, drench to the skin, 
his clothes hanging lank about him, and a ghastly dew besmear his 
hat, his only thought was of his health, of which he took studious care. 
so, after change his clothes and encase himself in a warm 
dressing-gown, he proceed to prepare a sudorific in the shape of a hot 
gin and water, warm the latter over one of those spirit-lamps which 
mitigate the austerity of the modern hermit's life. by the time this 
preparation had been exhibited, and salisbury's disturbed feelings had 
been soothe by a pipe of tobacco, he was able to get into bed in a 
happy state of vacancy, without a thought of his adventure in the dark 
archway, or of the weird fancies with which dyson had season his 
dinner. it was the same at breakfast the next morning, for salisbury 
made a point of not thinking of any thing until that meal was over; but 
when the cup and saucer were clear away, and the morning pipe was lit, 
he remember the little ball of paper, and began fumble in the 
pocket of his wet coat. he did not remember into which pocket he had 
put it, and as he dive now into one and now into another, he 
experienced a strange feeling of apprehension lest it should not be 
there at all, though he could not for the life of him have explain the 
importance he attach to what was in all probability mere rubbish. but 
he sigh with relief when his finger touched the crumple surface in 
an inside pocket, and he drew it out gently and laid it on the little 
desk by his easy-chair with as much care as if it had been some rare 
jewel. salisbury sat smoking and staring at his find for a few minutes, 
an odd temptation to throw the thing in the fire and have done with it 
struggle with as odd a speculation as to its possible contents, and as 
to the reason why the infuriate woman should have flung a bit of paper 
from her with such vehemence. as might be expected, it was the latter 
feeling that conquer in the end, and yet it was with something like 
repugnance that he at last took the paper and unroll it, and laid it 
out before him. it was a piece of common dirty paper, to all appearance 
torn out of a cheap exercise-book, and in the middle were a few lines 
written in a queer cramped hand. salisbury bent his head and stare 
eager at it for a moment, drawing a long breath, and then fell back in 
his chair gaze blank before him, till at last with a sudden 
revulsion he burst into a peal of laughter, so long and loud and 
uproarious that the landlady's baby on the floor below awoke from sleep 
and echo his mirth with hideous yells. but he laugh again and again, 
and took the paper up to read a second time what seem such meaningless 
nonsense. 

'q. has had to go and see his friends in paris,' it began. 'traverse 
handle s. "once around the grass, and twice around the lass, and 
thrice around the maple tree."' 

salisbury took up the paper and crumple it as the angry woman had done, 
and aim it at the fire. he did not throw it there, however, but 
toss it careless into the well of the desk, and laugh again. the 
sheer folly of the thing offend him, and he was ashamed of his own 
eager speculation, as one who pore over the high-sounding announcement 
in the agony column of the daily paper, and find nothing but 
advertisement and triviality. he walk to the window, and stare out at 
the languid morning life of his quarter; the maid in slattern print 
dress washing door-steps, the fish-monger and the butcher on their 
rounds, and the tradesmen standing at the door of their small shops, 
droop for lack of trade and excitement. in the distance a blue haze 
gave some grandeur to the prospect, but the view as a whole was 
depressing, and would only have interested a student of the life of 
london, who find something rare and choice in its very aspect. 
salisbury turn away in disgust, and settled himself in the easy-chair, 
upholster in a bright shade of green, and deck with yellow gimp, 
which was the pride and attraction of the apartments. here he compose 
himself to his morning's occupation--the peruse of a novel that dealt 
with sport and love in a manner that suggest the collaboration of a 
stud-groom and a ladies' college. in an ordinary way, however, salisbury 
would have been carry on by the interest of the story up to 
lunch-time, but this morning he fidget in and out of his chair, took 
the book up and laid it down again, and swore at last to himself and at 
himself in mere irritation. in point of fact the jingle of the paper 
found in the archway had 'got into his head,' and do what he would he 
could not help mutter over and over, 'once around the grass, and 
twice around the lass, and thrice around the maple tree.' it became a 
positive pain, like the foolish burden of a music-hall song, 
everlastingly quoted, and sung at all hour of the day and night, and 
treasure by the street-boys as an unfailing resource for six month 
together. he went out into the streets, and try to forget his enemy in 
the jostle of the crowd and the roar and clatter of the traffic, but 
presently he would find himself steal quiet aside, and pace some 
desert byway, vain puzzle his brains, and try to fix some 
meaning to phrase that were meaningless. it was a positive relief when 
thursday came, and he remember that he had made an appointment to go 
and see dyson; the flimsy reverie of the self-styled man of letters 
appear entertaining when compare with this ceaseless iteration, this 
maze of thought from which there seem no possibility of escape. 
dyson's abode was in one of the quietest of the quiet street that led 
down from the strand to the river, and when salisbury pass from the 
narrow stairway into his friend's room, he saw that the uncle had been 
beneficent indeed. the floor glow and flame with all the colours of 
the east; it was, as dyson pompous remarked, 'a sunset in a dream,' 
and the lamplight, the twilight of london streets, was shut out with 
strange work curtains, glittering here and there with thread of 
gold. in the shelves of an oak _armoire_ stood jar and plate of old 
france china, and the black and white of etching not to be found in the 
haymarket or in bond street, stood out against the splendour of a 
japan paper. salisbury sat down on the settle by the hearth, and 
sniff the mingle fumes of incense and tobacco, wonder and dumb 
before all this splendour after the green rep and the oleographs, the 
gilt-framed mirror, and the lustre of his own apartment. 

'i am glad you have come,' said dyson. 'comfortable little room, isn't 
it? but you don't look very well, salisbury. nothing disagree with you, 
has it?' 

'no; but i have been a good deal bother for the last few days. the 
fact is i had an odd kind of--of--adventure, i suppose i may call it, 
that night i saw you, and it has worried me a good deal. and the 
provoking part of it is that it's the merest nonsense--but, however, i 
will tell you all about it, by and by. you were go to let me have the 
rest of that odd story you began at the restaurant.' 

'yes. but i am afraid, salisbury, you are incorrigible. you are a slave 
to what you call matter of fact. you know perfectly well that in your 
heart you think the odd in that case is of my making, and that it is 
all really as plain as the police reports. however, as i have begun, i 
will go on. but first we will have something to drink, and you may as 
well light your pipe.' 

dyson went up to the oak cupboard, and drew from its depths a rotund 
bottle and two little glasses, quaint gilded. 

'it's benedictine,' he said. 'you'll have some, won't you?' 

salisbury assented, and the two men sat sip and smoking reflective 
for some minutes before dyson began. 

'let me see,' he said at last, 'we were at the inquest, weren't we? no, 
we had done with that. ah, i remember. i was telling you that on the 
whole i had been successful in my inquiries, investigation, or whatever 
you like to call it, into the matter. wasn't that where i left off?' 

'yes, that was it. to be precise, i think "though" was the last word you 
said on the matter.' 

'exactly. i have been thinking it all over since the other night, and i 
have come to the conclusion that that "though" is a very big "though" 
indeed. not to put too fine a point on it, i have had to confess that 
what i found out, or thought i found out, amount in reality to nothing. 
i am as far away from the heart of the case as ever. however, i may as 
well tell you what i do know. you may remember my saying that i was 
impress a good deal by some remark of one of the doctor who gave 
evidence at the inquest. well, i determined that my first step must be 
to try if i could get something more definite and intelligible out of 
that doctor. somehow or other i manage to get an introduction to the 
man, and he gave me an appointment to come and see him. he turn out to 
be a pleasant, genial fellow; rather young and not in the least like the 
typical medical man, and he began the conference by offering me whisky 
and cigars. i didn't think it worth while to beat about the bush, so i 
began by saying that part of his evidence at the harlesden inquest 
struck me as very peculiar, and i gave him the print report, with the 
sentence in question underlined. he just glance at the slip, and gave 
me a queer look. "it struck you as peculiar, did it?" said he. "well, 
you must remember that the harlesden case was very peculiar. in fact, i 
think i may safe say that in some features it was unique--quite 
unique." "quite so," i replied, "and that's exactly why it interests me, 
and why i want to know more about it. and i thought that if anybody 
could give me any information it would be you. what is your opinion of 
the matter?" 

'it was a pretty downright sort of question, and my doctor look rather 
taken aback. 

'"well," he said, "as i fancy your motive in inquiring into the question 
must be mere curiosity, i think i may tell you my opinion with tolerable 
freedom. so, mr., mr. dyson? if you want to know my theory, it is this: 
i believe that dr. black kill his wife." 

'"but the verdict," i answered, "the verdict was given from your own 
evidence." 

'"quite so; the verdict was given in accordance with the evidence of my 
colleague and myself, and, under the circumstances, i think the jury 
act very sensibly. in fact, i don't see what else they could have 
done. but i stick to my opinion, mind you, and i say this also. i don't 
wonder at black's do what i firm believe he did. i think he was 
justified." 

'"justified! how could that be?" i asked. i was astonished, as you may 
imagine, at the answer i had got. the doctor wheel round his chair and 
look steady at me for a moment before he answered. 

'"i suppose you are not a man of science yourself? no; then it would be 
of no use my go into detail. i have always been firm oppose myself 
to any partnership between physiology and psychology. i believe that 
both are bound to suffer. no one recognize more decidedly than i do the 
impassable gulf, the fathomless abyss that separate the world of 
consciousness from the sphere of matter. we know that every change of 
consciousness is accompany by a rearrange of the molecule in the 
grey matter; and that is all. what the link between them is, or why 
they occur together, we do not know, and most authority believe that 
we never can know. yet, i will tell you that as i did my work, the knife 
in my hand, i felt convinced, in spite of all theories, that what lay 
before me was not the brain of a dead woman--not the brain of a human 
being at all. of course i saw the face; but it was quite placid, devoid 
of all expression. it must have been a beautiful face, no doubt, but i 
can honestly say that i would not have look in that face when there 
was life behind it for a thousand guineas, no, nor for twice that sum." 

'"my dear sir," i said, "you surprise me extremely. you say that it was 
not the brain of a human being. what was it then?" 

'"the brain of a devil." he spoke quite coolly, and never move a 
muscle. "the brain of a devil," he repeated, "and i have no doubt that 
black found some way of putt an end to it. i don't blame him if he 
did. whatever mrs. black was, she was not fit to stay in this world. 
will you have anything more? no? good-night, good-night." 

'it was a queer sort of opinion to get from a man of science, wasn't it? 
when he was saying that he would not have look on that face when alive 
for a thousand guineas, or two thousand guineas, i was thinking of the 
face i had seen, but i said nothing. i went again to harlesden, and 
pass from one shop to another, making small purchases, and try to 
find out whether there was anything about the black which was not 
already common property, but there was very little to hear. one of the 
tradesmen to whom i spoke said he had known the dead woman well; she 
use to buy of him such quantity of grocery as were require for 
their small household, for they never kept a servant, but had a 
charwoman in occasionally, and she had not seen mrs. black for month 
before she died. according to this man mrs. black was "a nice lady," 
always kind and considerate, and so fond of her husband and he of her, 
as every one thought. and yet, to put the doctor's opinion on one side, 
i knew what i had seen. and then after thinking it all over, and putt 
one thing with another, it seem to me that the only person likely to 
give me much assistance would be black himself, and i made up my mind to 
find him. of course he wasn't to be found in harlesden; he had left, i 
was told, directly after the funeral. everything in the house had been 
sold, and one fine day black got into the train with a small 
portmanteau, and went, nobody knew where. it was a chance if he were 
ever heard of again, and it was by a mere chance that i came across him 
at last. i was walking one day along gray's inn road, not bound for 
anywhere in particular, but look about me, as usual, and holding on 
to my hat, for it was a gusty day in early march, and the wind was 
making the treetop in the inn rock and quiver. i had come up from the 
holborn end, and i had almost got to theobald's road when i notice a 
man walking in front of me, leaning on a stick, and to all appearance 
very feeble. there was something about his look that made me curious, i 
don't know why, and i began to walk brisk with the idea of overtake 
him, when of a sudden his hat blew off and came bound along the 
pavement to my feet. of course i rescue the hat, and gave it a glance 
as i went towards its owner. it was a biography in itself; a piccadil 
maker's name in the inside, but i don't think a beggar would have picked 
it out of the gutter. then i look up and saw dr. black of harlesden 
wait for me. a queer thing, wasn't it? but, salisbury, what a change! 
when i saw dr. black come down the steps of his house at harlesden he 
was an upright man, walking firm with well-built limbs; a man, i 
should say, in the prime of his life. and now before me there crouch 
this wretched creature, bent and feeble, with shrunken cheeks, and hair 
that was whitening fast, and limb that tremble and shook together, and 
misery in his eyes. he thank me for bring him his hat, saying, "i 
don't think i should ever have got it, i can't run much now. a gusty 
day, sir, isn't it?" and with this he was turning away, but by little 
and little i contrived to draw him into the current of conversation, and 
we walk together eastward. i think the man would have been glad to get 
rid of me; but i didn't intend to let him go, and he stop at last in 
front of a miserable house in a miserable street. it was, i verily 
believe, one of the most wretched quarters i have ever seen: house that 
must have been sordid and hideous enough when new, that had gather 
foul with every year, and now seem to lean and totter to their 
fall. "i live up there," said black, point to the tiles, "not in the 
front--in the back. i am very quiet there. i won't ask you to come in 
now, but perhaps some other day----" i caught him up at that, and told 
him i should be only too glad to come and see him. he gave me an odd 
sort of glance, as if he were wonder what on earth i or anybody else 
could care about him, and i left him fumble with his latch-key. i 
think you will say i did pretty well when i tell you that within a few 
week i had made myself an intimate friend of black's. i shall never 
forget the first time i went to his room; i hope i shall never see such 
abject, squalid misery again. the foul paper, from which all pattern or 
trace of a pattern had long vanished, subdued and penetrate with the 
grime of the evil street, was hanging in moulder pennon from the 
wall. only at the end of the room was it possible to stand upright, and 
the sight of the wretched bed and the odour of corruption that pervade 
the place made me turn faint and sick. here i found him munch a piece 
of bread; he seem surprise to find that i had kept my promise, but he 
gave me his chair and sat on the bed while we talked. i use to go to 
see him often, and we had long conversation together, but he never 
mention harlesden or his wife. i fancy that he supposed me ignorant of 
the matter, or thought that if i had heard of it, i should never connect 
the respectable dr. black of harlesden with a poor garret in the 
backwoods of london. he was a strange man, and as we sat together 
smoking, i often wonder whether he were mad or sane, for i think the 
wildest dream of paracelsu and the rosicrucian would appear plain and 
sober fact compare with the theory i have heard him earnest advance 
in that grimy den of his. i once venture to hint something of the sort 
to him. i suggest that something he had said was in flat contradiction 
to all science and all experience. "no," he answered, "not all 
experience, for mine count for something. i am no dealer in unproved 
theories; what i say i have prove for myself, and at a terrible cost. 
there is a region of knowledge which you will never know, which wise men 
seeing from afar off shun like the plague, as well they may, but into 
that region i have gone. if you knew, if you could even dream of what 
may be done, of what one or two men have done in this quiet world of 
ours, your very soul would shudder and faint within you. what you have 
heard from me has been but the merest husk and outer covering of true 
science--that science which means death, and that which is more awful 
than death, to those who gain it. no, when men say that there are 
strange things in the world, they little know the awe and the terror 
that dwell always with them and about them." there was a sort of 
fascination about the man that drew me to him, and i was quite sorry to 
have to leave london for a month or two; i miss his odd talk. a few 
days after i came back to town i thought i would look him up, but when i 
gave the two ring at the bell that use to summon him, there was no 
answer. i rang and rang again, and was just turning to go away, when the 
door open and a dirty woman ask me what i wanted. from her look i 
fancy she took me for a plain-clothes officer after one of her lodgers, 
but when i inquire if mr. black were in, she gave me a stare of another 
kind. "there's no mr. black lives here," she said. "he's gone. he's dead 
this six weeks. i always thought he was a bit queer in his head, or else 
had been and got into some trouble or other. he use to go out every 
morning from ten till one, and one monday morning we heard him come in, 
and go into his room and shut the door, and a few minutes after, just as 
we was a-sitting down to our dinner, there was such a scream that i 
thought i should have gone right off. and then we heard a stamping, and 
down he came, rage and curse most dreadful, swear he had been 
rob of something that was worth millions. and then he just drop 
down in the passage, and we thought he was dead. we got him up to his 
room, and put him on his bed, and i just sat there and waited, while my 
'usband he went for the doctor. and there was the wind wide open, and 
a little tin box he had lie on the floor open and empty, but of course 
nobody could possible have got in at the winder, and as for him have 
anything that was worth anything, it's nonsense, for he was often week 
and week behind with his rent, and my 'usband he threaten often and 
often to turn him into the street, for, as he said, we've got a living 
to myke like other people--and, of course, that's true; but, somehow, i 
didn't like to do it, though he was an odd kind of a man, and i fancy 
had been better off. and then the doctor came and look at him, and 
said as he couldn't do nothing, and that night he die as i was 
a-sitting by his bed; and i can tell you that, with one thing and 
another, we lost money by him, for the few bit of clothes as he had 
were worth next to nothing when they came to be sold." i gave the woman 
half a sovereign for her trouble, and went home thinking of dr. black 
and the epitaph she had made him, and wonder at his strange fancy 
that he had been robbed. i take it that he had very little to fear on 
that score, poor fellow; but i suppose that he was really mad, and die 
in a sudden access of his mania. his landlady said that once or twice 
when she had had occasion to go into his room (to dun the poor wretch 
for his rent, most likely), he would keep her at the door for about a 
minute, and that when she came in she would find him putt away his 
tin box in the corner by the window; i suppose he had become possessed 
with the idea of some great treasure, and fancy himself a wealthy man 
in the midst of all his misery. _explicit_, my tale is ended, and you 
see that though i knew black, i know nothing of his wife or of the 
history of her death.--that's the harlesden case, salisbury, and i think 
it interests me all the more deep because there do not seem the 
shadow of a possibility that i or any one else will ever know more about 
it. what do you think of it?' 

'well, dyson, i must say that i think you have contrived to surround the 
whole thing with a mystery of your own making. i go for the doctor's 
solution: black murder his wife, being himself in all probability an 
undeveloped lunatic.' 

'what? do you believe, then, that this woman was something too awful, 
too terrible to be allow to remain on the earth? you will remember 
that the doctor said it was the brain of a devil?' 

'yes, yes, but he was speaking, of course, metaphorically. it's really 
quite a simple matter if you only look at it like that.' 

'ah, well, you may be right; but yet i am sure you are not. well, well, 
it's no good discuss it any more. a little more benedictine? that's 
right; try some of this tobacco. didn't you say that you had been 
bother by something--something which happen that night we dine 
together?' 

'yes, i have been worried, dyson, worried a great deal. i----but it's 
such a trivial matter--indeed, such an absurdity--that i feel ashamed to 
trouble you with it.' 

'never mind, let's have it, absurd or not.' 

with many hesitations, and with much inward resentment of the folly of 
the thing, salisbury told his tale, and repeated reluctantly the absurd 
intelligence and the absurd doggerel of the scrap of paper, expect 
to hear dyson burst out into a roar of laughter. 

'isn't it too bad that i should let myself be bother by such stuff as 
that?' he asked, when he had stutter out the jingle of once, and 
twice, and thrice. 

dyson listen to it all gravely, even to the end, and meditate for a 
few minutes in silence. 

'yes,' he said at length, 'it was a curious chance, your taking shelter 
in that archway just as those two went by. but i don't know that i 
should call what was written on the paper nonsense; it is bizarre 
certainly, but i expect it has a meaning for somebody. just repeat it 
again, will you, and i will write it down. perhaps we might find a 
cipher of some sort, though i hardly think we shall.' 

again had the reluctant lip of salisbury slow to stammer out the 
rubbish that he abhorred, while dyson jot it down on a slip of paper. 

'look over it, will you?' he said, when it was done; 'it may be 
important that i should have every word in its place. is that all 
right?' 

'yes; that is an accurate copy. but i don't think you will get much out 
of it. depend upon it, it is mere nonsense, a wanton scribble. i must be 
go now, dyson. no, no more; that stuff of yours is pretty strong. 
good-night.' 

'i suppose you would like to hear from me, if i did find out anything?' 

'no, not i; i don't want to hear about the thing again. you may regard 
the discovery, if it is one, as your own.' 

'very well. good-night.' 


iv 

a good many hour after salisbury had return to the company of the 
green rep chairs, dyson still sat at his desk, itself a japan 
romance, smoking many pipes, and meditate over his friend's story. the 
bizarre quality of the inscription which had annoy salisbury was to 
him an attraction, and now and again he took it up and scan 
thoughtful what he had written, especially the quaint jingle at the 
end. it was a token, a symbol, he decided, and not a cipher, and the 
woman who had flung it away was in all probability entire ignorant of 
its meaning; she was but the agent of the 'sam' she had abuse and 
discarded, and he too was again the agent of some one unknown, possibly 
of the individual style q, who had been forced to visit his france 
friends. but what to make of 'traverse handle s.' here was the root and 
source of the enigma, and not all the tobacco of virginia seem likely 
to suggest any clue here. it seem almost hopeless, but dyson regard 
himself as the wellington of mysteries, and went to bed feeling assured 
that soon or later he would hit upon the right track for the next few 
days he was deep engaged in his literary labours, labour which were a 
profound mystery even to the most intimate of his friends, who search 
the railway bookstall in vain for the result of so many hour spent at 
the japan bureau in company with strong tobacco and black tea. on 
this occasion dyson confine himself to his room for four days, and it 
was with genuine relief that he laid down his pen and went out into the 
street in quest of relaxation and fresh air. the gas-lamps were being 
lighted, and the fifth edition of the evening papers was being howl 
through the streets, and dyson, feeling that he want quiet, turn 
away from the clamorous strand, and began to trend away to the 
north-west. soon he found himself in street that echo to his 
footsteps, and crossing a broad new thoroughfare, and verge still to 
the west, dyson discover that he had penetrate to the depths of soho. 
here again was life; rare vintage of france and italy, at price which 
seem contemptible small, allure the passer-by; here were cheeses, 
vast and rich, here olive oil, and here a grove of rabelaisian sausages; 
while in a neighbouring shop the whole press of paris appear to be on 
sale. in the middle of the roadway a strange miscellany of nation 
saunter to and fro, for there cab and hansom rarely ventured; and from 
window over window the inhabitant look forth in pleased contemplation 
of the scene. dyson made his way slow along, mingle with the crowd 
on the cobble-stones, listen to the queer babel of france and german, 
and italy and english, glancing now and again at the shop-windows with 
their level battery of bottles, and had almost gain the end of 
the street, when his attention was arrest by a small shop at the 
corner, a vivid contrast to its neighbours. it was the typical shop of 
the poor quarter; a shop entire english. here were vend tobacco and 
sweets, cheap pipes of clay and cherry-wood; penny exercise-books and 
penholder jostle for precedence with comic songs, and story papers 
with appalling cut show that romance claim its place beside the 
actuality of the evening paper, the bill of which flutter at the 
doorway. dyson glance up at the name above the door, and stood by the 
kennel trembling, for a sharp pang, the pang of one who has made a 
discovery, had for a moment left him incapable of motion. the name over 
the shop was travers. dyson look up again, this time at the corner of 
the wall above the lamp-post, and read in white letters on a blue ground 
the words 'handel street, w. c.,' and the legend was repeated in faint 
letters just below. he gave a little sigh of satisfaction, and without 
more ado walk bold into the shop, and stare full in the face the 
fat man who was sitting behind the counter. the fellow rose to his feet, 
and return the stare a little curiously, and then began in stereotype 
phrase-- 

'what can i do for you, sir?' 

dyson enjoy the situation and a dawn perplexity on the man's face. 
he prop his stick careful against the counter and leaning over it, 
said slow and impressively-- 

'once around the grass, and twice around the lass, and thrice around the 
maple-tree.' 

dyson had calculate on his words produce an effect, and he was not 
disappointed. the vendor of miscellany gasped, open-mouthed like a 
fish, and steady himself against the counter. when he spoke, after a 
short interval, it was in a hoarse mutter, tremulous and unsteady. 

'would you mind saying that again, sir? i didn't quite catch it.' 

'my good man, i shall most certainly do nothing of the kind. you heard 
what i said perfectly well. you have got a clock in your shop, i see; an 
admirable timekeeper, i have no doubt. well, i give you a minute by your 
own clock.' 

the man look about him in a perplexed indecision, and dyson felt that 
it was time to be bold. 

'look here, travers, the time is nearly up. you have heard of q, i 
think. remember, i hold your life in my hands. now!' 

dyson was shock at the result of his own audacity. the man shrank and 
shrivel in terror, the sweat pour down a face of ashy white, and he 
held up his hand before him. 

'mr. davies, mr. davies, don't say that--don't for heaven's sake. i 
didn't know you at first, i didn't indeed. good god! mr. davies, you 
wouldn't ruin me? i'll get it in a moment.' 

'you had better not lose any more time.' 

the man slunk piteous out of his own shop, and went into a back 
parlour. dyson heard his tremble finger fumble with a bunch of 
keys, and the creak of an opening box. he came back presently with a 
small package neat tie up in brown paper in his hands, and, still 
full of terror, hand it to dyson. 

'i'm glad to be rid of it,' he said. 'i'll take no more job of this 
sort.' 

dyson took the parcel and his stick, and walk out of the shop with a 
nod, turning round as he pass the door. traver had sunk into his 
seat, his face still white with terror, with one hand over his eyes, 
and dyson speculate a good deal as he walk rapid away as to what 
queer chord those could be on which he had play so roughly. he hail 
the first hansom he could see and drove home, and when he had lit his 
hanging lamp, and laid his parcel on the table, he pause for a moment, 
wonder on what strange thing the lamplight would soon shine. he 
lock his door, and cut the strings, and unfold the paper layer after 
layer, and came at last to a small wooden box, simply but solid made. 
there was no lock, and dyson had simply to raise the lid, and as he did 
so he drew a long breath and start back. the lamp seem to glimmer 
feeble like a single candle, but the whole room blaze with light--and 
not with light alone, but with a thousand colours, with all the glory 
of some paint window; and upon the wall of his room and on the 
familiar furniture, the glow flame back and seem to flow again to its 
source, the little wooden box. for there upon a bed of soft wool lay the 
most splendid jewel, a jewel such as dyson had never dream of, and 
within it shone the blue of far skies, and the green of the sea by the 
shore, and the red of the ruby, and deep violet rays, and in the middle 
of all it seem aflame as if a fountain of fire rose up, and fell, and 
rose again with sparks like stars for drops. dyson gave a long deep 
sigh, and drop into his chair, and put his hand over his eyes to 
think. the jewel was like an opal, but from a long experience of the 
shop-windows he knew there was no such thing as an opal one-quarter or 
one-eighth of its size. he look at the stone again, with a feeling 
that was almost awe, and placed it gently on the table under the lamp, 
and watch the wonderful flame that shone and sparkle in its centre, 
and then turn to the box, curious to know whether it might contain 
other marvels. he lift the bed of wool on which the opal had reclined, 
and saw beneath, no more jewels, but a little old pocket-book, worn and 
shabby with use. dyson open it at the first leaf, and drop the book 
again appalled. he had read the name of the owner, neat written in 
blue ink: 

steven black, m. d., 
oranmore, 
devon road, 
harlesden. 

it was several minutes before dyson could bring himself to open the book 
a second time; he remember the wretched exile in his garret; and his 
strange talk, and the memory too of the face he had seen at the window, 
and of what the specialist had said, surge up in his mind, and as he 
held his finger on the cover, he shivered, dread what might be 
written within. when at last he held it in his hand, and turn the 
pages, he found that the first two leaves were blank, but the third was 
cover with clear, minute writing, and dyson began to read with the 
light of the opal flaming in his eyes. 


v 

'ever since i was a young man'--the record began--'i devoted all my 
leisure and a good deal of time that ought to have been given to other 
study to the investigate of curious and obscure branch of 
knowledge. what are commonly call the pleasure of life had never any 
attraction for me, and i live alone in london, avoid my 
fellow-students, and in my turn avoid by them as a man self-absorbed 
and unsympathetic. so long as i could gratify my desire of knowledge of 
a peculiar kind, knowledge of which the very existence is a profound 
secret to most men, i was intense happy, and i have often spent whole 
nights sitting in the dark of my room, and thinking of the strange 
world on the brink of which i trod. my professional studies, however, 
and the necessity of obtain a degree, for some time forced my more 
obscure employment into the background, and soon after i had qualified i 
met agnes, who became my wife. we took a new house in this remote 
suburb, and i began the regular routine of a sober practice, and for 
some month live happily enough, share in the life about me, and only 
thinking at odd interval of that occult science which had once 
fascinate my whole being. i had learnt enough of the path i had begun 
to tread to know that they were beyond all expression difficult and 
dangerous, that to persevere meant in all probability the wreck of a 
life, and that they led to regions so terrible, that the mind of man 
shrink appall at the very thought. moreover, the quiet and the peace 
i had enjoy since my marriage had wile me away to a great extent from 
place where i knew no peace could dwell. but suddenly--i think indeed 
it was the work of a single night, as i lay awake on my bed gaze into 
the darkness--suddenly, i say, the old desire, the former longing, 
returned, and return with a force that had been intensify ten times 
by its absence; and when the day dawn and i look out of the window, 
and saw with haggard eyes the sunrise in the east, i knew that my doom 
had been pronounced; that as i had gone far, so now i must go farther 
with unfaltering steps. i turn to the bed where my wife was sleep 
peacefully, and lay down again, weeping bitter tears, for the sun had 
set on our happy life and had risen with a dawn of terror to us both. i 
will not set down here in minute detail what followed; outwardly i went 
about the day's labour as before, saying nothing to my wife. but she 
soon saw that i had changed; i spent my spare time in a room which i had 
fitted up as a laboratory, and often i crept upstairs in the grey dawn 
of the morning, when the light of many lamp still glow over london; 
and each night i had stolen a step near to that great abyss which i 
was to bridge over, the gulf between the world of consciousness and the 
world of matter. my experiment were many and complicated in their 
nature, and it was some month before i realize whither they all 
pointed, and when this was borne in upon me in a moment's time, i felt 
my face whiten and my heart still within me. but the power to draw back, 
the power to stand before the door that now open wide before me and 
not to enter in, had long ago been absent; the way was closed, and i 
could only pass onward. my position was as utterly hopeless as that of 
the prisoner in an utter dungeon, whose only light is that of the 
dungeon above him; the door were shut and escape was impossible. 
experiment after experiment gave the same result, and i knew, and shrank 
even as the thought pass through my mind, that in the work i had to do 
there must be elements which no laboratory could furnish, which no 
scale could ever measure. in that work, from which even i doubt to 
escape with life, life itself must enter; from some human being there 
must be drawn that essence which men call the soul, and in its place 
(for in the scheme of the world there is no vacant chamber)--in its 
place would enter in what the lip can hardly utter, what the mind 
cannot conceive without a horror more awful than the horror of death 
itself. and when i knew this, i knew also on whom this fate would fall; 
i look into my wife's eyes. even at that hour, if i had gone out and 
taken a rope and hang myself, i might have escaped, and she also, but 
in no other way. at last i told her all. she shuddered, and wept, and 
call on her dead mother for help, and ask me if i had no mercy, and 
i could only sigh. i conceal nothing from her; i told her what she 
would become, and what would enter in where her life had been; i told 
her of all the shame and of all the horror. you who will read this when 
i am dead--if indeed i allow this record to survive,--you who have 
open the box and have seen what lie there, if you could understand 
what lie hidden in that opal! for one night my wife consent to what i 
ask of her, consent with the tear running down her beautiful face, 
and hot shame flush red over her neck and breast, consent to 
undergo this for me. i threw open the window, and we look together at 
the sky and the dark earth for the last time; it was a fine star-light 
night, and there was a pleasant breeze blowing, and i kiss her on her 
lips, and her tear ran down upon my face. that night she came down to 
my laboratory, and there, with shutter bolt and barred down, with 
curtains drawn thick and close, so that the very stars might be shut out 
from the sight of that room, while the crucible hiss and boil over 
the lamp, i did what had to be done, and led out what was no long a 
woman. but on the table the opal flame and sparkle with such light as 
no eyes of man have ever gaze on, and the ray of the flame that was 
within it flash and glittered, and shone even to my heart. my wife had 
only ask one thing of me; that when there came at last what i had told 
her, i would kill her. i have kept that promise.' 

* * * * * 

there was nothing more. dyson let the little pocket-book fall, and 
turn and look again at the opal with its flaming inmost light, and 
then with unutterable irresistible horror surge up in his heart, 
grasp the jewel, and flung it on the ground, and trample it beneath 
his heel. his face was white with terror as he turn away, and for a 
moment stood sick and trembling, and then with a start he leapt across 
the room and steady himself against the door. there was an angry hiss, 
as of steam escape under great pressure, and as he gazed, motionless, 
a volume of heavy yellow smoke was slow issue from the very centre 
of the jewel, and wreathe itself in snake-like coil above it. and 
then a thin white flame burst forth from the smoke, and shot up into the 
air and vanished; and on the ground there lay a thing like a cinder, 
black and crumble to the touch.
